Why a Fire Appears Larger in Darkness
Imam Razi applies the emission theory to explain a visual phenomenon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the emission theory of vision to explain the optical illusion of a fire appearing larger in darkness.

Core Idea
Perception combines the fire and its glow when the visual ray fails to separate them.

Imam Razi applies the emission theory to a common visual phenomenon: why a fire seems larger at night. By analyzing three distances, he shows the mechanism is optical, not physical. This lesson traces his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the three conditions Imam Razi describes: close, very distant, and intermediate.
The passage uses technical terms like 'الشعاع' (ray) and 'زاوية الإبصار' (angle of vision).
2
Identify the three visual cases
List the three distances and the corresponding visual outcomes: close (normal size), very far (small), intermediate (larger).
For each case, understand how the visual ray behaves.
3
Reflect on the explanation
Ask yourself: why does the intermediate distance cause the fire to appear larger? Connect it to the ray's inability to separate the fire from its surrounding glow.
This is the key point: the eye perceives both fire and glow as a single larger object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولنعد إلى القول بالشعاع ونقول : إذا تقررت هذه القاعدة فاعلم أن النار في الظلمة إذا كانت قريبة من الرائى عند الشعاع فى الظلمة الرقيقة إلى الهواء المضىء ، لمجاورة النار فرأى البصر ما حولها بمعاونة من نورها و ميزها منها فرآها على ما تقتضيها زاوية الإبصار ، وإذا كانت بعيدة جدا لم ينفذ الشعاع فى الظلمة الكثيفة ولم ما حولها من النور المضىء بنورها ورآها وحدها بزاوية وأصغر فيراها أصغر كما في سائر المرئيات وإذا لم تسكن قريبة ولا بعيدة جدا فإن الشعاع البصرى المحاذى الما حولها لم ينفذ نفوذا تاما فلم يميز النار عن الهواء المضىء بها ، بل أدركتهما معا جملة واحدة فيراها البصر بزاوية أوسع من الزاوية التي تحدث من المحاذاة وحدها وذلك هو العلة لكونها في الرؤية أعظم مما لو رؤيت في غير الظلمة المذكورة المحاذاة وحدها
And let us return to the statement of the ray and say: when this principle is established, know that fire in darkness, when it is close to the observer, the ray passes through the thin darkness to the illuminated air due to the proximity of the fire, so the eye sees what is around it with the help of its light and distinguishes it from it, and sees it as required by the angle of vision. And when it is very distant, the ray does not penetrate the dense darkness and does not [illumine] what is around it with its light, and it sees it alone at a smaller angle, so it sees it smaller, as in other visible objects. And when it is neither close nor very distant, the visual ray opposite what is around it does not penetrate completely, so it does not distinguish the fire from the air illuminated by it, but perceives both together as one whole, so the eye sees it at a wider angle than the angle that occurs from the opposite alone. And that is the reason for its appearing larger in vision than if it were seen without that darkness, only by the opposite.
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage is an application of the emission theory to explain why a fire appears larger in darkness than in daylight. Imam Razi presents three cases: (1) When the fire is close, the visual ray passes through thin darkness, the eye distinguishes the fire from its illuminated surroundings, and sees it at its normal visual angle. (2) When the fire is very far, the ray cannot penetrate the dense darkness, the eye sees only the fire itself at a small angle, so it appears small. (3) When the fire is at an intermediate distance, the ray does not completely penetrate, so the eye fails to distinguish the fire from the illuminated air around it; it perceives both as a single larger object, resulting in a wider visual angle and hence a larger appearance. This explanation ties the phenomenon directly to the mechanical properties of the visual ray.

الظلمة
الشعاع
زاوية الإبصار
المحاذاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fire actually expands or changes size in darkness.
Correction: The passage shows it is an optical effect: the eye perceives the fire and its surrounding glow as a single larger object when the visual ray fails to distinguish them.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Razi's explanation, why does a fire appear larger when seen at an intermediate distance in darkness?
A) The fire expands in darkness
B) The visual ray combines the fire and its surrounding glow into one perceived object
C) The darkness magnifies the image
D) The eye adjusts its focus

Key Takeaway
A fire appears larger in darkness because the visual ray, unable to penetrate the partial darkness, fuses the fire and its glow into a single perceived object with a wider visual angle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that when the visual ray fails to penetrate the darkness, it fuses the fire and its glow into one larger object. What 'glow' in your own beliefs have you mistaken for the truth itself, because you were unwilling to let your reason penetrate the surrounding darkness? What would change if you forced that distinction?
?
Imam Razi teaches that our perception can be deceived by the failure to separate light from its source. When in your life have you been persuaded by the appearance of something greater or smaller than it actually is, simply because you did not examine it with sufficient directness? What price did you pay for that illusion?
?
If the fire appears larger only when the visual ray does not fully penetrate, what does that say about the 'fire' of truth in your soul? Are you content to see it enlarged by the glow of your desires, or will you press through the darkness to see it as it truly is—even if that truth is smaller than you hoped?

Seeing One as Two: The Illusion of Double Vision
Imam Razi enumerates examples of double vision and explains their causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the physical and neurological causes of double vision as explained by Imam Razi through examples like pressing the eye, squinting, and reflection from water.

Core Idea
Double vision occurs when light from one object enters the eyes from different directions.

Imam Razi begins his discussion of visual illusions with a common phenomenon: seeing one object as two. He gives several examples—pressing one eye, squinting, and seeing two moons in water—and then explains in footnotes the underlying causes involving the optic nerves and reflection. This passage establishes that such illusions are not errors of the eye but of the alignment of visual rays.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples
Pay attention to the three examples of double vision: pressing the eye, squinting, and reflection from water. Notice how they all result in seeing a single object as two.
2
Understand the cause
Read footnote (1) which explains the anatomical cause: the two optic nerves are normally aligned, but if they deviate, light from one object falls on different parts of the retina, causing the brain to perceive two images. Also note footnote (2) explains the reflection example.
3
Reflect on the role of the mind
Imam Razi notes that the mind judges the double vision as an error. This shows that correct perception depends on both sensory input and mental interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواحدة ثنتين كما إذا غمزنا إحدى العينين ونظرنا إلى القمر فإنا نرى قمرين ، وكما في حق الأحول (١) وكما إذا نظرنا إلى الماء عند طلوع القمر ، فإنا نرى في الماء قمرا وعلى السماء قمرا آخر (٢) وقد نرى الأشياء الكثيرة واحدة كالرحى إذا أخرجنا من مركزها إلى محيطها خطوطا كثيرة متقاربة بألوان مختلفة ، فإذا استدارت سريعا رأينا هالونا واحدا كأنه ممتزج من كل تلك الألوان (٣) وقد نرى المعدوم موجودا كالسراب أو كالأشياء التى يربيها صاحب خفة اليد و الشعبذة ، وكما نرى القطرة العازلة كالخط المستقيم والشعلة التي تدار بسرعة كالدائرة ونرى المتحرك ساكنا كالظل والساكن متحركا كراكب السفينة ، فإنه يشاهد الشط الساكن (٤) 
(١) أقول : النور ممتد من الدماغ في عصبتين مجوفتين تتلافيان قبل وصولهما إلى العينين ثم تتباعدان ويتصل كل واحد منهما بواحدة من العينين ، فإذا كاننا مستقيمتين تبصران الشيء معا شيئا واحدا وإذا انحرفتا أو انحرفت إحداهما عن الاستقامة صارت محاذاة إحديهما منحرفة عن محاذاة الأخرى وصار المبصر من أحديهما غير المبصر من الأخرى، وإذا أبصرنا شيئا واحدا حسبه المبصر شيئين لوقوع نور بصره عليه من محاذاتين مختلفتين وحكم العقل بالغلط ، وهكذا الحكم إذ تخالف الوسطى والسبابة من الأصابع في وصفهما فأحسنا معا شيئا واحدا كحمصة مثلا توهم أنهما أحستنا بحمصتين، والأحول النظري قلما لا يرى الشيء شينين لاعتياده بالوقوف على الصواب بل إنما يقع ذلك الأحول الذي يقصد الحول تكلفا
(٢) أقول هذا يكون بنفوذ الشعاع البصرى إلى قمر السماء وبانعكاسه من سطح الماء إليه ، فإنه يراه مرتين مرة بالشعاع النافذ ومرة بالشعاع المنعكس
(٣) أقول: كل ما أدركه حس يتأدى إلى الحس المشترك ثم إلى الخيال ، فإذا أدرك البصر لونا وانتقل بسرعة إلى لون اخر كان أثر اللون الأول فى الحس المشترك عند إدراك اللون الثاني ، وكأن الرائى رآهما معا، ولا يكون بينهما زمان ممكن للنفس أن تميز أحدهما فيه من الثاني فتدركهما ممتزجين وإن كان الإدراك بالتبين ، وأيضا إن زالت الألوان عن محاذات البصر وارتسمت في الحس المشترك على توال لا يدرك الحس تراخى بعضها عن بعض أدرك النفس من الحس المشترك لونا ممتزجا من جميعها .
(٤) أقول: السراب المرأى ليس معدوما مطلقا إنما هو شيء يترائى للبصر بسبب تزحزح شعاع ينعكس من أرض سبخة كما ينعكس عن المياه فيحسب ماء وليس للبصر فيه غلط والأشياء التى يربها خفيف اليد والمتعبد إنما يكون فى التوهم بخلاف ما يكون في الوجود بسبب عدم تمييز النفس بين الشيء وبين ما يشبهه ، أو بسبب سرعة الحركة من الشيء إلى شبيهه ، وإما بسبب إقامة البدل مقام الشيء المبدل عنه بسرعة على ما يقف عليه من تعرف تلك الأعمال، ورؤية القطرة النازلة كالخط المستقيم والشعلة الجوالة كدائرة إنما يكون لاتصال ما يدركه البصر في
The one as two: as when we press one of the two eyes and look at the moon, then we see two moons; and as in the case of the squinting person (1); and as when we look at water at moonrise, then we see in the water a moon and in the sky another moon (2). And we sometimes see many things as one: like the millstone if we draw from its center to its circumference many close lines of different colors, then when it rotates quickly we see a single halo as if mixed from all those colors (3). And we sometimes see the nonexistent as existent: like the mirage or the things that the sleight-of-hand artist and juggler produce; and as we see the falling drop as a straight line and the flame that is moved quickly as a circle; and we see the moving as stationary like the shadow, and the stationary as moving like the passenger on a ship, for he sees the stationary shore as moving (4). 
(1) I say: Light extends from the brain in two hollow nerves that meet before reaching the two eyes, then diverge, and each connects to one of the eyes. When they are straight, they see a thing together as one thing; when they deviate or one of them deviates from straightness, the direction of one becomes deviated from the direction of the other, and the object seen by one becomes different from that seen by the other. And when we see one thing, the seer considers it as two things because his visual light falls upon it from two different directions, and the mind judges it as an error. The same judgment occurs when the middle finger and index finger are crossed in their positioning, so they both feel one thing, like a chickpea, and imagine that they have felt two chickpeas. And the natural squinter rarely sees a thing as two due to his habit of discerning correctly; rather, this happens to the squinter who forces squinting deliberately. 
(2) I say: This occurs by the penetration of the visual ray to the moon in the sky and its reflection from the water surface to it; for he sees it twice: once by the penetrating ray and once by the reflected ray. 
(3) I say: Everything that a sense perceives is transmitted to the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) then to the imagination (al-khayāl). So if the eye perceives a color and quickly moves to another color, the trace of the first color remains in the common sense at the perception of the second color, and it is as if the seer saw them together; there is no time between them for the soul to distinguish one from the other, so it perceives them as mixed, even though the perception is sequential. Also, if the colors depart from opposite the eye and are imprinted in the common sense in succession without the sense perceiving the delay of some from others, the soul perceives from the common sense a color mixed from all of them. 
(4) I say: The seen mirage is not absolutely nonexistent; rather, it is something that appears to the sight due to the displacement of a ray reflected from a salt marsh, just as it reflects from water, so it is thought to be water; there is no error in the sight concerning it. And the things produced by the sleight-of-hand artist and conjurer occur only in imagination, contrary to what exists in reality, due to the soul's failure to distinguish between a thing and what resembles it, or due to the speed of movement from a thing to its likeness, or due to the swift substitution of something in place of what it replaces, as known by those familiar with such actions. And the vision of the falling drop as a straight line and the moving flame as a circle occurs only because of the continuity of what the eye perceives in [time?]
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a classified list of visual illusions with explanations. The author enumerates several categories: (1) Seeing one thing as two: caused by pressing one eye (double vision), natural or forced squint (al-ḥawal), or reflection from water (two moons). (2) Seeing many things as one: the example of a spinning wheel with colored lines merging into a single halo. (3) Seeing nonexistent things as existent: mirage, sleight of hand, a falling drop appearing as a line, a moving flame as a circle. (4) Seeing moving things as stationary (shadow) and stationary things as moving (shore from a ship). The footnotes provide detailed causes: for double vision, the two optic nerves are misaligned; for the merging colors, it is due to the persistence of images in the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and imagination (al-khayāl); for mirage, it is a real reflection misinterpreted; for sleight of hand, it is due to speed or substitution. The author concludes that these illusions are generally not errors of the eye but of the mind or sensory processing.

Key Terms
الواحدة ثنتين
الأحول
الشعاع البصري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Double vision is always a defect in the eyes.
Correction: Imam Razi explains that it can result from temporary misalignment or reflection, and the mind often corrects it or judges it as an error.

Checkpoint
According to footnote (1), what happens when the two optic nerves are not straight?
A) They merge into one
B) They cause double vision
C) They become blocked
D) They invert images

Key Takeaway
Double vision arises from misalignment of visual rays, whether due to physical pressure, natural squint, or reflection, and the mind recognizes it as an error.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain you saw something correctly, only to later realize you were mistaken. How does this lesson's explanation of double vision challenge your instinct to trust your senses without question? What will you do the next time you feel certain of a sensory experience that contradicts your knowledge?
?
If our eyes can be tricked by something as simple as pressing them, how much more cautious should we be when relying on our senses to judge matters of faith? Which of your beliefs are based on sensory perception, and how can you subject them to the same scrutiny as the double vision examples?
?
The mind in these examples recognizes the error and corrects it. Are you actively training your mind to recognize when your own perceptions—whether physical or intellectual—might be misleading you? What is one habit you can adopt today to develop that critical awareness?

Seeing Many as One: The Illusion of Blended Colors
Imam Razi explains the illusion of seeing many colors as one due to sensory persistence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how multiple colors can appear as a single blended color due to the persistence of sensory impressions in the common sense and imagination.

Core Idea
Rapid succession of colors creates a single mixed impression in the mind.

Imam Razi next turns to illusions where many distinct things appear as one. He gives the prominent example of a spinning millstone with colored lines that merge into a single halo. In the footnote, he explains the physiological basis: the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and imagination (al-khayāl) retain traces of previous perceptions, so when colors change quickly, they are perceived as simultaneous and blended.

Guided Path
1
Visualize the example
Imagine a millstone with many colored lines from center to edge. When it spins quickly, the lines blend into a single ring of mixed color. This is the core example.
2
Understand the sensory process
Read footnote (3) carefully. Imam Razi describes how visual impressions travel to the common sense and then to the imagination. If colors change rapidly, the trace of the first remains while the second is perceived, causing blending.
He also mentions that if colors are imprinted in succession without delay, the soul perceives a mixture.
3
Connect to the learning objective
This illusion shows that the eye itself does not blend the colors; it is the processing in the common sense and imagination that creates the illusion of unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الواحدة ثنتين كما إذا غمزنا إحدى العينين ونظرنا إلى القمر فإنا نرى قمرين ، وكما في حق الأحول (١) وكما إذا نظرنا إلى الماء عند طلوع القمر ، فإنا نرى في الماء قمرا وعلى السماء قمرا آخر (٢) وقد نرى الأشياء الكثيرة واحدة كالرحى إذا أخرجنا من مركزها إلى محيطها خطوطا كثيرة متقاربة بألوان مختلفة ، فإذا استدارت سريعا رأينا هالونا واحدا كأنه ممتزج من كل تلك الألوان (٣) وقد نرى المعدوم موجودا كالسراب أو كالأشياء التى يربيها صاحب خفة اليد و الشعبذة ، وكما نرى القطرة العازلة كالخط المستقيم والشعلة التي تدار بسرعة كالدائرة ونرى المتحرك ساكنا كالظل والساكن متحركا كراكب السفينة ، فإنه يشاهد الشط الساكن (٤) 
(١) أقول : النور ممتد من الدماغ في عصبتين مجوفتين تتلافيان قبل وصولهما إلى العينين ثم تتباعدان ويتصل كل واحد منهما بواحدة من العينين ، فإذا كاننا مستقيمتين تبصران الشيء معا شيئا واحدا وإذا انحرفتا أو انحرفت إحداهما عن الاستقامة صارت محاذاة إحديهما منحرفة عن محاذاة الأخرى وصار المبصر من أحديهما غير المبصر من الأخرى، وإذا أبصرنا شيئا واحدا حسبه المبصر شيئين لوقوع نور بصره عليه من محاذاتين مختلفتين وحكم العقل بالغلط ، وهكذا الحكم إذ تخالف الوسطى والسبابة من الأصابع في وصفهما فأحسنا معا شيئا واحدا كحمصة مثلا توهم أنهما أحستنا بحمصتين، والأحول النظري قلما لا يرى الشيء شينين لاعتياده بالوقوف على الصواب بل إنما يقع ذلك الأحول الذي يقصد الحول تكلفا
(٢) أقول هذا يكون بنفوذ الشعاع البصرى إلى قمر السماء وبانعكاسه من سطح الماء إليه ، فإنه يراه مرتين مرة بالشعاع النافذ ومرة بالشعاع المنعكس
(٣) أقول: كل ما أدركه حس يتأدى إلى الحس المشترك ثم إلى الخيال ، فإذا أدرك البصر لونا وانتقل بسرعة إلى لون اخر كان أثر اللون الأول فى الحس المشترك عند إدراك اللون الثاني ، وكأن الرائى رآهما معا، ولا يكون بينهما زمان ممكن للنفس أن تميز أحدهما فيه من الثاني فتدركهما ممتزجين وإن كان الإدراك بالتبين ، وأيضا إن زالت الألوان عن محاذات البصر وارتسمت في الحس المشترك على توال لا يدرك الحس تراخى بعضها عن بعض أدرك النفس من الحس المشترك لونا ممتزجا من جميعها .
(٤) أقول: السراب المرأى ليس معدوما مطلقا إنما هو شيء يترائى للبصر بسبب تزحزح شعاع ينعكس من أرض سبخة كما ينعكس عن المياه فيحسب ماء وليس للبصر فيه غلط والأشياء التى يربها خفيف اليد والمتعبد إنما يكون فى التوهم بخلاف ما يكون في الوجود بسبب عدم تمييز النفس بين الشيء وبين ما يشبهه ، أو بسبب سرعة الحركة من الشيء إلى شبيهه ، وإما بسبب إقامة البدل مقام الشيء المبدل عنه بسرعة على ما يقف عليه من تعرف تلك الأعمال، ورؤية القطرة النازلة كالخط المستقيم والشعلة الجوالة كدائرة إنما يكون لاتصال ما يدركه البصر في
The one as two: as when we press one of the two eyes and look at the moon, then we see two moons; and as in the case of the squinting person (1); and as when we look at water at moonrise, then we see in the water a moon and in the sky another moon (2). And we sometimes see many things as one: like the millstone if we draw from its center to its circumference many close lines of different colors, then when it rotates quickly we see a single halo as if mixed from all those colors (3). And we sometimes see the nonexistent as existent: like the mirage or the things that the sleight-of-hand artist and juggler produce; and as we see the falling drop as a straight line and the flame that is moved quickly as a circle; and we see the moving as stationary like the shadow, and the stationary as moving like the passenger on a ship, for he sees the stationary shore as moving (4). 
(1) I say: Light extends from the brain in two hollow nerves that meet before reaching the two eyes, then diverge, and each connects to one of the eyes. When they are straight, they see a thing together as one thing; when they deviate or one of them deviates from straightness, the direction of one becomes deviated from the direction of the other, and the object seen by one becomes different from that seen by the other. And when we see one thing, the seer considers it as two things because his visual light falls upon it from two different directions, and the mind judges it as an error. The same judgment occurs when the middle finger and index finger are crossed in their positioning, so they both feel one thing, like a chickpea, and imagine that they have felt two chickpeas. And the natural squinter rarely sees a thing as two due to his habit of discerning correctly; rather, this happens to the squinter who forces squinting deliberately. 
(2) I say: This occurs by the penetration of the visual ray to the moon in the sky and its reflection from the water surface to it; for he sees it twice: once by the penetrating ray and once by the reflected ray. 
(3) I say: Everything that a sense perceives is transmitted to the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) then to the imagination (al-khayāl). So if the eye perceives a color and quickly moves to another color, the trace of the first color remains in the common sense at the perception of the second color, and it is as if the seer saw them together; there is no time between them for the soul to distinguish one from the other, so it perceives them as mixed, even though the perception is sequential. Also, if the colors depart from opposite the eye and are imprinted in the common sense in succession without the sense perceiving the delay of some from others, the soul perceives from the common sense a color mixed from all of them. 
(4) I say: The seen mirage is not absolutely nonexistent; rather, it is something that appears to the sight due to the displacement of a ray reflected from a salt marsh, just as it reflects from water, so it is thought to be water; there is no error in the sight concerning it. And the things produced by the sleight-of-hand artist and conjurer occur only in imagination, contrary to what exists in reality, due to the soul's failure to distinguish between a thing and what resembles it, or due to the speed of movement from a thing to its likeness, or due to the swift substitution of something in place of what it replaces, as known by those familiar with such actions. And the vision of the falling drop as a straight line and the moving flame as a circle occurs only because of the continuity of what the eye perceives in [time?]
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage is a classified list of visual illusions with explanations. The author enumerates several categories: (1) Seeing one thing as two: caused by pressing one eye (double vision), natural or forced squint (al-ḥawal), or reflection from water (two moons). (2) Seeing many things as one: the example of a spinning wheel with colored lines merging into a single halo. (3) Seeing nonexistent things as existent: mirage, sleight of hand, a falling drop appearing as a line, a moving flame as a circle. (4) Seeing moving things as stationary (shadow) and stationary things as moving (shore from a ship). The footnotes provide detailed causes: for double vision, the two optic nerves are misaligned; for the merging colors, it is due to the persistence of images in the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) and imagination (al-khayāl); for mirage, it is a real reflection misinterpreted; for sleight of hand, it is due to speed or substitution. The author concludes that these illusions are generally not errors of the eye but of the mind or sensory processing.

Key Terms
الرحى
الحس المشترك
الخيال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The eye actually blends colors when they are close together.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that the blending occurs in the mind due to persistence of sensation, not in the eye itself.

Checkpoint
According to footnote (3), what role does the common sense (al-ḥiss al-mushtarak) play in the spinning wheel illusion?
A) It creates the colors
B) It retains the trace of previous colors
C) It moves the colors
D) It ignores the colors

Key Takeaway
The perception of blended colors from a spinning wheel is not an error of the eye but a result of the mind's inability to separate rapid sequential impressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you been convinced of a false unity because you were not patient enough to examine the parts? How can you train yourself to slow down and see the multiplicity in ideas?
?
Have you ever made a judgment about a person or situation based on a quick impression, only to realize later that you missed the details? What does this lesson teach you about the limits of your own perception?
?
If even your senses can deceive you about something as simple as colors, how much more careful must you be when evaluating matters of faith and knowledge? What step will you take today to seek deeper understanding before forming conclusions?

Seeing Nonexistent as Existent: Mirage, Sleight of Hand, and Motion Illusions
Imam Razi discusses illusions where nonexistent things are seen or motion is misperceived.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the various ways in which the mind can perceive nonexistent objects or misperceive motion, as explained by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Illusions of nonexistence and motion arise from mental interpretation, not from the eye itself.

Imam Razi concludes his treatment of visual illusions with a diverse set of examples that seem to show nonexistent things or misjudge motion: mirages, conjuring tricks, falling drops that look like lines, moving flames that look like circles, and the apparent motion of stationary objects. In the footnote, he explains that the mirage is a real reflection misinterpreted, sleight of hand is due to speed and substitution, and the drop/flame illusions are due to continuity of perception. This passage emphasizes that the senses do not lie—it is the mind that errs.

Guided Path
1
List the examples
Identify each illusion: mirage, juggler's tricks, falling drop as a line, moving flame as a circle, stationary shadow perceived as moving, and moving shore perceived as stationary.
2
Understand the explanations
Read footnote (4) carefully. For the mirage, it is a genuine reflection from a salt marsh misinterpreted as water. For sleight of hand, it is due to speed or substitution. For the drop and flame, it is because the eye perceives continuous motion.
The footnote is incomplete, but we have the beginning.
3
General principle
Note that Imam Razi says the mirage is not absolutely nonexistent—it is a real optical phenomenon. This shows that not all illusions are errors; some are correct perceptions misinterpreted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage is a classified list of visual illusions with explanations. Misconception: The mirage is an example of the eye being deceived.

Checkpoint
According to footnote (4), why does the sleight-of-hand artist's trick work?
A) The eyes are tricked by magic
B) The soul fails to distinguish or because of speed
C) The objects actually change
D) The light is bent

Key Takeaway
Illusions like mirage and sleight of hand are not errors of the eye but of the mind's interpretation of sensory data.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something that contradicts reality, do you immediately blame your senses or pause to examine your own interpretation? How can you train yourself to distrust your first impression in matters of faith as well?
?
Your mind constantly fills gaps in perception—like seeing motion where there is only a series of still images. Where in your life might you be filling spiritual gaps with false certainties? What would it take to let the discomfort of uncertainty drive you to seek truth rather than settle for illusion?
?
You trust your eyes for most of your daily decisions, yet this lesson shows they can be tricked. If your most relied-upon sense can deceive you, how much more careful must you be with your intellect when it comes to matters of the unseen? Will you commit to questioning your assumptions before acting on them?

The Optics of Illusion: Why Trees Appear Bent in Water
Razi uses geometry to explain why straight trees appear bent in water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric reasoning Razi uses to demonstrate that the apparent bending of trees in water is an optical illusion caused by reflection.

Core Idea
The soul's habit of assuming straight light paths creates the illusion of bent trees in water.

Imam Razi presents a fascinating case from optics to show how sensory perception can be mistaken. He uses geometry to prove that the straight tree appears bent due to the reflection of light rays at the water surface. This lesson unpacks his geometric proof and explains why the soul is deceived.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to get a sense of Razi's argument. Do not worry about every detail at this stage.
The passage is dense with geometric notation; focus on the overall flow.
2
Understand the initial claim and setup
Notice that Razi begins with the observed phenomenon (straight things appearing bent) and then sets up a geometric diagram with points A (observer), B D (water surface), and the tree.
The diagram is conceptual; the exact points are not crucial for the lesson.
3
Follow the geometric proof of impossibility
Razi shows that any alternative ray path would produce a logical contradiction (angle inequality). Follow his reasoning step by step to see why the reflected path is necessary.
The proof uses the law of equal angles of incidence and reflection.
4
Grasp the psychological explanation
After the geometric proof, Razi explains that the soul does not perceive the reflection and is accustomed to straight-line vision, so it imagines the tree is inverted.
This connects the geometry to the sensory illusion.
5
Reflect on the epistemological lesson
Consider why Razi includes this discussion: it demonstrates that the senses can be deceived, reinforcing the need for rational scrutiny.
This ties back to the broader course on methodology and rational judgments.

وقد نرى المستقيم منكسا كالأشجار التي على أطراف الأنهار (۳). أقول: إذا انعكس شعاع البصر من سطح الماء إلى الأشجار على وجه يكون زاويتا الشعاع والانعكاس متساويتين، ينعكس الشعاع إلى رأس الشجر من موضع أقرب إلى الرائي وإلى أسفله لا من موضع أبعد منه، إلى أن يتصل قاعدة الشجر بقاعدة عكسه. فليكن الرائي ا وسطح الماء ب د والشجر القائم على ذلك السماح [؟] ى د ولينعكس الشعاع النافذ من ا إلى نقطة منها إلى رأس الشجر وهو نقطة في بحيث يكون زاويتا ا ه ب و ه د متساويتين. أقول: لا يمكن أن ينعكس الشعاع على جهة بحيث ينعكس منه شعاع إلى جزء أسفل من رأس الشجر كنقطة [؟] وإلا فينعكس من نقطة ر ويكون الشعاع النافذ من ا إلى ر منعكسا عنه إلى ح وحينئذ يجب أن تكون زاوية ا ر ب الخارجة عن مثلث ا ر ه أعظم من زاوية ا ه ب لكن زاوية ا ر ب مساوية لزاوية ح ر د وزاوية ا ر ب مساوية لزاوية [؟] د فزاوية ح ر د أعظم من زاوية ه د فيكون أعظم كثيرا من زاوية ح ه د فالداخلة في مثلث ه ر ه أعظم من خارجتها هذا خلف محال. ولا يمكن أن ينعكس من [؟] شعاع إلى جزء أسفل من رأس الشجر كنقطة وإلا كانت زاوية ا [؟] ب مساوية لكل واحد من زاويتي ح ه د ا ح د العظمى والصغرى هذا خلف. فإذاً لابد من أن ينعكس إلى كل نقطة تميل من الرأس إلى أسفل من نقطة تكون من [؟] إلى د أميل حتى تتصل القاعدة بالقاعدة. ولما كانت النفس لا تدرك الانعكاس فهي متعودية لرؤية المرئيات بنفوذ الشعاع على الاستقامة بحسب الشعاع المنعكس نافذا في الماء، ولا يكون في نفس الأمر نافذا فإن الماء ربما لا يكون عميقا بقدر طول الشجر، أو يكون كدرا لا ينفذ فيه الشعاع أصلا، وحينئذ يحسب أن رأس الشجر أكثر نزولا في الماء لكونه أبعد من أصله وباقي أجزائه على الترتيب، فرآه كأنه منكوس تحت سطح الماء. وهذه المسائل وإن كانت متعلقة بالهندسة أوردناها هاهنا لأن الكلام انجر إليها.
And we may see the straight as bent, like trees on the banks of rivers (3). I say: When the ray of sight reflects from the water surface to the trees in such a way that the angles of incidence and reflection are equal, the ray reflects to the top of the tree from a position closer to the observer and to its bottom not from a farther position, until the base of the tree connects with the base of its reflection. So let the observer be A, the water surface B D, and the tree standing on that shore [?] Y D, and let the ray passing from A to a point reflect from it to the top of the tree which is point F, such that the two angles A H B and H D are equal. I say: It is impossible that the ray reflect in such a way that a ray reflects from it to a part lower than the top of the tree, like point [?]; otherwise it would reflect from point R, and the ray passing from A to R would reflect from it to H, and then angle A R B external to triangle A R H would be greater than angle A H B, but angle A R B is equal to angle H R D, and angle A R B is equal to angle [?] D, so angle H R D is greater than angle H D, thus it is much greater than angle H H D, so the interior angle in triangle H R H is greater than its exterior, which is an absurdity. And it is impossible that a ray reflect from [?] to a part lower than the top of the tree, like point [?]; otherwise angle A [?] B would be equal to each of the two angles H H D and A H D, the greater and the smaller, which is absurd. Therefore, the reflection must occur to every point from the top downward from a point that is [?] to D more inclined, until the base connects with the base. And since the soul does not perceive the reflection, it is accustomed to seeing visible objects by the direct penetration of the ray according to the reflected ray penetrating in the water, but in reality it is not penetrating, because the water may not be as deep as the length of the tree, or may be murky such that the ray does not penetrate at all. Then it is imagined that the top of the tree is more submerged in the water because it is farther from its base and the rest of its parts in order, so he sees it as if inverted under the water surface. These issues, although they belong to geometry, we have mentioned them here because the discussion led to them.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage presents a geometric proof that the apparent bending of straight trees in water is a sensory illusion caused by reflection. The author begins with the observed phenomenon: we see straight things as bent (e.g., trees on riverbanks). He then explains the optics: the visual ray reflects off the water surface, and due to the law of equal angles, the image of the tree appears to come from a different path. He provides a detailed geometric proof using points and angles to show that the reflected ray must connect the observer's eye to each part of the tree in a specific way, and any assumption of a direct view of lower parts leads to a contradiction. The key conclusion is that the soul does not perceive the reflection itself, but habitually interprets the light as traveling straight, so it imagines the tree to be inverted and submerged. This demonstration reinforces the overall argument that the senses can be deceived, and such examples are introduced because the discussion of sensory error leads to them.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: People think the tree itself is bent in the water, or that water distorts the tree physically.
Correction: The tree is straight; the apparent bending is an optical illusion caused by reflection of light at the water surface, as proven geometrically.

Checkpoint

B) That the light travels straight into the water.
C) That the light bends at the tree surface.

Key Takeaway
The bending of trees in water is not a property of the tree but a trick of reflection, revealing that the senses can be deceived.

The soul did not perceive the reflection itself. Are there areas of your life where you are seeing only the surface and missing the deeper reality? What one step will you take to correct that blind spot before you sin or misjudge?

The Fifth Argument: Snow and Glass
Razi presents the fifth proof that sense judgment can be false using snow and glass examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's fifth argument that sense perception can be deceptive, demonstrated through the examples of snow, powdered glass, and a crack in glass.

Core Idea
Sense perception can attribute qualities to things that they do not inherently possess.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a compelling empirical argument against the reliability of sense perception. He uses everyday observations — snow, powdered glass, and a crack in thick glass — to demonstrate that our senses can be systematically mistaken about the true nature of objects. This argument challenges the notion that sense perception is a trustworthy foundation for knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three examples
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the three distinct examples Razi provides: snow, powdered glass, and a crack in glass. Notice how each example is structured similarly.
Razi uses observation and analysis to show that our senses perceive color where none exists in the object itself.
2
Understand how each example demonstrates false sense judgment
For each example, ask: What does sense perceive? What is the actual state of the object? How does Razi prove that the perceived quality is not inherent? The key is that the components are transparent and colorless, yet we see white.
Razi anticipates and refutes an objection about light reflection in the snow example, showing his thoroughness.
3
Grasp the conclusion: sense cannot be the primary judge
After the three examples, Razi concludes that sense judgments can be false or true. Because they can be false, we cannot rely on them without a higher arbiter. This establishes that sense is not the primary judge of truth.
This conclusion is a key step in the broader argument that reason must judge sense perceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وخامسا : أنا ترى الثلج في غاية البياض ، ثم إذا ما لغتا فى النظر إليه رأيناه مركبا من أجزاء جمدية صفار وكل واحد من تلك الأجزاء شفاف خال عن اللون ، فالثلج فى نفسه غير ملون ، مع أنا نراه ملونا بلون البياض. وليس لأحد أن يقول إن ذلك إنما كان لانعكاس الشعاع عن سطوح بعض تلك الأجزاء الجدية إلى بعض . لانا نقول : هذا لا يقدم فى غرضنا ، لأن الذي ذكرته ليس إلا بان الملة التي لاجلها فى الثلج أبيض ، مع أنه فى نفسه ليس بأبيض ، ونحن ما سعينا إلا لهذا القدر ، وأيضاً فالرجاج المدقوق نراه أبيض مع أن كل من أجزائه شفاف خال عن اللون ولم يحدث فيما بينها كيفية مزاجية، لأن تلك الأجزاء صلبة يابسة لم يحصل فيما بينها فعل وانفعال، وأيضا نرى موضع الشق من الزجاج الثخين الشفاف أبيض مع أنه ليس هناك إلا الهواء المحتبس في ذلك الشق ، والهواء غير ملون والزجاج غير ملون فعلمنا أنا نرى الهواء مع أنه في نفسه غير ملون ملونا (٢) فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن حكم الحس قد يكون باطلا وقد يكون حقا وإذا كان كذلك لم يجز الاعتماد على حكمه إذ لا شهادة المتهم بل لابد من حاكم آخر فوقه ليميز خطاه عن صوابه ، على هذا التقدير لا يكون الحس هو الحاكم الأول وهو المطلوب (١).
And fifth: We see snow extremely white, then when we look closely at it we see it composed of small icy particles, and each of those particles is transparent and devoid of color. So snow in itself is not colored, while we see it colored with whiteness. And no one can say that this is only due to the reflection of light from the surfaces of some of those icy particles onto others. For we say: This does not harm our purpose, because what you mentioned is only an explanation of the cause on account of which snow is white, while in itself it is not white, and we only seek this much. Also, powdered glass we see as white, while each of its parts is transparent and devoid of color, and no mixture quality occurs among them, because those parts are solid and dry and there is no action and reaction among them. And also we see the place of a crack in thick transparent glass as white, while there is only trapped air in that crack, and air is not colored and glass is not colored. So we know that we see air—while it is in itself not colored—as colored. (2) So it is established by these ways that the judgment of sense may be false and may be true. And if so, it is not permissible to rely on its judgment, as there is no testimony from an accused party; rather there must be another judge above it to distinguish its errors from its correctness. On this basis, sense is not the primary judge, and that is what was sought. (1)
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is a proof: the fifth argument that sense judgment can be false. Razi begins by giving the example of snow: we perceive it as intensely white, but when examined closely it is composed of tiny transparent and colorless icy particles. So snow itself is not colored, yet we perceive it as white. He then anticipates an objection that this whiteness arises from the reflection of light between surfaces. Razi responds that even if that is the cause, the object is not actually white; the perception of whiteness is false. This is sufficient for his purpose, which is to show that sense perception misrepresents reality. He then provides two additional examples: powdered glass appears white despite each particle being transparent and colorless, and a crack in thick transparent glass appears white due to trapped air, which is itself colorless. In all these cases, we perceive a color where there is none. From these examples, Razi concludes that sense judgments can be either false or true. Since they can be false, one cannot rely on them without a higher arbiter to distinguish errors from truths. Therefore, sense cannot be the primary judge of truth, which is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that snow has any whiteness at all in the external world.
Correction: The argument does not deny that snow appears white; it shows that the perceived whiteness is not an inherent quality of the object itself, demonstrating the fallibility of sense perception.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Razi draw from the examples of snow, powdered glass, and a crack in glass?
A) Sense perception is always false.
B) Sense perception can be false, so it cannot be relied upon without a higher judge.
C) Snow, glass, and air are all inherently white.
D) Reflection of light is the only cause of whiteness.

Key Takeaway
Sense perception can attribute qualities to objects that they do not inherently possess, proving that sense cannot be the ultimate judge of truth.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi shows that even something as universally accepted as snow's whiteness can be an illusion. What belief about yourself or your life have you held as 'obvious' that might actually be a deception of your senses or unchecked assumptions? Will you examine it today?
?
The passage concludes that there must be a higher judge to distinguish truth from error in sense perception. In what areas of your faith or daily decisions are you trusting your own perception over Allah's revelation? What step will you take this week to submit that area to God's judgment?
?
If your senses can be wrong about something as simple as color, how much more can they mislead you about matters of the unseen (e.g., divine attributes, afterlife)? How will you actively guard against relying on 'what you see' in your spiritual life?

Senses Do Not Apprehend Universals
Razi argues that the senses cannot provide universal judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why sense perception cannot deliver universal judgments, according to Razi's argument.

Core Idea
Sense perceives only individuals, not the universal 'every'.

This passage from Razi's work presents a crucial epistemological argument: universals, such as the proposition 'every whole is greater than its part', cannot be derived from sense experience alone. Razi explains that sense only grasps particular instances, not the universal quantification that covers all possible cases. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic original once to get the rhythm, then read the translation. Note the key terms: كليات, جزئيات, كل, جزء, أعظمية.
2
Identify the main claim
Razi states that sense does not give universals at all. Find where he makes this claim and how he supports it.
3
Follow the example
Razi uses the example of 'every whole is greater than its part'. Notice how he breaks it down: sense sees this whole and this part, but not the universal 'greaterness' or the universal quantifier.
4
Understand the final point
Razi argues that 'every whole' includes possible, not just actual, wholes. Since sense only perceives actuals, it cannot cover possibles.
This refutes the idea that sense could infer universals by induction.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi concludes that sense has no power to give universals. This shows that universal knowledge requires a different faculty (like reason).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الكليات فالحس لا يعطيها البتة، فإن الحمس لا يشاهد إلا هذا الكل وهذا الجزء ، فأما وصف الأعظمية فهو غير مدرك بالحس، وبتقدر أن يكون ذلك الوصف مدركا لكن المدرك هو أن هذا الكل أعظم من هذا الجزء ، فأما أن كل «كل ، فهو أعظم من جزئه فغير مدرك بالحس ، ولو أدرك كل ما في الوجود من الكليات والأجزاء ، لأن قولنا كل كذا ليس المراد منه كل ما فى الوجود الخارجي من تلك الماهية فقط ، بل كل ما لو وجد فى الخارج أصدق عليه أنه فرد من أفراد تلك الماهية ، وذلك مما لا يمكن وقوع الإحساس به فثبت ، أن الحس لا نة له على إعطاء الكليات البتة (٢)
As for universals, sense does not give them at all. For sense does not observe anything except this whole and this part. As for the description of 'greaterness', it is not perceived by sense. And if it is assumed that that description is perceived, yet what is perceived is that this whole is greater than this part. But that 'every whole is greater than its part' is not perceived by sense. Even if one perceived every whole and every part existing in the world, because our saying 'every such thing' does not mean only every thing existing externally of that essence, but rather everything that would—if it existed externally—truly be called an individual of that essence. And that is impossible to be sensed. So it is established that sense has no power [؟] to give universals at all. (2)
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is a proof: the argument that the senses cannot provide universal judgments. Razi begins by stating that sense only observes particular instances: this specific whole and this specific part. The universal property of 'greaterness' (i.e., the relation of being greater) is not perceived by the senses. Even if one could perceive the greaterness of this whole relative to this part, the universal proposition 'every whole is greater than its part' involves a universal quantifier that is not sensed. Moreover, Razi adds an epistemological point: the statement 'every whole' does not refer only to actually existing wholes, but to all possible wholes that would exist if they were to come into existence. Since sense can only perceive actual objects, it cannot encompass possible objects. Therefore, sense has no capacity to deliver universal judgments. This demonstrates that universals are not derived from sense alone.

جزئيات

جزء
أعظمية

مدرك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that because we sense particular wholes and parts, we can derive universal statements by induction.
Correction: Razi argues that universal statements involve quantification over possible individuals, not just actual ones, so induction from sensed instances is insufficient.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say sense cannot give the universal 'every whole is greater than its part'?
A) Because sense perceives only this whole and this part, not the universal relation.
B) Because sense cannot perceive any wholes or parts.
C) Because the universal is always true, but sense is fallible.
D) Because 'greaterness' is a quality that cannot be perceived.

Key Takeaway
Sense perception alone is insufficient to establish universal judgments; universals require a non-sensory source such as reason.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Has your reliance on sensory experience ever hindered your submission to a broader Islamic truth (e.g., the wisdom in a command you find difficult)? What would it take for you to trust the universal over the particular today?

First Argument Against Primary Intuitions: Non-Contradiction
Razi presents the third group's first argument that the strongest primary intuition is not certain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third group argues that even the strongest primary intuition (the law of non-contradiction) is not certain, thereby weakening the foundation of all primary intuitions.

Core Idea
If the strongest primary intuition fails, all weaker ones fall.

This passage presents a powerful skeptical challenge. The third group accepts sense data but attacks primary intellectual principles. They argue that the greatest primary intuition—the law of non-contradiction—is itself uncertain because other supposedly basic principles are merely derived from it. This sets the stage for Razi's refutation later.

Guided Path
1
Identify the group's strategy
Read the passage and note that the group first claims intelligibles derive from sensibles, then introduces five arguments. Focus on the first argument.
They accept sense perceptions, so their attack is on rational intuitions alone.
2
Locate the four examples
Find the four examples of primary intuitions that defenders typically cite. The first is the law of non-contradiction; the other three are listed.
The examples are: (1) non-contradiction, (2) whole > part, (3) things equal to same are equal, (4) one body not in two places.
3
Understand the derivation claim
Notice that the group says the last three are derived from the first. This is the key move: if they are derived, the first is not truly foundational.
Derivation here means the first principle is presupposed by the others.
4
Grasp the conclusion
If the strongest (non-contradiction) is not certain, then the weaker derived principles are even less certain. Thus primary intuitions as a class are unreliable.
The passage ends here; the remaining four arguments are presumably in later sections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الفرقة الثالثة : الذين يعترفون بالحسبات ويقدحون في البديهيات )
قالوا : المعقولات فرع المحسوسات ، ولذلك فإن من فتمد حا فقد علما ، كالأكمه والعنين والأصل أقوى من الفرع (٣)
ثم الذي يدل على ضعف البديهات" وجوه خمسة .
أحدها: أن أجلى البديهيات العلم بأن الشيء إما أن يكون وإما أن لا يكون ، ثم إن هذه القضية ليست يقينية ، وإذا لم يكن أقوى الأوليات يقينيا فما ظنك بأضعفها . بيان الأول وهو أنا رأينا المعولين على البديهيات يذكرون لها أمثلة أربعة .
أحدها : أن النفى والإثبات لا يجتمعان ولا يرتفعان ) وثانيها ) : أن الكل أعظم من الجزء (وكالتها) أن الأشياء المساوية للشيء الواحد متساوية . ورابعها : أن الجسم الواحد لا يكون في مكانين مما ووجدنا هذه الثلاثة الأخيرة متفرعة على الأول (٤)
(The third group: those who acknowledge sense perceptions and impugn primary intuitions)
They said: The intelligibles are branches of the sensibles, and therefore whoever loses a sense loses knowledge, like a blind person and a eunuch, and the root is stronger than the branch. (3)
Then that which indicates the weakness of primary intuitions is five ways.
The first: The clearest of primary intuitions is the knowledge that a thing either is or is not. Then this proposition is not certain. And if the strongest of primary principles is not certain, what do you think about its weakest? Explanation of the first: We see that those who rely on primary intuitions mention for them four examples: First: Negation and affirmation cannot coexist nor both be absent. Second: The whole is greater than the part. Third: Things equal to a single thing are equal. Fourth: A single body cannot be in two places. And we found these last three derived from the first. (4)
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is an argument from an opposing group (the third group) against the reliability of primary intuitions (badihiyyat). Razi first identifies the group: they accept sense perceptions but attack primary intellectual principles. They claim that intelligible concepts are derived from sensations, so sensation is the root and is stronger. They then present five arguments to show the weakness of primary intuitions. Here we have the first argument. They state that the clearest and strongest primary intuition is the law of non-contradiction: a thing either exists or does not exist. However, this proposition is not certain. Why? Because those who defend primary intuitions typically cite four examples of such intuitions: (1) affirmation and negation cannot be true together nor both false; (2) the whole is greater than the part; (3) things equal to the same thing are equal to each other; (4) one body cannot be in two places. The last three are derived from the first, meaning they are not truly primary but depend on the first. This implies that even the first principle is not foundational in the strong sense, because its certainty should be independent. If the strongest is not certain, then the weaker ones (the other examples) are even less so. Thus the group argues that primary intuitions are weak. The passage ends with this first argument; the remaining four are presumably dealt with later. Note: Razi is presenting this to refute it, as indicated by his role as a compiler of arguments.

Key Terms

أوليات
يقيني
نفي وإثبات
كل وجزء
تساوي
جسم في مكانين
حسيات
معقولات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is Razi's own view that primary intuitions are uncertain.
Correction: Razi is reporting an argument from a skeptical group to refute it later; he does not endorse the conclusion.

Checkpoint
According to the third group, what is the basis for claiming that the law of non-contradiction is not certain?
A) It contradicts sense experience.
B) The other three examples of primary intuitions are derived from it, showing it is not independent.
C) It cannot be proven by logic.
D) It is not universally accepted.

Key Takeaway
The first argument against primary intuitions claims that the law of non-contradiction, the strongest, is not certain because other supposedly primary principles are derived from it, weakening its foundational status.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just encountered an argument that the most obvious truth you rely on may not be certain. Is your faith in Allah built on a foundation you have never examined? Are you willing to probe the bedrock of your convictions, knowing that doubt may be the first step to deeper certainty?
?
The skeptics claim that all knowledge comes from the senses. Do you live as if your senses are enough to know God? When did you last let revelation question what your eyes and mind take for granted? How comfortable are you with being uncomfortable about the source of your beliefs?
?
If the law of non-contradiction itself is challenged, what does that do to your sense of intellectual security? Are you prepared to have your most basic assumptions tested, or do you prefer the comfort of unexamined certainties? What would it take for you to truly own your beliefs, rather than inherit them?

Third Argument Against Intuitive Knowledge
Third argument and its refutation regarding intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third argument against intuitive knowledge is refuted by appealing to the impossibility of transforming realities.

Core Idea
Possibilities that contradict the nature of reality are not real possibilities.

The third argument against intuitive knowledge suggests that when we are away from our house, objects might have changed and then reverted. This challenges our certainty about the world. Imam Razi refutes this by showing that such transformations are deemed impossible by both theologians and philosophers, so the original certainty remains intact.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the objection: while away from home, you could be wrong because objects might have changed and then reverted.
Notice the vivid examples: utensils turning into people, stones into gold, water into oil.
2
Understand the refutation
The refutation (أقول) states that most of these possibilities are impossible. For theologians, transforming realities (qalb al-haqa'iq) is impossible; for philosophers, substituting forms is impossible.
The refutation does not appeal to empirical observation but to metaphysical principles.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this refutation protects the reliability of intuitive knowledge by rejecting certain logical possibilities as genuinely impossible.
This argument shows that not every logical possibility undermines certainty; some possibilities are ruled out by the nature of reality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : أنى إذا خرجت من دارى فإني أعلم أن ما فيها من الأوانى وغيره لم ينقلب أناسا فضلاء مدققين في علوم المنطق والهندسة ، ولم ينقلب ما فيها من الأحجار ذهبا وياقوتا ، وأنه ليس تحت رحلى ياقوت بمقدار مائة ألف من ، وأن مياه البحار والأودية لم ينقلب أدما ودهنا ، والاحتمال في الكل قائم ، ولا يندفع ذلك بأني إذا نظرت إليها ثانيا وجدتها كما كانت لاحتمال أن يقال إنها انقلبت إلى هذه الصفات في زمان فيدى عنها ثم عند عودى اليها صارت كما كانت للفاعل المختار أو للشكل الغريب . أقول : أكثر ما ذكر من هذه الاحتمالات محال ؛ فإن قلب الحقائق عند المتكلمين محال مقدور عليه ، وتبديل هذه الصورة بالصور التي ذكرها عند الفلاسفة ممتنع .
The third: That when I leave my house, I know that the utensils and other things in it have not turned into excellent, precise people in the sciences of logic and geometry, and that the stones in it have not turned into gold and rubies, and that under my saddle there is not a ruby weighing a hundred thousand mann, and that the waters of the seas and valleys have not turned into oil and fat. The possibility remains in all of this, and it is not refuted by my looking at them again and finding them as they were, due to the possibility that it is said they changed into these qualities at a time [I was away?], then when I returned they became as they were by the free agent or by the strange form. I say: Most of these possibilities mentioned are impossible; for the transformation of realities is impossible for the theologians, and the substitution of these forms with the forms he mentioned is impossible according to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument against the reliability of intuitive knowledge (badihiyyat). The objector argues that when you leave your house, you are certain that objects inside have not turned into other things (e.g., utensils into people, stones into gold, water into oil), but this certainty is not justified because the possibility of such transformations exists—they could have changed while you were away and then reverted when you returned, either by divine action or some strange form. Your subsequent perception does not guarantee they remained unchanged. The refutation (أقول) responds that these possibilities are false. For theologians (mutakallimun), the transformation of realities (qalb al-haqa'iq) is impossible; for philosophers, such substitution of forms is also impossible. Thus the original certainty stands.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any logical possibility, however far-fetched, can undermine certainty.
Correction: The refutation shows that possibilities that violate metaphysical principles are not genuine possibilities and thus do not threaten certainty.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why is the objection that objects might change while one is away invalid?
A) Because the objects are always observed by someone.
B) Because such transformations are impossible for theologians and philosophers.
C) Because the house is locked.
D) Because the objects are too heavy to move.

Key Takeaway
The third argument against intuitive knowledge is refuted by showing that the hypothetical transformations are impossible according to both theology and philosophy, preserving the reliability of certain knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are certain about something in your life (like the safety of your home while you're away), have you ever doubted it due to 'what if' possibilities? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to entertain possibilities that are actually impossible? What would it mean for your daily trust in Allah's preservation if you truly accepted that some possibilities are not real possibilities?
?
The objector uses examples of stones turning to gold, etc. Are you guilty of allowing similar 'possibilities' to erode your confidence in what you know from revelation and reason? Commit to identifying one area where you have allowed hypothetical doubts to weaken your certainty, and decide how to apply the refutation's principle today.
?
Imagine you are leaving your house tomorrow. Will you truly walk out with the certainty that the utensils will not turn into people? Or will you entertain the 'possibility'? How does embracing the impossibility of such transformations affect your sense of security and reliance on Allah's consistent order?

Fourth Argument: Speech and Actions as Indicators of Understanding
Fourth argument and its refutation regarding speech and actions as indicators of understanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against intuitive certainty from speech and actions, and how the refutation distinguishes human from non-human indicators.

Core Idea
Organized speech directly indicates rationality in humans, but in non-humans it indicates a powerful agent – a key distinction.

This lesson examines a skeptical challenge: when you hear organized speech, you feel certain the speaker understands, but is that certainty justified? The argument highlights that sounds are just disconnected sounds and actions could have other causes. The refutation, however, shows that for humans the inference is necessary, and for non-humans it still points to a powerful knowing agent. Understanding this clarifies how we rationally know minds from behavior.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument
Start by reading the objector's claim that our certainty of understanding from speech and actions is not rationally grounded.
2
Deconstruct the reasoning
Notice the argument's two parts: speech and actions. Why do they fail to indicate a living rational essence according to the objector?
The objector suggests possibilities that could produce the same effects.
3
Examine the refutation
Read the theologians' response. They distinguish human speech from other speech and note that precise actions always indicate a knowing agent.
Pay attention to the shift: for humans it indicates life and rationality; for non-humans it indicates a living, knowing, powerful agent.
4
Synthesize the defense
Consider how this defense resolves the doubt. Does it preserve intuitive knowledge for humans while also explaining non-human cases?
The objection fails because it conflates different contexts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : إذا خاطبت إنسانا يتكلم بكلام منظوم مرتب يوافق الخطابي ، فعلمت بالضرورة أنه فاهم ، وهذا الجزم غير ثابت لأن المقتضى لذلك الجزم إما أقواله أو أفعاله ، أما الأول فلا أصوات منقطعة وحصولها فى الذات لا يقتضى كون الذات حيا عاقلا ، وأما الأفعال فلا تدل أيضاً لاحتمال يوجب أن الفاعل المختار أو الشكل الغريب اقتضى حصول تلك الأفعال المخصوصة الدالة على ما يوافق غرض المخاطب ، فثبت أن القول والفعل لا يدلان على كونه حيا عاقلا فاهما . مع أنا نضطر إلى العلم بذلك . أقول : قال المتكلمون : صدور الكلام المنظوم من شخص هو إنسان يدا، بالضرورة على كونه حيا عاقلا ، ولا يندفع ذلك بما قاله ، أما فى غير الإنسان فلا يدل على كون ذلك الشخص حيا عاقلا ، إنما يدل على أن الذات الفني يصدر عنها ذلك الكلام حى عالم قادر ، وأما الأفعال فلا خلاف في أنها إذا كانت محكمة متقنة كان فاهلها عالما قادرا ، فهذا الشك ليس بقادح فيما أراد قدحه لا على مذهب المتكلمين ولا على مذهب الفلاسفة .
The fourth: When you address a person who speaks organized, ordered speech that matches the discourse, you know by necessity that he understands. But this certainty is not established, because what necessitates that certainty is either his words or his actions. As for the first, sounds are discontinuous and their occurrence in the essence does not necessitate that the essence is living, rational. As for actions, they also do not indicate, due to the possibility that the free agent or the strange form necessitated the occurrence of those specific actions that indicate what suits the purpose of the addressee. So it is established that speech and action do not indicate that he is living, rational, understanding, even though we are compelled to know that. I say: The theologians said: The occurrence of organized speech from a person who is a human indicates necessarily that he is living, rational, and that is not refuted by what he said. As for other than humans, it does not indicate that that person is living, rational; rather it indicates that the essence from which that speech originates is living, knowing, powerful. As for actions, there is no disagreement that when they are precise and perfect, their doer is knowing and powerful. So this doubt does not affect what he intended to refute, neither according to the theologians nor according to the philosophers.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against intuitive knowledge. The objector claims that when you hear organized speech from a person, you instinctively know they understand, but this is not certain. The grounds for certainty would be either words or actions. Words are merely disconnected sounds that do not necessitate a living, rational essence. Actions could be produced by a free agent or a strange form (shakl gharib) that causes them to indicate the speaker's intent, so they don't prove understanding. The refutation (أقول) responds that theologians affirm that organized speech from a human necessarily indicates life and rationality, and this cannot be refuted by such possibilities. For non-humans, speech indicates a powerful, knowing agent, not necessarily the speaker's humanity. As for actions, when they are perfect and precise, they undeniably indicate a knowing and powerful doer. Thus the objection has no force against either theologians or philosophers.

Key Terms
كلام منظوم
الفاعل المختار
الشكل الغريب
بديهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All cases of organized speech and actions are equally uncertain indicators of understanding.
Correction: The refutation distinguishes human speech (which necessarily indicates rationality) from non-human speech (which indicates a powerful agent), and precise actions always indicate a knowing doer.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, how does the argument from organized speech differ for humans compared to non-humans?
A) It indicates life and rationality only for humans.
B) It indicates a powerful agent in both cases.
C) It does not indicate anything for humans.
D) It indicates life for non-humans but not for humans.

Key Takeaway
Organized speech from a human necessarily indicates rationality, while from non-humans it indicates a powerful knowing agent, thus the skeptical doubt is resolved.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite or hear the Quran—organized speech from Allah—do you truly feel the weight of it as a direct sign that He is living, knowing, and powerful? Or do you read it as mere words and lose the certainty this lesson demands? Today, commit to pausing at one verse and letting that certainty penetrate your heart.
?
In your interactions with others, do you recognize that their organized speech is a direct indication of their soul and rationality? How does this change the way you listen to them, especially when you disagree? Will you choose to honour that certainty by being patient and respectful?
?
The objector raised 'possibilities' to sow doubt about what is obvious. Are there areas of your faith where you entertain intellectual doubts that, upon reflection, are shown to be groundless by the very structure of reality? Will you actively seek the refutation of such doubts from scholars and scripture, rather than let them fester?

The Fifth Argument: Angelic Disguises and the Refutation
Fifth argument and refutation regarding angelic disguises and intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how skeptics argue that angelic appearances undermine perceptual certainty, and how Muslim scholars refute this by distinguishing between possible and impossible reports and affirming the intuitive nature of basic knowledge.

Core Idea
Angelic appearances do not make all perception unreliable; intuitive knowledge remains certain even for children.

The fifth argument attacks the reliability of innate intuitive judgments by pointing to reported angelic appearances. If an angel can appear as a person, then any perceived person could be an angel in disguise, casting doubt on our certainty about everyday perceptions. The response clarifies the principles for handling such reports and shows that basic perceptual certainty is intuitive, not inferential, and thus unaffected by such hypothetical possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the skeptical argument
Read the first part of the passage where the skeptics present the issue of Gabriel appearing as Dihya, and the extrapolation to general doubt about perceptual judgments.
Notice how the argument extends from a single case to all perceived objects.
2
Identify the objection
Focus on the line 'It is not said...' which introduces a counterargument that these judgments might be inferential rather than intuitive.
This objection tries to save the skeptic's point by reclassifying the knowledge.
3
Study the principle for handling reports
Examine the section beginning 'I say: The investigators...' where the response gives the rule for handling reports from truthful informants.
This principle resolves confusion about angelic appearances.
4
Understand the intuitive nature argument
Read the final part where the author argues that certainty about identity is intuitive, proven by children and madmen having it.
This is the decisive refutation showing intuitive knowledge is real.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس : أنكم رويتم فى الأخبار أن جبريل عليه السلام كان يظهر فى صورة دحية الكلبي ، وإذا لم يمتنع فى بديهة العقل لم يمتنع أن يظهر في صورة سائر الأشخاص ، فإذا رأيت ولدى فامله ليس ولدى بل هو جبريل ، بل الذبابة التي طارت فى الهواء لعلها ليست ذبابة بل هى ملك دن الملائكة ، فثبت أن هذا التجويز ثابت ، أن العلم الضرورى بعدمه حاصل ؛ فثبت بهذه الوجوه أن البديهة جازمة بهذه الأحكام مع أن جزمها باطل ولما تطرقت التهمة إليها لم يكن حكمها مقبولا إذ لا شهادة منهم . لا يقال : جزم العقل بهذه القضايا استدلالي لا بديهى . مع الكل أعظم من الجزء ، اكن التفاوت بينهما لا يبلغ حدا يجعل أحد الجزئين طبعا واعتبر القضايا التجريبية ، فإنها لا تبلغ فى الجزم حد الأوليات مع أنها يقينية بعيدة عن الارتياب ، وأما عند الفلاسفة فحال أن يتولد شبح من غير أسباب مادية واستعدادات وتربية كما مر . أقول : المحققون من المسلمين وغيرهم من أهل الملل يقولون كل ما أخبر به مخبر صادق فإن كان ممكن الوقوع حكمنا بصحته وأحلناه إلى القادر المختار ، وإن كان ممتنع الوقوع إما أن ترجع فيه إلى تأويل مطابق الأصول ديننا أو تتوقف فيه ، وإذا تقرر هذا الأصل لم يبق حيرة في موضع بماذكره أو لم يذكره ، ومن المقرر أن العلم القطعى لا ينقدح بالظنون الفاسدة والأوهام البعيدة الكاذبة . لانا نقول : لو كان كذلك لو جب أن لا يحصل هذا الجزم إلا لمن عرف ذاك الدليل، ولما لم يكن كذلك بل هو حاصل للتبيان والمجانين ولمن لا يمارس شيئا من الدلائل . علمنا أنه بديهى لا نظري، على أنا إذا ربنا إلى أنفسنا وتأملنا أحوالنا علمنا أن على أن زيدا الذي أشاهده الآن هو الذى شاهدت قبل ذلك بلحظة وأنه لا يجوز أن يقال عدم الأول وحدث مثله ليس أضعف من علمى بأن الشيء إما أن يكون موجوداً أو معدوما .
The fifth: You have narrated in reports that Gabriel (peace be upon him) used to appear in the form of Dihya al-Kalbi. Since that is not impossible in the innate intellect, it is not impossible that he appears in the form of other persons. So when you see my son, perhaps he is not my son but Gabriel. Even the fly that flew in the air—perhaps it is not a fly but an angel from the angels. So it is established that this licensing is established, that necessary knowledge of its absence is obtained. So by these points it is established that the innate intellect is certain of these judgments while its certainty is false, and when doubt has affected it, its judgment is not acceptable, since there is no testimony from them. It is not said: The mind's certainty of these propositions is inferential, not intuitive. While the whole is greater than the part, the difference between them does not reach a limit that makes one of the two parts a [natural?] disposition. Consider empirical propositions, for they do not reach in certainty the level of primaries, even though they are certain, far from doubt. As for the philosophers, it is such that a phantom is generated without material causes, preparations, and upbringing as has passed. I say: The investigators among Muslims and others from the people of religions say: Whatever a truthful informant reports, if it is possible of occurrence, we judge it as true and refer it to the powerful chooser, and if it is impossible of occurrence, either we resort to an interpretation consistent with the principles of our religion or we suspend judgment. When this principle is established, there remains no confusion in any place regarding what he mentioned or did not mention. It is established that certain knowledge is not generated by corrupt conjectures and distant false illusions. We say: If it were so, then this certainty would not occur except to one who knows that proof. Since it is not so, but rather it occurs to children, madmen, and those who have no practice with proofs, we know it is intuitive, not theoretical. Moreover, if we return to ourselves and reflect on our states, we know that [we know] that Zaid whom I now see is the one I saw a moment ago, and that it is not permissible to say that the first ceased and an example occurred, is not weaker than my knowledge that a thing is either existent or non-existent.
Source pages p. 39

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument against intuitive knowledge and then responds. The objector notes that according to Islamic reports, Gabriel appeared as Dihya, so in principle angels can take human form. Thus when you see your son, he might be Gabriel; even a fly might be an angel. Hence perceptual judgments (e.g., 'this is my son') are not certain; the intuitive mind is mistaken, so its verdicts are unreliable. The response begins with 'It is not said' (لا يقال), which is a hypothetical objection that these judgments are inferential, not intuitive. The author then counters that the difference between whole and part does not make one part natural, and empirical judgments are certain though less than primaries. Then 'I say' (أقول) gives the Muslim investigator's principle: a truthful report is accepted if possible; if impossible, interpret or suspend. Thus no confusion. Then 'We say' (لانا نقول) returns: if certainty were inferential, only those who know the proof would have it, but children and madmen have it, so it is intuitive. Self-reflection shows that recognizing Zaid as the same person is as certain as the law of non-contradiction.

Key Terms
بديهة
جزم
تجويز
مخبر صادق
القادر المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All perceptual judgments are unreliable because angels can disguise themselves.
Correction: Perceptual judgments about immediate identity (like seeing Zaid) are intuitive and unaffected; reports of angelic disguises are handled by religious principles and do not undermine basic certainty.

Checkpoint
How does the response counter the claim that certainty about Zaid is not intuitive?
A) By saying that only scholars have that certainty.
B) By pointing out that even children and madmen have it, showing it is intuitive.
C) By denying that angelic appearances are possible.
D) By saying that proof is required for all knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Intuitive knowledge of perceptible identity remains certain even when angelic disguises are possible, because that certainty is immediate and shared by all rational beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own child, do you ever genuinely doubt that it is your child? How does this lesson challenge you to trust the basic perceptions Allah has given you, even when skeptics raise hypothetical doubts?
?
Are there areas of your faith where you have allowed 'what if' scenarios to erode your certainty in foundational truths? What would it take for you to regain that intuitive confidence that even children possess?
?
If you could not trust your senses to recognize your own family, how would that affect your daily life and your relationship with Allah? How can you actively thank Allah for the gift of reliable perception?
