Three Proofs Against Intuitive Knowledge
Imam Razi enumerates three skeptical proofs against intuitive knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three skeptical arguments that challenge the reliability of intuitive knowledge (badihiyyat).

Core Idea
Intuitive knowledge is attacked from three fronts: conflicting proofs, changing beliefs, and temperament and habit.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents three proofs that skeptics use to argue that intuitive knowledge is not trustworthy. The proofs highlight situations where people hold contradictory certainties, change their minds, or are influenced by temperament and upbringing. Understanding these challenges is key to grasping the nature of foundational knowledge in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the third proof
Read the third proof about conflicting proofs and inability to refute either. Notice how it claims intuitive certainty can be wrong.
2
Read the fourth proof
Read the fourth proof about changing beliefs over time, showing that initial certainty can be false.
3
Read the fifth proof
Read the fifth proof about the influence of temperament and habits on beliefs, suggesting that even common intuitions may be culturally conditioned.
4
Reflect on the collective challenge
Consider how these three proofs together question the foundation of all knowledge, setting the stage for the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : مزاولة الصنائع العقلية تدل على أن الإنسان قد يتعارض عنده دلايلان في مسألة عقلية بحيث يعجز عن القدح فى كل واحد منهما ، إما عجزا دائما أو فى بعض الأحوال . والعجز لا يتحقق إلا عند كونه مضطراً إلى اعتقاد صحة جميع المقدمات التي في الدليلين ، ولا شك أن واحدا منهما خطأ ، وإلا لصدق النقيضان ، وهذا يدل على أن البديهة قد تجزم بما لا يجوز الجزم به . الحجة الرابعة : قد يكون الإنسان جازما بصحة جميع مقدمات دليل معين ثم يتبين له خطأ في بعض تلك المقدمات ولأجل ذلك ينتقل الرجل من مذهب إلى مذهب فجزمه بصحة تلك المقدمة الباطلة باطل فظهر أن البديهة متهمة . الحجة الخامسة : أنا نو نرى لاختلاف الأمزجة والعادات تأثيرا في الاعتقادات وذلك يقدح في البديهيات أما الأمزجة غلان ضيف المزاج يستقيح الإيلام وغليظ المزاج القاسي القلب قد يستحسنه . فرب إنسان يستحسن شيئا ويستقبحه غيره . وأما العادات فهو أن الإنسان إذا مارس كلمات الفلاسفة وألفها من أول عمره إلى آخره ربما صار بحيث يقطع بصحة كل ما يقولونه وبفساد كل ما يقول مخالفوهم ، ومن مارس كلام المتكلمين كان الأمر بالعكس. وكذا القول فى أرباب الملل فإن المسلم المقلد يستقبح كلام اليهودى فى أول الوهلة واليهودى بالعكس ، وما ذاك إلا بسبب العادات، وإذا ثبت أن لاختلاف الأمزجة والعادات أثرا في الجزم بما لا يجب الجزم به ، فلعل الجزم بهذه البديهيات مزاج عام أو لإلف عام وعلى هذا القدير لا يجب الوثوق . لا يقال : إن الإنسان يفرض نفسه خالية من مقتضيات الأمزجة والعادات فما يجزم العقل به فى تلك الحالة كان حقا، لأن الجازم به فى هذه الحالة فهو صريح العقل لا المزاج والمادة . لانا نقول : هب أنا فرضنا خلو النفس على المزاج والعادة لكن فرض الخلو لا يوجب حصول الخلو، فلملنا . أقول : هذا الكلام و الدليل على أن القدح في الضروريات بما أورده من الاحتمالات لاتؤثر في (1) أقول : جزم العقل أصلا. (٢) أقول : قصور أفهام بعص الناس عن التمييز بين الحق والباطل واعتمادهم على ما يتقلدونه من آبائهم وأساتذتهم بموجب حسن ظنهم فيهم ليس بقادح في الأوليات، وأيضا التشكك في النظريات ليست تعارض الدليلين أو النقل من مذهب إلى مذهب بسبب ترجح أحد دليلين متعارضين لا يقدح في النظريات، وصناعة المنطق لا سما صناعة سوفسطيقا منه إنما بين لإرشاد العقلاء إلى طريق الحقي ومجانبة ما يقتضى الضلال في العقائد والمباحث النظرية .
The third proof: Engagement in intellectual disciplines indicates that a person may have two proofs conflicting in a rational matter such that he is unable to refute each of them, either permanently or in some circumstances. Inability does not occur except when he is compelled to believe the truth of all the premises in both proofs, and there is no doubt that one of them is false, otherwise the two contradictories would be true. This shows that the intuitive intellect may be certain of what it is not allowed to be certain of. The fourth proof: A person may be certain of the truth of all premises of a specific proof, then error becomes evident in some of those premises, and because of that he moves from one school to another. His certainty in the truth of that false premise is false, so it appears that the intuitive intellect is accused. The fifth proof: We see that differences in temperaments and habits have an effect on beliefs, and this undermines the intuitive axioms. As for temperaments, a person of delicate temperament finds causing pain repulsive, while a coarse-hearted person may find it good. So one person may consider something good and another consider it bad. As for habits, if a person has practiced the words of philosophers and become familiar with them from early life to the end, perhaps he becomes such that he is certain of the truth of everything they say and the falsehood of everything their opponents say. The one who practices the speech of theologians is the opposite. The same applies to the adherents of religions: a Muslim imitator finds the speech of a Jew repulsive at first glance, and a Jew vice versa. That is only due to habits. And if it is established that differences in temperaments and habits have an effect on certainty of what ought not to be certain of, perhaps certainty in these intuitions is due to a general temperament or general familiarity, and on this estimation trust is not obligatory. It is not said: A person can suppose himself free from the influences of temperaments and habits, and what the mind judges in that state is true, because the one who judges in that state is pure reason, not temperament and matter. We say: Even if we suppose the soul free from temperament and habit, the supposition of freedom does not necessitate actual freedom. So... I say: This speech and the proof show that the criticism of the necessary [intuitions] by the possibilities he mentioned does not affect [them]. (1) I say: The mind's certainty is fundamental. (2) I say: The deficiency of understanding of some people in distinguishing truth from falsehood, and their reliance on what they imitate from their fathers and teachers due to good opinion of them, does not criticize the primary [axioms]. Also, skepticism in theoretical matters—whether conflicting proofs or moving from one school to another due to the preponderance of one of two conflicting proofs—does not criticize theoretical [knowledge]. The science of logic, especially the sophistic art, was only established to guide rational people to the path of truth and to avoid what leads to error in beliefs and theoretical investigations.
Source pages p. 40

Commentary
This passage presents three further proofs against the reliability of intuitive knowledge (badihiyyat). The third proof: in intellectual disciplines, a person may encounter two conflicting proofs and be unable to refute either, implying certainty in both sets of premises—yet one must be false, so intuitive certainty can be mistaken. The fourth proof: a person may be certain of a proof's premises, then discover error and change beliefs; that initial certainty was false, so intuition is untrustworthy. The fifth proof: temperament and habits influence beliefs; e.g., a delicate person finds pain bad, a coarse person finds it good; habits from early training (philosophers vs. theologians, Muslim vs. Jew) shape convictions. Since such factors affect certainty, perhaps all intuitive certainty is due to a general temperament or universal custom, not objective truth. The response begins with 'It is not said'—a hypothetical defense that one can strip away these influences and rely on pure reason. But the author retorts (لانا نقول) that merely supposing freedom does not achieve it. Then 'I say' (أقول) provides the final refutation: these criticisms do not affect necessary truths (ضروريات). The first 'أقول' emphasizes that the mind's certainty is foundational. The second clarifies that deficiencies in understanding or reliance on imitation do not undermine primary axioms. Theoretical skepticism from conflicting proofs or changing schools also does not impugn theoretical knowledge. Logic, especially distinguishing genuine reasoning from sophistry, is meant to guide rational people to truth and away from error.

Key Terms
بديهة
جزم
بديهيات
مزاج
عادة
تأثير

Checkpoint
What does the third proof claim about intuitive knowledge?
A) Intuitive knowledge is always reliable.
B) Intuitive knowledge can be certain of contradictory premises, showing it can be mistaken.
C) Intuitive knowledge is unaffected by temperament.
D) Intuitive knowledge is based on habit.

Key Takeaway
These three proofs challenge the reliability of intuitive knowledge by showing situations where certainty leads to error, setting the stage for a response that defends foundational truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time when you were absolutely certain about a belief, only to later realize you were wrong. What does that say about the reliability of your own intuitive judgments? How will you guard against similar mistakes in the future?
?
Examine a conviction you hold strongly today. To what extent is it shaped by your temperament, upbringing, or the habits of your community? If you had been born into a different environment, would you still hold that belief? What does that imply about your certainty?
?
If even our most basic intuitions can be influenced by factors like temperament and habit, on what basis can you trust that your Islamic faith is not merely a product of your environment? What specific steps will you take to ensure your faith is built on solid evidence rather than inherited custom?

Method of Learning

Refutation of the Skeptical Challenges to Intuitive Knowledge
Imam Razi refutes the three skeptical proofs and defends necessary truths.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the three skeptical proofs by distinguishing necessary truths from theoretical matters and showing that the criticisms do not affect foundational intuitions.

Core Idea
The criticisms only affect theoretical matters, not foundational necessary truths.

After presenting three challenges to intuitive knowledge, the author anticipates a counter-argument and provides a definitive refutation. He argues that even if we suppose the soul free from temperament and habit, that supposition does not make it so. More importantly, he asserts that necessary truths (daruriyyat) are immune to such skeptical possibilities, and that theoretical disagreements and changing schools are not problems for foundational axioms. Logic, especially distinguishing genuine reasoning from sophistry, is meant to guide rational people to truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-argument
Read the hypothetical defense: one can suppose oneself free from temperament and habit, and then what the mind judges is pure reason.
2
Read the reply to the counter
Read the refutation: merely supposing freedom does not achieve actual freedom.
3
Read the first 'أقول' statement
Read the assertion that the mind's certainty is fundamental and the criticisms do not affect necessary truths.
4
Read the second 'أقول' statement
Read the clarification that theoretical skepticism (conflicting proofs, changing beliefs) does not undermine theoretical knowledge, and logic is meant to guide to truth.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that because beliefs vary with temperament and culture, all knowledge is relative and no truth is certain.

Key Takeaway
The skeptical proofs do not undermine necessary truths; they only affect theoretical matters, and logic is meant to guide rational people to truth.

?

Razi's Response to Skepticism: Compelling Admission Through Punishment
Razi proposes to ignore skeptical arguments and compel admission through punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's controversial method of dealing with radical skepticism by compelling admission through punishment rather than engaging in argument.

Core Idea
To defeat radical skepticism, compel admission of sensory facts through punishment.

Razi faces a radical skeptic who questions all knowledge, including sensory and self-evident truths. Instead of offering detailed refutations, Razi takes a surprising turn: he argues that the best response is to force the skeptic to admit basic sensory realities through physical punishment. This approach shocks modern sensibilities but reveals a deep point about the indubitability of certain knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Note Razi's direct and uncompromising tone.
The passage is short but dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the two types of knowledge
Razi mentions sensory things (ḥissiyyāt) and intuitive things (badīhiyyāt). Find where each is mentioned and how they relate.
Intuitive things include the distinction between pain and its absence.
3
Understand why Razi refuses to engage
Razi says that answering the skeptic's arguments actually helps them achieve their goal. He advises against it.
This is a strategic move: he knows the skeptic's goal is to create endless doubt.
4
Grasp the method of punishment
Razi proposes torture (ta'dhīb) until the skeptic admits sensory facts. Consider why punishment would work where argument fails.
This is a practical, not theoretical, response.
5
Reflect on the approach
Contrast this with a philosophical refutation. Ask yourself: does this method actually prove the skeptic wrong, or does it bypass the issue?
Razi defers detailed answers to later chapters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لانا نقول : هذا الكلام الذي ذكرته أنت يفيد القطع بالثبوت والذى ذكرته أنا يفيد التهمة والشك إنما يتولد من هذه المآخذ ، فأناشاك وشاك في أنى شاك وهلم جرا . واعلم أن الاشتغال بالجواب عن هذه الشبهة يحصل غرضهم على ما قرروه في كلماتهم ، فالصواب أنا لا نشتغل بالجواب عنها ؛ لأنا تعلم أن علمنا بأن الواحد نصف الاثنين وأن النار حارة والشمس مضيئة لا يزول بما ذكروه. بل الطريق أن يعذبوا حتى يعترفوا بالحسبات ، وإذا اعترفوا بالحسيات فقد اعترفوا بالبديهيات ، أعنى الفرق بين وجود الألم وعدمه ، وأما الأجوبة المفصلة عن هذه الأسئلة فسيجيء في الأبواب المستقيلة إن شاء الله تعالى
We say: This speech of yours that you mentioned indicates certainty of affirmation, and what I mentioned indicates accusation and doubt that only arises from these considerations. So you are a doubter and I am a doubter in that I am a doubter, and so on. Know that occupying oneself with answering this sophistry achieves their goal as they have established in their words. So the correct approach is that we do not occupy ourselves with answering it; because we know that our knowledge that one is half of two, and that fire is hot and the sun is bright, does not disappear by what they mentioned. Rather, the way is that they be tortured until they confess to sensory things. And if they confess to sensory things, then they have confessed to intuitive things, I mean the distinction between the existence of pain and its absence. As for detailed answers to these questions, they will come in the upcoming chapters, God willing.
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a response to skeptical arguments. The author begins by characterizing the skeptic's position: while your argument suggests certainty, mine yields only doubt. He then advises against engaging with such sophistries, as doing so plays into their hands. Instead, the proper method is to compel the skeptic through physical punishment until they admit basic sensory facts (ḥissiyyāt). Once they admit sensory knowledge, they implicitly admit self-evident truths (badīhiyyāt), such as the difference between pain and its absence. Detailed refutations are deferred to later chapters. Note: This reflects a practical pedagogical approach, not an epistemological compromise.

Key Terms
حسيات
بديهيات
شك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think Razi endorses epistemological brutality; rather, he is arguing that certain knowledge is indubitable and need not be defended against sophistry.
Correction: Razi's punishment method is a practical demonstration that sensory knowledge is undeniable, not an endorsement of violence as a valid argument.

Checkpoint
What method does Razi propose for dealing with the skeptic who denies sensory knowledge?
A) Offer detailed logical proofs
B) Ignore them completely
C) Torture them until they admit sensory facts
D) Appeal to intuition

Key Takeaway
Razi's response to skepticism is practical: compel admission of sensory facts through punishment rather than argue, because sensory knowledge is indubitable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever let someone's endless philosophical criticism shake your confidence in something you know by experience—like the warmth of the sun or the love of your parents? What would Razi say to you about that doubt, and what 'punishment' might you apply to yourself to silence the endless questioning and trust what you know?
?
Think of a time when you were in a debate and felt yourself getting lost in clever arguments. Razi says that engaging with sophistry 'achieves their goal.' Could it be that your desire to win the argument actually feeds your own doubt? Are you willing to walk away from a debate for the sake of preserving your certainty in Allah's signs?
?
Razi says that admitting sensory facts leads to admitting intuitive truths—like the reality of pain. When was the last time you sat with a simple, undeniable experience (heat, cold, hunger) and let it remind you that your Creator made you to know truth through your very body? Will you today pause and thank Allah for the certainty of your senses, rather than letting doubts steal that gift?

The Observer Must Not Know the Sought
Razi states the first condition and replies to an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the observer must not already know the sought conclusion, and how Razi addresses the objection of seeking a second proof.

Core Idea
Reflection is a quest; you cannot seek what you already have.

Razi begins by establishing a fundamental condition for valid reflection: the observer must not already know the conclusion. This might seem trivial, but it has implications for how we understand the purpose of reasoning. Let's examine his argument and the subtle response to a potential objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Razi presents the condition and then an objection.
The objection is introduced with 'لا يقال'.
2
Identify the condition and the objection
Identify the condition (observer must not know the sought) and the objection (one might know a thing and then seek a second proof). See how Razi responds by redefining the 'sought' in that case.
3
Reflect on why the objection fails
Razi says that in the objection, the sought is not the known thing itself but that the second proof is evidence for it—something unknown. This preserves the condition.
This shows careful conceptual distinction.

مسألة : الناظر يجب أن لا يكون عالما بالمطلوب لأن النظر طلب وطلب الحاصل محال. لا يقال : ربما علينا الشيء ثم ننظر في الاستدلال عليه بدليل ثان. لانا نقول : المطلوب هناك ليس المسلول بل كون الثاني دليلا عليه وهو غير معلوم ، وأن لا يكون جاهلا جهلا مركبا لأن صاحب هذا الجهل جازم بكونه عالما، وذلك يمنعه من الإقدام على الطلب ، ثم امتناع الاجتماع ذاتي أو للصارف فيه خلاف (1) . ثم قال المحقق في الحاشية: (1) أقول: أما من قال اجتماع النظر والجهل المركب في واحد بعينه ممتنع لذاته كاجتماع النقيضين أو المدين احتج بأن النظر يجب أن يكون مقارنا للشك والجهل المركب مقارن للجزم واجتماعه ما هو اجتماع القيمين و مناقضة اللوازم قريبة من مناقضة الملزومات ، وقال بذلك أبو هاشم ، ومن قال عدم اجتماعهما لوجود الصارف كالأكل مع الامتلاء إنما قال بذلك لأنه يجوز وجود النظر مع عدم الشك وإليه ذهب القاضي وهو مذهب الحكماء ، قالوا إن كثيراً من الناس يتعلمون من غير أن يسبق شك إلى أذهانهم ، وذهب أبو إسحاق الاسفرائيني إلى أن الناظر ممتنع أن يكون شاكا .
Question: The observer must not be knowing of the sought, because reflection is a quest, and seeking what is already obtained is impossible. It cannot be said: Perhaps we knew the thing then we reflect on the evidence for it through a second proof. Because we say: the sought there is not the known thing, but rather that the second proof is evidence for it, and that is unknown. And he must not be ignorant with compound ignorance, because the possessor of this ignorance is certain that he is knowing, and that prevents him from proceeding to the quest. Then whether the impossibility of combining them is intrinsic or due to a deterrent is disputed (1). Then the verifier said in the commentary: (1) I say: as for one who said that combining reflection and compound ignorance in one individual is intrinsically impossible like the combination of two contradictories or two opposites, he argued that reflection must be accompanied by doubt, while compound ignorance is accompanied by certainty; their combination is a combination of opposites, and the opposition of consequences is close to the opposition of antecedents. Abu Hashim held this. And one who said that they do not combine due to a deterrent, like eating with fullness, held that because it is permissible for reflection to exist without doubt; al-Qadi held this, and it is the school of the philosophers. They said that many people learn without doubt preceding their minds. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held that the observer is prevented from being doubtful.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala (question) defining the proper state of the person engaged in reflection (nazar). Razi states two necessary conditions. First, the observer must not already know the conclusion (al-matlub) because reflection is a quest (talab) and seeking what one already has is impossible. He addresses an objection: perhaps one knows a thing and then reflects on a second proof for it; but he responds that the sought in that case is not the known thing itself but the fact that the second proof is evidence for it, which is unknown. Second, the observer must not be in a state of compound ignorance (jahl murakkab), because that person is certain that he knows, which prevents him from undertaking the quest. Then Razi notes a dispute: is the impossibility of simultaneously having reflection and compound ignorance an intrinsic logical impossibility or merely due to a deterrent? The marginal note (by a later commentator) elaborates: Abu Hashim held it is intrinsic, arguing that reflection requires doubt while compound ignorance involves certainty, so they cannot coexist like opposites. Al-Qadi (Abd al-Jabbar?) and the philosophers held that it is merely due to a deterrent, citing instances of people learning without prior doubt. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held that the observer cannot be doubtful at all.

Key Terms
الناظر
المطلوب
نظر
طلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The condition means one cannot reflect on any topic about which one has prior knowledge.
Correction: The condition only excludes cases where the very same sought conclusion is already certain. Prior general knowledge does not invalidate reflection.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why must the observer not know the sought?
A) Because knowing the sought leads to arrogance
B) Because reflection is a quest and seeking what is already obtained is impossible
C) Because it causes confusion
D) Because it is forbidden by revelation

Key Takeaway
The first condition for valid reflection is that the observer does not already know the conclusion, as seeking what one already has is absurd.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sit to learn about your deen, do you come with the humility of a seeker or the confidence of one who already knows? In what specific area of your faith have you stopped seeking because you think you've already 'attained' it? What would change if you treated that area as unknown again?
?
Reflect on a belief you hold strongly. Can you recall the evidence you first relied on? Have you ever sought a second proof for that belief, not to confirm it but to deepen your understanding of why it is true? If not, what keeps you from doing so?

Avoiding Compound Ignorance in Reflection
Razi states the second condition and the scholarly dispute on its impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why compound ignorance prevents reflection, and why scholars debated whether the incompatibility is intrinsic or due to a deterrent.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance is a barrier because it breeds false certainty.

The second condition Razi sets is that the observer must not be in a state of compound ignorance. He explains the reason, then notes a scholarly dispute about the nature of the incompatibility. The marginal commentary elaborates on the positions of Abu Hashim, al-Qadi, and the philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on compound ignorance
Read the Arabic and translation covering the second condition and the ensuing dispute. Notice the definitions and the two positions.
Pay attention to the terms 'جهل مركب', 'شك', and 'جزم'.
2
Understand why compound ignorance prevents reflection
A person with compound ignorance is certain they know the truth, so they have no motivation to seek the truth. This blocks the quest (talab) that is essential to reflection.
3
Analyze the dispute: intrinsic vs. deterrent
Some scholars (Abu Hashim) say reflection (which requires doubt) and compound ignorance (which requires certainty) are intrinsically incompatible like opposites. Others (al-Qadi, philosophers) say it is merely due to a psychological deterrent, as reflection can occur without doubt.
Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held a third view that the observer cannot be doubtful at all.
4
Reflect on the implications
This dispute affects how we understand the role of doubt in reflection and whether certainty can coexist with inquiry.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Compound ignorance is simply not knowing something.
Correction: Compound ignorance is not just ignorance; it is being certain that one knows when in fact one does not. This certainty blocks the desire to seek truth.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why does compound ignorance prevent reflection?
A) Because it causes confusion

Compound ignorance blocks reflection because false certainty eliminates the motivation to seek the truth, though scholars differ on whether this is a logical or psychological barrier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there an area of your Islamic knowledge where you feel certain you are correct, but have never actually examined the evidence or considered alternative views? What would it take for you to admit that you might be in a state of compound ignorance?
?
When you approach learning about your faith, do you genuinely start with doubt and openness, or do you already assume you know the answer? How does your certainty—or lack of it—affect your willingness to seek deeper understanding?
?
The scholars differed on whether the barrier between reflection and compound ignorance is logical or only due to a deterrent. Reflect on your own experience: is your reluctance to question certain beliefs rooted in a logical impossibility to doubt, or in a psychological aversion? What step can you take today to break that barrier?

The Obligation of Reflection and the Objection from Conception
Razi states the obligation of reflection and presents an objection involving conception and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some argue that knowledge of God cannot be made obligatory due to the nature of conception and assent, and the logical challenge of infinite regress.

Core Idea
If knowledge depends on unacquired conceptions, can it truly be obligatory?

Razi begins by stating the well-known position that reflection is obligatory because it is the only way to attain obligatory knowledge of God. He then presents a powerful objection: since assent relies on conceptions that are not acquired, and either assent follows necessarily (making it unacquired) or depends on an infinite chain, knowledge cannot be commanded. This lesson unpacks that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice how Razi first states the obligation, then immediately raises an objection.
2
Identify the key components
Find the statement of obligation (وجوب النظر) and the objection (الاعتراض). The objection has two parts: the dependence of assent on conception, and the problem of infinite regress.
The objection uses terms like تصديق, تصور, أوليات.
3
Understand the logical challenge
Ask yourself: why does the objector say knowledge cannot be obligatory? Trace the reasoning: if conception is not acquired, and assent either follows necessarily or requires another premise, then no new knowledge is acquired through reflection.
This challenges the very possibility of obligation for knowledge.
4
Reflect on the stakes
Consider how this objection threatens the foundation of Islamic theology. If knowledge of God cannot be obligated, then the entire enterprise of kalam becomes questionable.
This sets up Razi's response in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: المشهور في بيان وجوب النظر أن معرفة الله تعالى واجبة ولا يمكن تحصيلها إلا بالنظر وما يتوقف الواجب المطلق عليه وكان مقدوراً المكلف فهو واجب على ما سيأتي بيانه في أصول الفقه - إن شاء الله تعالى . الاعتراض عليه : لا نسلم أنه يمكن إيجاب العلم ، وهذا لأن التصديق يتوقف على حصول تصور الطرفين والتصور غير مكتسب على ما مر ، ثم إذا حصلا فإن كان التصديق من لوازمهما كما في الأوليات لم يكن التصديق مكتسبا أيضاً ، وإن لم يكن ضروريا افتقر فيه إلى توسط مقدمة أخرى، والحال فيها كما في الأول ولا يتسلسل معترفون بوجود الصانع كما حكى عنهم فى قوله عز من قائل : « ولئن سألتهم من خلق السموات والأرض ليقولن الله ، وفى أمثاله فلو كانت العقول كافية لقالت العرب نحن نثبت الصانع بعقولنا ونعرف توحيده ولا نحتاج في ذلك إليك. وقد اختصر مقدمتهم هذه في كلام موجز وهو قوله النقل يكنى أم لا فإن كان يكفى فليس لأحد من الخلق حتى الأنبياء عليهم السلام هداية غيره من العقلاء ، وإن لم يكف فهو اعتراف بالاحتياج إلى التعليم ، ولهم كلام كثير في إثبات مذهبهم . والحق أن التعليم في المعقولات ليس بضرورى مع أنه إعانة وهداية وحث على استعمال العقل وفى المقولات ضرورى ، والأنبياء ما جاءوا لتعليم العقل الأول وحده بل له وللمصنف الثاني فإن المقال لا يتطرق إلى ما يرشدون إليه . وأما قوله إنا نطالبهم بتعين ذلك الإمام ونبين أنه من أجهل الناس ، فغير لازم عليهم لأنهم ما يدعون أن إمامهم يعلمهم علما إما يدعون أن متابعته والاعتراف بإمامته إذا صار مضافا إلى المعارف العقلية وغيرها حصل النجاة ، وإلا فلا، وضعف هذه الدعوى وتعريها عن الحجة ظاهر غير محتاج فيها إلى إطناب
Question: The well-known in explaining the obligation of reflection is that knowledge of God, Exalted, is obligatory, and it cannot be obtained except through reflection. And whatever the absolute obligation depends on, and is within the capacity of the accountable person, is obligatory, as will be explained in usul al-fiqh, God willing. Objection to it: we do not concede that knowledge can be made obligatory. This is because assent depends on obtaining a conception of both ends, and the conception is not acquired, as has passed. Then when they are obtained, if the assent is a necessary consequence of them, as in primary truths, then the assent is not acquired either; and if it is not necessary, it depends on the mediation of another premise, and the situation with it is as with the first, and so on without infinite regress. Those who acknowledge the existence of the Maker, as related about them in His saying, 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say God,' and similar verses. So if intellects were sufficient, the Arabs would have said: we affirm the Maker by our intellects and know His oneness and have no need of you in that. And they summarized this premise of theirs in a concise statement, namely his saying: 'Does transmission suffice or not?' If it suffices, then no one among creation, not even the prophets, peace be upon them, has guidance other than the intellectual ones; and if it does not suffice, then it is an admission of the need for teaching. And they have much speech in proving their school. The truth is that teaching in intelligible matters is not necessary, while it is assistance, guidance, and incitement to use the intellect; and in transmitted matters it is necessary. The prophets did not come to teach the first intellect alone, but for it and for the second [aspect]; for the [objection] does not apply to what they guide to. As for his statement: 'We demand them to specify that imam and show that he is from the most ignorant of people,' it is not binding on them, because they do not claim that their imam teaches them knowledge; rather they claim that following him and acknowledging his imamate, when added to intellectual and other cognitions, results in salvation; otherwise not. The weakness of this claim and its lack of evidence is apparent and does not require elaboration.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala (question) concerning the well-known position that reflection (nazar) is obligatory. Razi states the argument: knowledge of God is obligatory, and it can only be obtained through reflection; therefore reflection is obligatory, by the principle that whatever an absolute obligation depends on and is within the capacity of the accountable person is itself obligatory. An objection is then raised: one cannot make knowledge obligatory because assent (tasdiq) depends on conception (tasawwur), which is not acquired, and if assent follows necessarily from conception (as in primary truths), it is also not acquired; if not, it requires another premise ad infinitum. Razi counters by citing the Arabs' acknowledgment of the Maker, showing that their intellects recognized His existence but they still needed prophets for guidance. He summarizes their argument in a concise dilemma: either transmitted guidance (naql) suffices or not; if it does, no one needs prophets; if not, it admits the need for teaching. Razi then clarifies the truth: teaching is not necessary for intelligible matters (ma'qulat) but is assistance and incitement to use the intellect; for transmitted matters (manqulat) it is necessary. Prophets came to teach both the first intellect (innate knowledge) and the second aspect (detailed guidance). The objection about specifying the imam is dismissed because the opponents claim salvation through following the imam combined with intellectual knowledge, not through teaching alone; their claim is weak and evidentially unsupported.

Key Terms
وجوب النظر
معرفة الله
تصديق
تصور
أوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the obligation of reflection is straightforward and uncontested.
Correction: Razi shows that there is a deep objection based on the nature of knowledge acquisition, which must be addressed.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why can't knowledge of God be made obligatory?
A) Because reflection is not within human capacity
B) Because assent depends on conception, which is not acquired, leading to infinite regress or necessity
C) Because knowledge of God is impossible
D) Because reflection leads to doubt

Key Takeaway
The objection challenges the very possibility of commanding knowledge acquisition, arguing that reflection cannot produce new obligatory knowledge due to the nature of conception and assent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the objection were sound and knowledge of God could not be commanded, would that give you an excuse to stop seeking Him? Yet here you are, studying this very lesson—what does that tell you about the reality of the obligation you feel?
?
The objection claims that conception is not acquired—that your basic ideas of things come to you without effort. But reflect on your own experience: are there not moments when you struggle to conceive of God's attributes? Does that struggle itself feel like a command from within?
?
If assent can only be necessary or involve infinite regress, then your current act of reading and understanding this lesson must either be necessary or absurd. Which do you truly believe it is, and what does your answer commit you to in your relationship with your Creator?

Razi's Response: Intellect, Transmission, and the Role of Prophets
Razi responds to the objection using the example of Arabs, a dilemma about transmission, and clarifies the role of teaching.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's response to the objection: he shows that the Arabs' innate acknowledgment of the Creator does not suffice for complete guidance, and clarifies the complementary roles of intellect and prophetic teaching.

Core Idea
Intellect grasps the Creator, but prophets are needed to complete guidance.

Razi counters the objection by pointing out that even unbelievers like the Arabs acknowledge a Creator—yet they still need prophets for detailed guidance. He then presents a dilemma about transmission (naql) and clarifies that teaching in intelligible matters is not necessary but helpful, while in transmitted matters it is essential. This lesson unpacks that nuanced position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'معترفون بوجود الصانع' to the end. Follow the translation carefully.
Notice the shift from objection to response.
2
Analyze Razi's counter-example
Razi invokes the Arabs who, despite acknowledging the Creator, did not think their intellects sufficed for worship. Ask: what does this show about the necessity of prophetic guidance?
This refutes the idea that intellect alone is enough.
3
Understand the dilemma of transmission
Razi presents a concise dilemma: either transmitted guidance (naql) suffices or not. If it suffices, no one needs prophets; if not, teaching is needed. He then resolves it by distinguishing between intelligible and transmitted matters.
The dilemma forces the opponent to clarify.
4
Grasp the role of teaching
Razi explains that teaching in intelligibles is not necessary but assists, while in transmitted matters it is essential. Prophets address both the innate intellect (first intellect) and the need for detailed guidance (second aspect).
This preserves the role of both reason and revelation.
5
Examine the final rebuttal
The objection about specifying an imam is dismissed: the opponent claims salvation through following the imam combined with intellectual knowledge, not through teaching alone. Razi calls this weak.
This is a side note against a specific group.

مسألة: المشهور في بيان وجوب النظر أن معرفة الله تعالى واجبة ولا يمكن تحصيلها إلا بالنظر وما يتوقف الواجب المطلق عليه وكان مقدوراً المكلف فهو واجب على ما سيأتي بيانه في أصول الفقه - إن شاء الله تعالى . الاعتراض عليه : لا نسلم أنه يمكن إيجاب العلم ، وهذا لأن التصديق يتوقف على حصول تصور الطرفين والتصور غير مكتسب على ما مر ، ثم إذا حصلا فإن كان التصديق من لوازمهما كما في الأوليات لم يكن التصديق مكتسبا أيضاً ، وإن لم يكن ضروريا افتقر فيه إلى توسط مقدمة أخرى، والحال فيها كما في الأول ولا يتسلسل معترفون بوجود الصانع كما حكى عنهم فى قوله عز من قائل : « ولئن سألتهم من خلق السموات والأرض ليقولن الله ، وفى أمثاله فلو كانت العقول كافية لقالت العرب نحن نثبت الصانع بعقولنا ونعرف توحيده ولا نحتاج في ذلك إليك. وقد اختصر مقدمتهم هذه في كلام موجز وهو قوله النقل يكنى أم لا فإن كان يكفى فليس لأحد من الخلق حتى الأنبياء عليهم السلام هداية غيره من العقلاء ، وإن لم يكف فهو اعتراف بالاحتياج إلى التعليم ، ولهم كلام كثير في إثبات مذهبهم . والحق أن التعليم في المعقولات ليس بضرورى مع أنه إعانة وهداية وحث على استعمال العقل وفى المقولات ضرورى ، والأنبياء ما جاءوا لتعليم العقل الأول وحده بل له وللمصنف الثاني فإن المقال لا يتطرق إلى ما يرشدون إليه . وأما قوله إنا نطالبهم بتعين ذلك الإمام ونبين أنه من أجهل الناس ، فغير لازم عليهم لأنهم ما يدعون أن إمامهم يعلمهم علما إما يدعون أن متابعته والاعتراف بإمامته إذا صار مضافا إلى المعارف العقلية وغيرها حصل النجاة ، وإلا فلا، وضعف هذه الدعوى وتعريها عن الحجة ظاهر غير محتاج فيها إلى إطناب
Question: The well-known in explaining the obligation of reflection is that knowledge of God, Exalted, is obligatory, and it cannot be obtained except through reflection. And whatever the absolute obligation depends on, and is within the capacity of the accountable person, is obligatory, as will be explained in usul al-fiqh, God willing. Objection to it: we do not concede that knowledge can be made obligatory. This is because assent depends on obtaining a conception of both ends, and the conception is not acquired, as has passed. Then when they are obtained, if the assent is a necessary consequence of them, as in primary truths, then the assent is not acquired either; and if it is not necessary, it depends on the mediation of another premise, and the situation with it is as with the first, and so on without infinite regress. Those who acknowledge the existence of the Maker, as related about them in His saying, 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say God,' and similar verses. So if intellects were sufficient, the Arabs would have said: we affirm the Maker by our intellects and know His oneness and have no need of you in that. And they summarized this premise of theirs in a concise statement, namely his saying: 'Does transmission suffice or not?' If it suffices, then no one among creation, not even the prophets, peace be upon them, has guidance other than the intellectual ones; and if it does not suffice, then it is an admission of the need for teaching. And they have much speech in proving their school. The truth is that teaching in intelligible matters is not necessary, while it is assistance, guidance, and incitement to use the intellect; and in transmitted matters it is necessary. The prophets did not come to teach the first intellect alone, but for it and for the second [aspect]; for the [objection] does not apply to what they guide to. As for his statement: 'We demand them to specify that imam and show that he is from the most ignorant of people,' it is not binding on them, because they do not claim that their imam teaches them knowledge; rather they claim that following him and acknowledging his imamate, when added to intellectual and other cognitions, results in salvation; otherwise not. The weakness of this claim and its lack of evidence is apparent and does not require elaboration.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a mas'ala (question) concerning the well-known position that reflection (nazar) is obligatory. Razi states the argument: knowledge of God is obligatory, and it can only be obtained through reflection; therefore reflection is obligatory, by the principle that whatever an absolute obligation depends on and is within the capacity of the accountable person is itself obligatory. An objection is then raised: one cannot make knowledge obligatory because assent (tasdiq) depends on conception (tasawwur), which is not acquired, and if assent follows necessarily from conception (as in primary truths), it is also not acquired; if not, it requires another premise ad infinitum. Razi counters by citing the Arabs' acknowledgment of the Maker, showing that their intellects recognized His existence but they still needed prophets for guidance. He summarizes their argument in a concise dilemma: either transmitted guidance (naql) suffices or not; if it does, no one needs prophets; if not, it admits the need for teaching. Razi then clarifies the truth: teaching is not necessary for intelligible matters (ma'qulat) but is assistance and incitement to use the intellect; for transmitted matters (manqulat) it is necessary. Prophets came to teach both the first intellect (innate knowledge) and the second aspect (detailed guidance). The objection about specifying the imam is dismissed because the opponents claim salvation through following the imam combined with intellectual knowledge, not through teaching alone; their claim is weak and evidentially unsupported.

Key Terms
عقل
نقل
تعليم
إمام
أوليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that Razi denies the necessity of teaching altogether or that intellect is completely sufficient.
Correction: Razi affirms that teaching is necessary in transmitted matters and helpful in intelligibles, preserving the role of prophets.

Checkpoint
What does Razi say about the role of teaching in intelligible matters?
A) It is obligatory but not necessary
B) It is not necessary but is assistance and encouragement
C) It is completely unnecessary
D) It is the only way to gain knowledge

Key Takeaway
Razi maintains that intellect alone can know the Creator, but prophetic teaching is essential for complete guidance, especially in transmitted matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely solely on your intellect to make important decisions about your faith, have you ever felt a need for divine guidance? How has ignoring prophetic teaching led you astray?
?
Razi says that teaching in transmitted matters is necessary. Are you actively seeking that teaching through the Quran and Sunnah, or do you think your intellect alone can guide you to salvation?
?
The Arabs acknowledged the Creator but still needed prophets. In what areas of your life do you acknowledge God's existence but refuse to follow His messengers' guidance? What concrete step will you take this week to submit to that teaching?

The Inquirer Must Not Know the Sought Conclusion
Razi states the condition that the inquirer must not know the sought conclusion and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the inquirer must not already know the sought conclusion in theological reasoning, and how an objection about knowing something then inquiring into its proof is resolved.

Core Idea
Inquiry is a request; requesting the already obtained is impossible.

Imam al-Razi establishes a foundational condition for valid theological inquiry: the inquirer must not already know the conclusion he seeks. This may seem counterintuitive, as we often examine evidence for things we think we know. Razi addresses this tension by clarifying what is truly being sought in such cases, setting the stage for a clear methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the main principle
Read the opening statement: the inquirer must not know the sought conclusion because inquiry is a request and requesting what is already obtained is impossible.
Notice the logical reasoning: if you already have something, you cannot seek it.
2
Examine the objection
Consider the potential counterexample: we sometimes know something and then inquire into its evidence. Read Razi's response carefully.
The key is distinguishing between the known thing and the evidence being proven as evidence.
3
Understand the resolution
Razi clarifies that the 'sought' in that case is not the known fact but the proof's status as evidence, which is not yet known.
This preserves the rule that inquiry seeks the unknown.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: الناظر يجب أن لا يكون عالما بالمطلوب، لأن النظر طلب وطلب الحاصل محال. لا يقال: ربما علينا الشيء ثم ننظر في الاستدلال عليه بدليل ثان. لانا نقول: المطلوب هناك ليس المسلول بل كون الثاني دليلا عليه وهو غير معلوم، وأن لا يكون جاهلا جهلا مركبا لأن صاحب هذا الجهل جازم بكونه عالما، وذلك يمنعه من الإقدام على الطلب، ثم امتناع الاجتماع ذاتي أو للصارف فيه خلاف. أقول: أما من قال اجتماع النظر والجهل المركب في واحد بعينه ممتنع لذاته كاجتماع النقيضين أو المدين احتج بأن النظر يجب أن يكون مقارنا للشك والجهل المركب مقارن للجزم واجتماعه ما هو اجتماع القيمين و مناقضة اللوازم قريبة من مناقضة الملزومات، وقال بذلك أبو هاشم، ومن قال عدم اجتماعهما لوجود الصارف كالأكل مع الامتلاء إنما قال بذلك لأنه يجوز وجود النظر مع عدم الشك وإليه ذهب القاضي وهو مذهب الحكماء، قالوا إن كثيراً من الناس يتعلمون من غير أن يسبق شك إلى أذهانهم، وذهب أبو إسحاق الاسفرائيني إلى أن الناظر ممتنع أن يكون شاكا.
Issue: The inquirer must not be knowledgeable of the sought [conclusion], because inquiry is a request and requesting what is already obtained is impossible. It cannot be said: sometimes we know something, then we inquire into the evidence for it by a second proof. For we say: the sought here is not the known, but rather that the second be evidence upon it, and it is not known. And [he] must not be [afflicted with] compound ignorance, because the possessor of this ignorance is certain that he is knowledgeable, and that prevents him from embarking on inquiry. Then whether the prevention of combination [of inquiry and compound ignorance] is essential or due to a deterrent is a matter of disagreement. I say: As for those who say that the combination of inquiry and compound ignorance in one individual is impossible in itself, like the combination of two contradictories or two opposites, they argued that inquiry must be accompanied by doubt, and compound ignorance is accompanied by certainty, and their combination is a combination of two opposites, and the contradiction of necessary consequences is close to the contradiction of the necessary things. Abu Hashim held this. And those who say that their non-combination is due to the existence of a deterrent, like eating with fullness, only said that because they allow the existence of inquiry without doubt. Al-Qadi held this, and it is the doctrine of the philosophers. They said that many people learn without any doubt preceding in their minds. Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held that the inquirer is prevented from being doubtful.
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage is a doctrinal issue (مسألة) concerning the condition of the inquirer (الناظر) in theological reasoning. Imam al-Razi states that the inquirer must not already know the sought conclusion, because inquiry is a request, and requesting what is already obtained is impossible. He then addresses a potential objection: what if one knows something and then inquires into a second proof for it? He responds that in that case, the sought is not the known thing itself but the fact that the second proof is evidence for it, which is not yet known. He adds that the inquirer must also not have compound ignorance, because that would make him certain of his false belief and prevent him from seeking. Then he notes a disagreement: is the impossibility of combining inquiry with compound ignorance essential (like impossibility of opposites) or due to a deterrent? He elaborates the two views: Abu Hashim held it is essential; Al-Qadi and the philosophers held it is due to a deterrent, giving the example of learning without prior doubt; Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini held that the inquirer is necessarily not doubtful.

Key Terms
ناظر
مطلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that inquiring into evidence for something already known violates the condition.
Correction: The condition applies to the specific conclusion being sought; when seeking evidence, the evidential relationship is unknown, so the rule is not violated.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why must the inquirer not know the sought conclusion?
A) Because it leads to pride
B) Because inquiry is a request and requesting the obtained is impossible
C) Because it is a sin to seek what you know
D) Because doubt is required for inquiry

Key Takeaway
The inquirer must seek something unknown; if a conclusion is known, the inquiry shifts to its evidence, which remains unknown.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you approached a question in your faith with a fixed answer already in mind. Did your 'inquiry' become a search for confirmation rather than a genuine request for truth? How might you change that habit starting today?
?
When you recite the Qur'an or listen to a lecture, do you assume you already know what it means? What if the real sought knowledge is the evidence and understanding that links the proof to the truth you claim to hold? Will you commit to asking 'how' and 'why' until the unseen relationship becomes known to you?

Compound Ignorance and the Nature of Its Incompatibility with Inquiry
Razi explains the condition of no compound ignorance and the disagreement on its incompatibility with inquiry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why compound ignorance prevents inquiry and the scholarly disagreement on whether its incompatibility with inquiry is essential or due to a deterrent.

Core Idea
Compound ignorance makes one certain of false knowledge, blocking inquiry.

Razi adds another condition: the inquirer must not have compound ignorance, where a person is mistakenly certain of knowing something. Such a person sees no need to inquire. However, scholars disagree on why inquiry and compound ignorance cannot coexist: is it inherently impossible, like two opposites, or merely prevented by a psychological deterrent? This passage explores that debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand compound ignorance
Read the description of compound ignorance: the person is certain that he knows, even though he is ignorant. This certainty prevents him from seeking knowledge.
2
Identify the two positions
Find the two views: one says the combination is impossible in itself (like opposites), the other says it is impossible due to a deterrent (like eating when full).
Pay attention to the scholars named: Abu Hashim, Al-Qadi, and Abu Ishaq al-Isfarayini.
3
Analyze the reasoning
For the essential impossibility view: inquiry requires doubt, compound ignorance requires certainty, and combining doubt and certainty is contradictory. For the deterrent view: inquiry can occur without doubt, as in learning without prior doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It cannot be said: sometimes we know something, then we inquire into the evidence for it by a second proof. ?
If inquiry requires doubt, and you have no doubt about the truth of your current understanding, does that mean you have stopped seeking? What would it take to reintroduce a healthy doubt that drives deeper inquiry without destroying faith?

The Objection of Circular Dependence in Knowing Allah
Razi presents the objection that knowledge of God requires prior knowledge of the obligator, and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that knowledge of God requires prior knowledge of the obligator and how Ahl al-Sunna resolves it without circular dependence.

Core Idea
Hearing the command itself triggers investigation, breaking the dependency on prior knowledge of the obligator.

This passage addresses a second objection: if knowledge of obligation depends on knowledge of the obligator, how can we be commanded to know Allah before we know Him? Imam al-Razi distinguishes between two schools. The Mu'tazila are not affected because they ground obligation in reason. For Ahl al-Sunna, hearing the command itself initiates the process, and investigation yields scriptural knowledge of the obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the structure: first the objection, then Razi's response.
Pay attention to the key terms: معرفة الإيجاب and معرفة الموجب.
2
Identify the core objection
The objector claims that knowledge of obligation (معرفة الإيجاب) depends on knowledge of the obligator (معرفة الموجب). So commanding knowledge of God is impossible because it would require prior knowledge of God, creating a circular dependency.
3
Examine Razi's answer for each school
Razi gives two separate answers: one for the Mu'tazila (who base obligation on reason, not command) and one for Ahl al-Sunna (who base obligation on the command itself, which prompts investigation and leads to scriptural knowledge).
Notice that Ahl al-Sunna break the circle by saying hearing the command suffices to establish the possibility of obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا إمكان الأمر بالعلم لكن لا تسلم إمكان الأمر بمعرفة الله تعالى لأن معرفة الإيجاب تتوقف على معرفة الموجب فقبل معرفة الموجب لا يمكن معرفة الإيجاب فيكون الأمر بمعرفة الموجب تكليفا بالمحال. أقول: أما المعتزلة فلا يقولون بوجوب المعرفة من جهة الأمر بل من جهة العقل فلا يرد عليهم هذا الإشكال، وأما أهل السنة فيقولون استماع الأمر بالوجوب وإمكانه يوجبان في المستمع التفحص منه، وإذا تفحص حصل العلم السمعى بالوجوب، وهذا هو المراد من قولهم: وجوب المعرفة سمعى، وإمكان معرفة الإيجاب لا يتوقف على معرفة الموجب ويكفى بالاستماع في تحقيق الإيجاب، ولا يلزم منه تكليفه بالمحال.
We accept the possibility of commanding knowledge, but we do not accept the possibility of commanding knowledge of God Most High, because knowledge of obligation depends on knowledge of the obligator; so before knowing the obligator, knowledge of obligation is impossible, so commanding knowledge of the obligator is an impossible imposition. I say: As for the Mu'tazila, they do not say that knowledge is obligatory by way of command, but by way of reason, so this problem does not apply to them. As for Ahl al-Sunna, they say that hearing the command of obligation and its possibility obligates the hearer to examine it, and when he examines, he acquires scriptural knowledge of obligation. This is what is meant by their statement: 'The obligation of knowledge is scriptural.' And the possibility of knowing the obligation does not depend on knowing the obligator; it suffices to hear in order to realize the obligation, and it does not entail imposing the impossible on him.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a second objection to the possibility of commanding knowledge of God. The objector accepts that commanding knowledge is possible but argues that commanding knowledge of God specifically is impossible because knowledge of obligation requires knowledge of the obligator, leading to a circular dependence. Al-Razi's answer (أقول) distinguishes between the Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna: the Mu'tazila base obligation on reason, not command, so the objection does not affect them. For Ahl al-Sunna, hearing the command itself obligates investigation, and through investigation one gains knowledge of the obligation from scripture; the possibility of knowing the obligation does not require prior knowledge of the obligator, thus no impossibility.

Key Terms
معرفة الإيجاب
معرفة الموجب
سمعي
تكليف بالمحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obligation to know God requires prior knowledge of God.
Correction: The command itself obligates investigation, which yields knowledge of the obligation; it does not require prior knowledge of the obligator.

Checkpoint
How does Ahl al-Sunna answer the objection that commanding knowledge of God is impossible?
A) They deny that knowledge of God is obligatory
B) They say hearing the command obligates investigation, which leads to knowledge
C) They argue that the obligator can be known by intuition
D) They concede and rely on reason

Key Takeaway
The objection that knowledge of God is impossible because it requires prior knowledge of Him is resolved by Ahl al-Sunna by noting that the command itself triggers investigation, breaking the circular dependency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever delayed seeking knowledge of Allah because you felt you didn't know enough about Him to begin? How does this lesson's point that the command itself triggers investigation challenge that hesitation? What will you change today?
?
Do you rely more on rational arguments for God's existence or on responding to the divine call found in revelation? Which do you think has shaped your certainty more, and why?
?
When you hear a command from Allah, do you immediately examine it, or do you wait until you feel ‘ready’? What stops you from responding right now to what you know He has commanded?

Third Objection: No Scriptural Command for Rational Inquiry
Razi presents the third objection and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection against the necessity of rational inquiry and how Razi resolves it by distinguishing between Mu'tazila and Ahl al-Sunna, and by clarifying the scriptural command and its obligatory nature.

Core Idea
The absence of a specific command does not negate the duty of rational inquiry.

This passage presents a third objection against the necessity of using rational proofs for faith: that no scriptural command exists for specific proofs. Razi responds by distinguishing between the Mu'tazila, who do not require a scriptural command, and Ahl al-Sunna, who affirm such commands from the Quran. He also notes the disagreement among them regarding whether verifying proofs is a communal or individual obligation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection argues that the Prophet did not command anyone to use specific rational proofs, only to have correct belief, whether by imitation or knowledge. Consider the force of this objection.
This objection challenges the necessity of rational inquiry.
2
Razi's initial response
Razi notes that the Mu'tazila do not rely on scriptural commands for their method, so the objection does not apply to them. This shows that the objection only concerns those who require scriptural backing.
3
Scriptural evidence for Ahl al-Sunna
For Ahl al-Sunna, there are clear Quranic commands like 'Say: Observe' (Quran 10:101). This shows that rational inquiry is indeed commanded in scripture.
The verse is a command to observe and reflect.
4
The scope of obligation
The only remaining disagreement among Ahl al-Sunna is whether verifying proofs is a communal obligation (فرض كفاية) or an individual obligation (فرض عين). Think about the implications of each.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمناه لكن لا نسلم ورود الأمر به بل الأمر إنما جاء بالاعتقاد المطابق تقليدا كان أو علما، لأنه عليه الصلاة والسلام ما كلف واحدا بهذه الأدلة. أقول: أما المعتزلة فلا يحتاجون إلى ورود الأمر، وأما أهل السنة فيقولون بورود الأمر والتكليف به كما في قوله تعالى: {قل انظروا} وفي أمثاله ظاهر متفق عليه، إنما الخلاف في أن تحقيق الأدلة فرض على الكفاية أو على الأعيان.
We accept that, but we do not accept that a command for it exists; rather the command is only for correct belief, whether it be imitation or knowledge, because he (peace and blessings be upon him) did not charge anyone with these proofs. I say: As for the Mu'tazila, they do not need the existence of a command. As for Ahl al-Sunna, they affirm the existence of the command and the duty of it, as in His statement 'Say: Observe' and its like, which is clear and agreed upon. The only disagreement is whether verifying the proofs is a communal obligation or an individual obligation.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a third objection: even if commanding knowledge were possible, no scriptural command exists for it; the Prophet only required correct belief whether through imitation or knowledge, not particular proofs. Al-Razi's answer (أقول) notes that the Mu'tazila do not rely on scriptural command, so the objection does not apply to them. For Ahl al-Sunna, there are clear Quranic commands like 'Say: Observe,' and the only disagreement is whether verifying proofs is a communal or individual duty.

Misconception: The Quran does not command rational inquiry.
Correction: The Quran commands observation and reflection, e.g., 'Say: Observe' (Quran 10:101).

Why Probability is Insufficient for Knowledge of God
Razi presents the objection that probable belief suffices, and answers that certainty is required.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's argument that probable belief is insufficient for the knowledge of God and why certainty is required.

Core Idea
Probability can vanish; certainty is necessary to avoid spiritual danger.

The objector concedes that imitation (taqlid) is insufficient, but challenges the need for certainty, arguing that probable belief (zann) might suffice. Al-Razi responds by highlighting the risk of relying on probability: it can vanish, and its loss entails great danger. Since there is an explicit command to avoid danger, certainty is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector concedes that imitation is insufficient but questions why probability is insufficient, citing weak evidence and non-certainty of verbal proofs.
Notice the objector's arguments: the verse 'Know' is weak, the address is specific, and verbal proofs are not certain.
2
Identify the objector's points
List the three reasons the objector gives for why probability might be sufficient. This sets up the need for al-Razi's response.
3
Read al-Razi's answer
Read the second part (starting with 'I say') where al-Razi responds. Note the key points: probability is liable to vanish, vanishing brings great danger, and there is an explicit command to avoid danger.
Also note the additional argument: legal obligations are established by probable proofs, but those proofs create the probability that certainty is required.
4
Understand the danger argument
Reflect on why the possibility of probability vanishing is dangerous. Consider the consequences if one's belief in God could be lost – this would endanger one's faith and salvation.
This argument connects to the broader Islamic emphasis on certainty (yaqin) in matters of faith.
5
Summarize the conclusion
Summarize in your own words why al-Razi concludes that certainty is required. The key is the command to avoid danger and the nature of legal proofs pointing to the necessity of certainty.
This reinforces the lesson's objective: probability is insufficient because it is unreliable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن التقليد غير كاف، لكن لم قلتم أن الظن غير كاف، والاعتماد على قوله تعالى: {فاعلم} ضعيف لأن [؟] ولأن الخطاب خاص، ولأن الأدلة اللفظية غير يقينية على ما سيأتي إن شاء الله، الظن الغالب قد يسمى [؟] فلا يمكن بناء المطالب اليقينية عليها. أقول: الظن ممكن الزوال وفي زواله خطر عظيم، وقد ورد النص الصريح بالأمر بالاحتراز من الخطر فيتعين الأمر بتحصيل اليقين قطعا، وأيضاً الوجوب الشرعي يثبت بالأدلة الظنية وهذه الأدلة توقع الظن بوجوب المعرفة على الوجه اليقيني.
We accept that imitation is insufficient, but why do you say that probability is insufficient? Reliance on His statement 'Know' is weak because [؟] and because the address is specific, and because verbal proofs are not certain, as will come, God willing. Overwhelming probability may be called [؟] so it is impossible to build certain demands upon them. I say: Probability is liable to vanish and in its vanishing is a great danger. The explicit text has come commanding avoidance of danger, so the command to acquire certainty is definitely determined. Also, the legal obligation is established by probable proofs, and these proofs create the probability that knowledge is obligatory in a certain manner.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage is a fourth objection: even if imitation is insufficient, why is probability (ظن) insufficient? The objector argues that reliance on the verse 'Know' is weak because verbal proofs are not certain. Al-Razi's answer (أقول) explains that probability can vanish, and that vanishing entails great danger; since there is an explicit command to avoid danger, one must acquire certainty. Furthermore, the legal obligation itself is established by probable proofs, and those proofs produce the probability that knowledge is obligatory in a certain manner, thus requiring certainty.

يقين
احتراز من الخطر

Misconception: Probable belief (zann) is sufficient for religious duties because the Quran says 'Know' which can be interpreted as probable knowledge.
Correction: Al-Razi argues that probability is liable to vanish, and its loss entails great danger; since there is a command to avoid danger, certainty is necessary.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi concludes that the risk of relying on vanishing probability makes certainty obligatory for knowledge of God.

Objection: Alternative Paths to Knowledge Beyond Rational Inference
Razi transmits an objection listing three alternative paths to knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative sources of knowledge proposed by some traditions and their challenge to the exclusivity of rational inference (al-naẓar) in theology.

Core Idea
Knowledge may come from infallible authority, inspiration, or inner purification — not just reason.

In this passage, Imam Razi presents an objection raised by those who question the necessity of rational inference for attaining knowledge of God. The objector concedes the prior argument but demands proof that reason is the only path. Then, as a gracious offering, they list three alternative sources: the statement of an infallible Imam, direct inspiration, and inner purification as taught by the Sufis. This objection forces us to consider the epistemological foundations of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a concession, then a request for proof, then a list of alternatives.
The objector says 'سلمنا' (we concede), indicating they accept the preceding argument but want further evidence.
2
Identify the three alternatives
The passage lists three alternative paths to knowledge: (1) the statement of the infallible Imam, (2) inspiration (al-ilhām), and (3) purification of the inner self. Think about what each might mean in context.
These correspond to Shīʿī, philosophical, and Sufi sources of knowledge.
3
Reflect on the challenge
Consider why this objection is significant. If these alternatives are valid, then the necessity of rational inference (al-naẓar) for theology is undermined. How might a kalam theologian respond?
This sets up the next part of the text where Razi will answer this objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا ولكن ما الدليل على أنه لا طريق إلى المعرفة سوى النظر ، ثم إنا على سبيل التبرع نذكر طرقا أخر وهى قول الإمام المعصوم أو الإلهام أو تصفية الباطن كما يقوله أهل التصوف
We concede, but what is the proof that there is no path to knowledge (al-maʿrifa) other than rational inference (al-naẓar)? Then we, by way of gratuitous offering, mention other paths: the statement of the infallible Imam (qawl al-imām al-maʿṣūm), or inspiration (al-ilhām), or purification of the inner self (taṣfiyat al-bāṭin) as the people of Sufism (ahl al-taṣawwuf) say.
Source pages p. 47

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the exclusivity of rational inference (al-naẓar) as the path to knowledge of God. The objector concedes the previous point but asks for proof that no other path exists. Then, as a gift, they list three alternatives: (1) the statement of an infallible Imam, (2) inspiration (al-ilhām), and (3) purification of the inner self (taṣfiyat al-bāṭin) as advocated by the Sufis. This objection challenges the necessity of rational theology by offering alternative epistemological sources.

The Obligation of Theoretical Reasoning is Based on Revelation
Razi states that the obligation of nazar is sam'i, not rational.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the obligation of theoretical reasoning (nazar) is considered samʿī (based on revelation) in classical Ashʿarī theology, contrary to the Muʿtazilī position.

Core Idea
The obligation to investigate God is known only through revelation, not reason alone.

In this passage, Razi addresses objections about the rational basis for the obligation to engage in theoretical reasoning. He argues that while rational answers are possible, the preferred method is to rely on the apparent meanings of revealed texts. He then declares the Ashʿarī doctrine that the obligation of nazar is samʿī—based on revelation—and provides Quranic and rational evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice that Razi first responds to objections, then states his doctrine, then gives evidence.
The passage is compact; each sentence carries significant weight.
2
Identify Razi's answer to the objections
Razi says that although a detailed answer is possible, the preferred course is to refer to the apparent meanings of revealed texts. Observe the Quranic quote 'Say: Observe'.
This shows a methodological preference for textual reliance over speculative reasoning.
3
Understand the doctrine of samʿī obligation
Razi explicitly states that the obligation of nazar is samʿī (based on revelation), contrary to the Muʿtazila and some jurists. Reflect on why this is a significant theological claim.
4
Analyze the evidence
Razi gives two pieces of evidence: the Quranic verse 'We would not punish until We send a messenger' and a rational argument about the impossibility of knowing reward/punishment through reason alone. Connect them to the main claim.
The rational argument relies on the Ashʿarī premise that nothing is inherently good or evil from God's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الجواب عن هذه الأسئلة وإن كان ممكنا لكن الأولى التحويل على ظواهر النصوص كقوله تعالى قل انظروا. مسألة: وجوب النظر سمعي خلافا للمعتزلة وبعض الفقهاء من الشافعية والحنفية. لنا قوله تعالى: وما كنا معذبين حتى نبعث رسولا، ولأن فائدة الوجوب الثواب والعقاب، ولا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء من أفعاله، فلا يمكن القطع بالثواب والعقاب من جهة العقل، فلا يمكن القطع بالوجوب.
The answer to these questions, though possible, nevertheless the preferred course is to refer to the apparent meanings of the texts (ẓawāhir al-nuṣūṣ), like His statement the Exalted: 'Say: Observe' (Q. 6:11). Issue: The obligation of theoretical reasoning (wujūb al-naẓar) is based on revelation (samʿī), contrary to the Muʿtazila and some jurists among the Shāfiʿīs and Ḥanafīs. Our evidence is His statement the Exalted: 'We would not punish until We send a messenger' (Q. 17:15), and because the benefit of obligation is reward and punishment, and nothing is deemed ugly from God the Exalted, so it is impossible to be certain of reward and punishment through reason alone, thus it is impossible to be certain of the obligation.
Source pages p. 48

Commentary
This passage provides the final answer to the preceding objections and states the positive doctrine. The author says that although a detailed answer to the objections is possible, the preferred method is to rely on the apparent meanings of the revealed texts (ẓawāhir al-nuṣūṣ), citing the Quranic verse 'Say: Observe' (Q. 6:11) as a command for rational consideration. Then he declares the doctrine: the obligation of theoretical reasoning (wujūb al-naẓar) is based on revelation (samʿī), not reason. This is contrary to the Muʿtazila and some Shāfiʿī and Ḥanafī jurists who held the obligation to be rational. The evidence is the Quranic verse 'We would not punish until We send a messenger' (Q. 17:15), which implies that punishment is contingent on revelation, not on reason alone. Additionally, the purpose of obligation is reward and punishment, but since nothing is inherently ugly or beneficial from God's actions (as He is not subject to rational norms of good and evil), reason cannot independently determine the reward or punishment that follows from obligation. Therefore, reason cannot establish the obligation itself; it must rely on revelation.

Key Terms
wujūb al-naẓar
samʿī
ẓawāhir al-nuṣūṣ
Muʿtazila

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The obligation to investigate God is rationally self-evident and can be known by reason alone.
Correction: Razi argues that the obligation is only known through revelation (samʿī), as reason cannot determine reward/punishment without revelation.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the basis for the obligation of theoretical reasoning (nazar)?
A) Rational necessity.
B) The consensus of scholars.
C) Revelation (samʿī).
D) The principle of causality.

Key Takeaway
The obligation to engage in theoretical reasoning about God is not a rational necessity but is established through revelation, highlighting the Ashʿarī emphasis on the primacy of divine communication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to know Allah, do you rely more on your own reasoning or on the revelation He sent? What does this lesson reveal about the limits of your intellect and the necessity of receiving guidance from Allah? Could your faith be built on something other than His word?
?

What will you do today to submit to the revelation rather than your own reasoning?
?
You believe that studying theology is obligatory—but is that belief based on what Allah commanded or on what you think is rational? If the obligation is only known through revelation, have you sought that revelation with the seriousness it demands? Are you willing to abandon the comfort of rational self-sufficiency and rely wholly on Allah's guidance?
