The Objection to Scriptural Obligation of Reflection and Its Answer
An objection and answer concerning the obligation of reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that if the obligation to reflect is known only through scripture, one could avoid reflection, and how the answer resolves this by showing that the objection also applies to rational obligation and that possibility of knowledge suffices.

Core Idea
Reflection's obligation does not require knowing it's obligatory; possibility is enough.

This passage tackles a critical objection in Islamic theology: if we only know we must reflect on God's existence through scripture, and scripture's truth is known by reflection, then a skeptical person could simply refuse to reflect and escape all obligation. The author presents this objection and then gives a two-part answer. First, he shows that the same problem applies to those who say reflection is rationally obligatory. Second, he explains that obligation does not depend on actual knowledge of the obligation, but on the possibility of knowing it.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection in Arabic and translation to understand the skeptical argument: if obligation depends on scripture, and scripture on reflection, one can avoid both by not reflecting.
Note the phrase 'لا أنظر حتى لا أعرف كون السمع صدقا' — 'I do not reflect so that I do not know that scripture is truthful.'
2
Analyze the first part of the answer
The author first turns the tables: the Mu'tazila also face the same problem because their obligation to reflect is theoretical, not intuitive. So the objection is not unique to the scriptural view.
This is a tuqshif (counter-objection) showing inconsistency.
3
Understand the second part of the answer
Even if the objection applied, the obligation does not depend on actual knowledge of the obligation. Rather, the possibility of knowing it (imkan al-'ilm) is sufficient. This avoids circularity.
The key phrase: 'بل يكفي فيه إمكان العلم بالوجوب'.
4
Consider the footnote's insights
The commentary discusses that some Sunni scholars made knowledge of God rationally obligatory, and clarifies that the answer's reliance on 'possibility' is sound only if it avoids the same regress.
Notice the difference between Mu'tazili intellectual obligation and scriptural obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجوا : بأنه لو لم يثبت الوجوب إلا بالسمع الذى لا يعلم صحته إلا بالنظر فللمخاطب أن يقول : إنى لا أنظر حتى لا أعرف كون السمع صدقا وذلك مفض إلى إبطال الأنبياء
والجواب : أن هذا لازم عليكم أيضاً لأن وجوب النظر وإن كان عندكم عقليا لكنه غير معلوم بضرورة العقل ، لا أن العلم بوجوب النظر يتوقف عند المعتزلة على العلم بوجوب معرفة الله تعالى ، وأن النظر طريق إليها ولا طريق إليها سواه ، وأن ما لا يتم الواجب إلا به فهو واجب ، وكل واحد من هذه المقدمات نظرى والموقوف على النظرى نظرى، فكان العلم بوجوب النظر عندهم نظريا، فللمخاطب أن يقول لا أنظر حتى لا أعرف وجوب النظر ، ثم الجواب أن الوجوب لا يتوقف على العلم بالوجوب وإلا لزم الدور بل يكفي فيه إمكان العلم بالوجوب ، والإمكان هنا حاصل في الجملة (1)
(1) أقول : حكى عن القفال الشاشى من أصحاب الشافعي ، وعن بعض الفقهاء الحنفية مع كونهم من أهل السنة أنهم قالوا بوجوب المعرفة عقلا ، والقول بأن المعرفة واجبة هى إحدى مقدمى المسألة المتقدمة وقوله فائدة الوجوب الثواب والعقاب فيه نظر ، لأن أهل السنة لا يوجبون الثواب على الطاعة ، والمعتزلة يقولون بأن القول بوجوب الثواب على الطاعة سمعى ، والوجوب العقلى يثبت باستحقاق تاركه الذم عقلا فهنا الاستدلال ساقط ، وأما قوله في معارضة المعتزلة فغير متوجه عليهم ، لأن وجوب النظر عندهم ليس بمتوقف على العلم بالوجوب، بل قالوا دفع الضرر المظنون الذى لعله يلحق بسبب الجهل بالمنعم واجب في البديهة العقلية وذلك لا يمكن إلا بمعرفته وذلك لا يزول بترك النظر بل إنما يزول بالنظر ، وأما الوجوب السمعى فلو كفى فيه إمكان العلم بالوجوب للزم أن يجب على المكلفين مالا يعلمونه أصلا ، لأن إمكان العلم بصدق الواجبات غير الذي يعلمونه حاصل، والصواب أن يقال : إمكان العلم بصدق الأوامر السمعية يقتضى وجوب النظر فيها .
They argued: Because if obligation is only established through scripture (al-sam') whose correctness is not known except through reflection (al-nazar), then the addressable person can say: I do not reflect, so that I do not know that scripture is truthful, and this leads to nullifying the prophets.
The answer: That this is also necessary upon you, because the obligation of reflection, even if it is intellectual according to you, is not known by the necessity of intellect. Rather, the knowledge of the obligation of reflection according to the Mu'tazila depends on the knowledge of the obligation of knowing God, and that reflection is the way to it and no way to it other than it, and that what the obligatory is not completed except by it is obligatory, and each of these premises is theoretical (nazari), and what depends on the theoretical is theoretical. Thus, the knowledge of the obligation of reflection according to them is theoretical. So the addressable person can say: I do not reflect, so that I do not know the obligation of reflection. Then the answer: The obligation does not depend on the knowledge of the obligation, otherwise circularity would occur, rather the possibility of knowledge of the obligation is sufficient for it, and possibility here is available in general. (1)
(1) I say: It is related from al-Qaffal al-Shashi from the followers of al-Shafi'i, and from some Hanafi jurists despite their being from Ahl al-Sunna, that they said the obligation of knowledge is intellectual. The statement that knowledge is obligatory is one of the premises of the previous issue. His statement: 'The benefit of obligation is reward and punishment' is questionable, because Ahl al-Sunna do not make reward obligatory for obedience, and the Mu'tazila say that the statement of the obligation of reward for obedience is scriptural, and intellectual obligation is established by the rational deserving of blame for its abandoner. So here the argument is invalid. As for his statement in opposing the Mu'tazila, it is not directed at them, because the obligation of reflection according to them does not depend on knowledge of the obligation. Rather, they said: Repelling the presumed harm that might befall due to ignorance of the Benefactor is obligatory by intellectual intuition, and that cannot be except by knowing Him, and that does not cease by abandoning reflection but only ceases by reflection. As for scriptural obligation, if the possibility of knowledge of obligation were sufficient for it, then it would necessarily be obligatory upon the responsible what they do not know at all, because the possibility of knowing the truthfulness of the obligations other than what they know is present. The correct opinion is to say: The possibility of knowing the truthfulness of scriptural commands requires reflection upon them.
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the obligation of reflection (al-nazar). The objection argues that if the obligation to reflect is only known through scripture, and scripture's truthfulness itself requires reflection to be known, then a person could avoid reflection entirely and thus never be obligated. The answer first points out that the objector's argument also applies to the Mu'tazila, since their own obligation to reflect is known theoretically, not necessarily. It then explains that obligation does not require actual knowledge of the obligation; rather, the possibility of such knowledge suffices. The commentary that follows expands on this, noting that some Hanafi and Shafi'i scholars held that knowledge of God is intellectually obligatory, and discusses the Mu'tazili position that the obligation to reflect is based on repelling presumed harm from ignorance of the Benefactor, which is an intuitive obligation. The commentary concludes that the correct view is that the possibility of knowing scripture's truthfulness necessitates reflection upon it.

Key Terms
النظر
الوجوب
السمع
العلم
الإمكان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the obligation of reflection requires actual knowledge of its obligation.
Correction: The answer clarifies that the possibility of knowing the obligation is sufficient; actual knowledge is not required.

Checkpoint
According to the answer given in this passage, what is sufficient for the obligation of reflection (nazar) to be binding?
A) Actual knowledge of the obligation
B) The possibility of knowledge of the obligation
C) The scripture explicitly commanding it
D) The intuitive fear of harm

Key Takeaway
The obligation to reflect on God does not require knowing that it is obligatory; it is enough that one can come to know its obligation through reflection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you actively reflecting on the signs of God, or are you avoiding reflection because you think you need to know it's obligatory first? What is stopping you from reflecting today? Commit to a specific time to reflect.
?
The passage says possibility of knowledge suffices for obligation. Is there any area in your life where you have the possibility to seek truth but you are not doing so because you feel you lack certainty? What truth are you avoiding?
?
If the obligation to reflect does not require prior knowledge, what excuse do you have for not reflecting on the purpose of your existence? Write down one concrete step you will take to start reflecting on God's signs today.

Refutation of the Ash'ari Use of Qiyas in Kalam Arguments
Al-Razi critiques the Ash'ari application of qiyas in theological reasoning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Razi critiques the Ash'ari use of qiyas in theological reasoning, particularly showing that the analogy between reflection and recollection fails due to the role of intent.

Core Idea
Qiyas yields only probability, and a flawed analogy on intent undermines the Ash'ari argument.

Al-Razi challenges the Ash'ari method of using qiyas in kalam by first explaining its fiqh-based structure and epistemic limitation, then dismantling a specific Ash'ari analogy that compares reflection (nazar) to recollection (tadhakkur). This passage shows that a crucial difference in the necessity of intent makes the analogy invalid, thus refuting the Ash'ari's use of qiyas as a source of certainty in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify its two parts
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice that the first part describes the standard qiyas used in fiqh and the general objection that it yields only probability. The second part, introduced by 'فقال المصنف', presents Al-Razi's specific refutation of the Ash'ari's analogy between reflection and recollection.
The passage is a continuous argument, but these two sections have different functions.
2
Understand the definition and limitation of qiyas
The passage defines qiyas as attaching a branch (far') to an original (asl) due to a shared attribute (jami') that is the cause ('illa) of the ruling. It then states that seekers of certainty do not rely on qiyas because it only produces probable knowledge (zann), not certainty (yaqin).
This sets up the general framework for the critique.
3
Examine the specific Ash'ari qiyas on nazar and tadhakkur
Al-Razi presents the Ash'ari's argument: reflection does not generate knowledge, analogous to recollection. The Mu'tazila accept that recollection does not generate knowledge, but they provide a reason: recollection can occur without intent, whereas reflection requires intent. This distinction (farq) invalidates the analogy.
Focus on the cause ('illa) mentioned: the presence or absence of intent.
4
Conclude the refutation
Al-Razi concludes that if the Mu'tazila's reason (the difference in intent) is sound, then the Ash'ari's qiyas fails; if it is not sound, then the original ruling (that recollection does not generate) would be denied. Either way, the qiyas does not yield certainty.
This is a classic refutation by showing a relevant difference that breaks the analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن الاشعرية ردوا قول المعتزلة باستعمال القياس ، فإن القدماء من المتكلمين كانوا يستعملون القياس في علم الأصول الذى يستعمل في الفقه وهو الحاق فرع الأصل في حكم بسبب جامع لهما يدعون أنه السبب في الحكم بالأصل وهو موجود فى الفرع ، فيجب أن يكون مسببه وهو الحكم موجوداً أيضاً في الفرع . وطلاب اليقين لا يعتمدون عليه بل يقولون : هذا القياس على تقدير صحته لا يفيد اليقين بل يوقع ظنا فقط وعلى تقدير المساعدة في استعماله ربما يفرقون بين الأصل والفرع بما يمنع كون الجامع مقتضيا للحكم فى الفرع وإن كان مقتضيا له في الأصل . فقال المصنف : قياس الأشعرى النظرى فى قوله إن النظر لا يولد العلم على التذكر فإن المعتزلى يوافقه في أن التذكر لا يولد العلم الذى يعود بالتذكر لا يفيد اليقين لكونه قياسا غير مفيد لليقين ولا الزام على تقدير المساعدة في استعماله القياس فى المطالب العقلية ، لأن المعتزلة لم يقولوا بالتولد في التذكر، لعلة توجد في التذكر ولا توجد في النظر وتلك العلة أن التذكر ربما يحصل من غير قصد المتذكر ، والنظر لا يعمل من غير قصد الناظر ، فإن صحت تلك العلة ظهر الفرق فسقط الاستدلال بهذا القياس ، وإلا منع
Then the Ash'ariyya refuted the Mu'tazila's statement by using qiyas. For the early theologians used qiyas in the science of usul that is used in fiqh, which is attaching a branch to an original in a ruling due to a common attribute that they claim is the cause of the ruling in the original and is present in the branch, so its effect, which is the ruling, must also be present in the branch. But seekers of certainty do not rely on it; rather they say: this qiyas, assuming its validity, does not yield certainty but only probability, and assuming its use, perhaps they distinguish between the original and the branch by something that prevents the common attribute from being effective in the branch even if it is effective in the original. So the author said: The Ash'ari's theoretical qiyas in his statement that reflection does not generate knowledge, analogized to recollection, does not provide certainty because it is a qiyas that is not useful for certainty, nor does it bind if they allow its use in intellectual matters, because the Mu'tazila did not say that generation occurs in recollection, due to a cause that exists in recollection and does not exist in reflection, and that cause is that recollection may occur without the intent of the rememberer, while reflection does not occur without the intent of the reflector. If that cause is sound, the difference appears and the argument by this qiyas falls; otherwise they would prevent [the ruling in the original].
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage critiques the Ash'ariyya's use of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in theological arguments. It first describes the standard qiyas used in fiqh: applying a ruling from an original case to a branch due to a shared attribute claimed to be the cause. Seekers of certainty reject it because it yields only probability, not certainty. The author then examines the Ash'ari's specific qiyas: reflection does not generate knowledge, using the analogy of recollection (tadhakkur). The Mu'tazila accept that recollection does not generate knowledge, but they give a reason: recollection can occur without intent, while reflection requires intent. Therefore, the analogy fails due to a relevant difference. If the Mu'tazila's reason is sound, the qiyas is invalid; otherwise, the original claim (that recollection does not generate) would be denied.

قصد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That qiyas is completely rejected in kalam.
Correction: The author does not reject qiyas outright; he critiques its use for producing certainty and shows a specific instance where the analogy fails, but qiyas may still be used for probable knowledge.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the Ash'ari's qiyas on recollection does not yield certainty?
A) Because analogy is never valid in kalam.
B) Because there is a difference: recollection can be unintentional, but reflection requires intent.
C) Because the Mu'tazila reject qiyas entirely.
D) Because the common attribute is not present in the original.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi shows that the Ash'ari's analogy between reflection and recollection fails because recollection can occur without intent, while reflection requires intent, thus invalidating the qiyas and undermining the argument for using qiyas as a source of certainty in theology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever built an argument about your faith on an analogy that seemed right on the surface, but when you examined the underlying reasons, you realized they didn't really match? What analogy was it, and what did you discover?
?
Al-Razi shows that seeking certainty means rejecting even well-formed analogies if their causes are not identical. Is there an area of your belief where you have settled for 'likely true' when you should be demanding 'certain'? What would it cost you to pursue certainty there?
?
The Mu'tazila's distinction between recollection and reflection hinges on the role of intent. When you 'think' about your beliefs, is it a deliberate, intentional reflection, or a passive recollection of what you've been taught? Which one actually strengthens certainty?

Ten Conditions for Certainty in Scriptural Proofs
Razi enumerates ten conditions for linguistic evidence to yield certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten conditions that Imam Razi argues must be satisfied for linguistic evidence to produce certainty, and why each condition introduces a potential doubt.

Core Idea
Certainty from scripture requires verifying ten aspects of transmission and meaning.

Imam Razi challenges the common assumption that scriptural texts always yield certainty. In this passage, he lays out ten conditions that must be checked before one can be confident in a linguistic proof. This lesson unpacks each condition and shows why they are necessary.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of conditions
Carefully read the Arabic passage and identify the ten matters Razi mentions. Note that the list begins with عصمة رواة and ends with عدم المعارض العقلي.
The commentary adds that some copies include an extra condition about abrogation.
2
Reflect on each condition
Consider how each condition (e.g., integrity of transmitters, absence of homonymy) introduces a possible obstacle to certainty. For example, if a word has multiple meanings, we cannot be sure which is intended without further context.
3
Connect to broader methodology
Understand that this list reflects a rigorous epistemological approach in kalam and usul al-fiqh, emphasizing that textual evidence is not automatically certain but requires verification.
4
Compare with alternative views
Many jurists hold that linguistic evidence can yield certainty without all conditions. Razi's position is more skeptical. Think about the implications of requiring such conditions for everyday interpretation.
This will be explored further in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الدليل اللفظ لا يفيد اليقين إلا عند تيقن أمور عشرة ) عصمة رواة مفردات تلك الألفاظ وإعرابها وتصريفها وعدم الاشتراك والمجاز والنقل والتخصيص بالأشخاص والأزمنة وعدم الإضمار والتأخير والتقديم والنسخ وعدم المعارض العقلي الذي لو كان لرجح عليه، إذ ترجيح النقل على العقل يقتضى القدح في العقل المستلزم للقدح في النقل لافتقاره إليه وإذا كان المنتج ظنيا فما ظنك بالنتيجة . أقول: كثير من الفقهاء يقولون : الدليل اللفظي يفيد العلم ، وذهب المصنف إلى أنه لا يفيده بعد هذه الأمور ، ويزاد في بعض النسخ وعدم النسخ ومحكمات القرآن لا يقع فيها شك بسبب رواة الألفاظ وتصريفها وإعرابها والاشتراك والنسخ والتقديم والتأخير وبسبب المعارض العقلي فإن وقع فيها شك بسبب المجاز أو التخصيص أو الإضمار فممكن .
A question: The linguistic evidence does not yield certainty except when one is certain of ten matters: integrity of the transmitters of the individual words, their syntax and morphology, absence of homonymy (ishtirāk), figurative usage (majāz), transference (naql), specification by persons and times, absence of ellipsis (iḍmār), delay and advance (taqdīm wa ta’khīr), abrogation (naskh), and absence of a rational objection (mu‘āriḍ ‘aqlī) that, if there were one, would outweigh it. For giving preference to transmitted over rational necessitates disparagement of reason, which entails disparagement of transmission due to its need for reason. And if the producer (al-muntij) is probable, what do you think of the result? I say: Many jurists say that linguistic evidence yields certainty, but the author (al-muṣannif) held that it does not yield it after these matters. And in some copies, it is added: and absence of abrogation. And the decisive verses (muḥkamāt) of the Quran are not subject to doubt due to transmitters of words, morphology, syntax, homonymy, abrogation, advance and delay, and due to rational objection. If doubt arises due to figurative usage, specification, or ellipsis, it is possible.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a مسألة about the epistemic status of linguistic evidence (الدليل اللفظي) in theology and law. The author, Imam Razi, asserts that linguistic evidence does not yield certainty (اليقين) unless ten conditions are met. He lists these conditions: 1) integrity of the transmitters of the individual words, 2) certainty of syntax, 3) certainty of morphology, 4) absence of homonymy (ishtirāk), 5) absence of figurative usage (majāz), 6) absence of transference (naql), 7) absence of specification by persons and times (takhṣīṣ), 8) absence of ellipsis (iḍmār), 9) certainty about advance and delay (taqdīm wa ta’khīr), and 10) absence of abrogation (naskh). Additionally, he mentions the absence of a rational objection that would outweigh the transmission, because favoring transmitted over rational undermines reason, and reason is foundational for transmission. He concludes that if even the producer (the evidence) is probable, the result (the conclusion) is even more probable. The commentary that follows notes that many jurists hold that linguistic evidence can yield certainty, but the author (al-muṣannif) denies it until these conditions are verified. Some copies add a condition about absence of abrogation. The commentary also notes that the decisive verses of the Quran are free from doubt regarding word transmission, morphology, etc., but may be subject to doubt from figurative usage, specification, or ellipsis. This lesson highlights the rigorous conditions required for certainty from scriptural proofs.

Key Terms
دليل لفظي
يقين
عصمة رواة
اشتراك
مجاز
نقل
تخصيص
إضمار
تقديم وتأخير
نسخ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Linguistic evidence always yields certainty without any conditions.
Correction: Imam Razi shows that many factors can introduce doubt, and certainty requires verifying all ten conditions.

Checkpoint
How many conditions does Razi require for linguistic evidence to yield certainty?
A) Five
B) Seven
C) Ten
D) Twelve

Key Takeaway
Scriptural texts require rigorous verification of ten conditions before they can yield certainty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Quran or a hadith, do you ever pause to consider whether your understanding might be based on a word with multiple meanings (ishtirāk) or a figurative usage (majāz)? How would your certainty change if you discovered you had overlooked such a possibility?
?
You have likely been certain about some religious rulings based on texts. Reflect on one such belief. Did you ever verify all ten conditions? If not, are you willing to re-examine that belief with the same rigor Razi demands—even if it challenges your comfort?
?
Razi lists 'absence of a rational objection' as a condition. Is there a rational objection to any of your deeply held beliefs that you have been avoiding? What would it mean for your faith if that objection were valid, and how would you respond?

The Rational Objection and Its Epistemic Impact
Razi explains the rational objection condition and its methodological implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that rejecting a rational objection for the sake of transmission undermines reason, and how this affects the certainty of scriptural proofs.

Core Idea
Reason is the foundation for transmission; undermining reason destroys both.

After listing the ten conditions, Razi adds a crucial nuance: if a rational objection exists, giving preference to transmission over reason would discredit reason itself, which is the very tool needed to validate transmission. This lesson explores that circularity and its consequences for theological method.

Guided Path
1
Read Razi's argument on rational objection
Focus on the sentence starting with 'إذ ترجيح النقل على العقل'. Notice how he links the validity of transmission to the integrity of reason.
This is a key methodological point in Islamic theology.
2
Understand the dependency relationship
Reason is used to establish the reliability of transmission (e.g., historical criticism). If we reject reason in favor of transmission, we lose the basis for trusting transmission itself.
3
Analyze the conclusion
Razi says: if the evidence itself is probable, the result is even more probable. Think about what this means for the strength of conclusions drawn from texts.
This is a probabilistic epistemology.
4
Consider the jurists' alternative view
Many jurists hold that linguistic evidence yields certainty despite these conditions. Why might they disagree? How does Razi's position affect practical interpretation?
Compare with the preceding list of conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

Have you ever assumed that a text's meaning is obvious without considering these linguistic subtleties? How does this realization affect your approach to reading and interpreting scripture?
?
The passage notes that if the evidence (the 'producer') is only probable, the conclusion is even less certain. In your daily life, do you base important decisions on probable evidence from texts without verifying the reliability of the chain? How does this lesson call you to examine the epistemic foundations of your beliefs?

Transmitted Matters and the Circle of Proof
Razi explains the dependency of transmitted matters on the Messenger's truthfulness and the problem of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why transmitted reports cannot prove the truthfulness of the Messenger due to circularity, and how Razi categorizes matters that can be proven by transmission.

Core Idea
Transmitted knowledge presupposes the Messenger's truthfulness; proof cannot be circular.

In this passage, Razi establishes a critical epistemological principle for Islamic theology: all transmitted knowledge ultimately depends on the trustworthiness of the Messenger. But if we try to prove that trustworthiness through transmission alone, we fall into a logical circle. Razi explains what can and cannot be established via transmission, and introduces the categories of general and specific reports.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let the logical flow settle.
The passage starts with a statement and follows with a rule and classification.
2
Identify the circularity rule
The passage says: anything whose knowledge depends on knowledge of the Messenger's truthfulness cannot be proven by transmission, otherwise circularity (dawr). Why is that?
Circularity would occur if we used a report to prove the very thing needed to trust that report.
3
Map the categories of reports
Find the three categories: (1) reports about events not rationally necessary must be from transmission only, either general (ʿādiyāt) or specific (Book & Sunnah); (2) matters outside these can be proven by reason and transmission together.
Notice that the Messenger's truthfulness itself is not provable by transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : النقليات بأثرها مستندة إلى صدق الرسول ) كل ما يتوقف العلم بصدق الرسول على العلم به لا يمكن إثباته بالنقل وإلا لزم الدور ، أما الذي لا يكون كذلك فكل ما كان خبرا بوقوع بما لا يجب عقلا وقوعه كان الطريق إليه النقل ليس إلا وهو إما العام كالعاديات أو الخاص كالكتاب والسنة ، والخارج عن القسمين يمكن إثباته في الجملة بالعقل والنقل معا . أقول: الذي يستند إلى صدق الرسول فقط كالأشياء التي نقلت عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام بالتواتر فإن
A question: Transmitted matters as a whole are based on the truthfulness of the Messenger. Everything the knowledge of which depends on knowledge of the truthfulness of the Messenger cannot be proven by transmission, otherwise it would lead to circularity. As for that which is not so, then everything that is a report (khabar) about an occurrence that is not rationally necessary can only be accessed through transmission, and it is either general, like normal events (ʿādiyāt), or specific, like the Book and the Sunnah. And that which falls outside the two categories can be proven in general by both reason and transmission together. I say: That which depends only on the truthfulness of the Messenger, like those things transmitted from him (peace be upon him) by tawātur, then...
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational principle in kalam epistemology: all transmitted knowledge (النقليات) ultimately depends on the truthfulness of the Messenger (صدق الرسول). The author establishes a rule: anything that requires knowledge of the Messenger's truthfulness to be known cannot itself be proven through transmission, because that would lead to circularity (dawr). For example, to accept a report that the Messenger said something, we must already know he is truthful, so that report cannot be used to prove his truthfulness. Conversely, matters that do not require this prior knowledge can be transmitted. The author divides transmitted reports into two types: general reports about normal events (العاديات) and specific reports like the Quran and Sunnah. Any matter outside these two categories can be proven by both reason and transmission. The commentary adds that things that rely solely on the Messenger's truthfulness are those transmitted via tawātur (massive concurrent testimony). This lesson underscores the priority of rational verification of prophethood before accepting any transmission.

Key Terms
نقليات

دور
عاديات
كتاب
سنة
تواتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that transmitted proofs can independently establish the truth of the Messenger.
Correction: Razi shows they presuppose knowledge of his truthfulness, leading to circularity if used for that purpose.

Checkpoint
Why can't the truthfulness of the Messenger be proven by transmitted reports alone?
A) Because they are not reliable
B) Because it would lead to circularity
C) Because reason is not needed
D) Because the Messenger is infallible

Key Takeaway
The truthfulness of the Messenger must be established rationally before any transmitted report can be accepted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a teaching from a scholar or a book, do you ever pause to ask whether your trust in that source is rationally grounded, or do you simply rely on habit? How does Razi's warning about circularity expose a weakness in your own pursuit of certainty?
?
Is your conviction in the truthfulness of the Messenger Muhammad (ﷺ) a product of inherited culture or a reasoned conviction you could defend to a skeptic? If you were asked today to prove his prophethood using only reason, could you do it?
?
You regularly read the Quran and Hadith as authoritative. But have you ever earnestly examined the rational proof that makes them authoritative in the first place? Commit this week to study at least one rational proof of prophethood so that your faith rests on a foundation that cannot be shaken.

Ten Conditions for Verbal Certainty
Razi lists ten prerequisites for a verbal proof to yield certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ten conditions that must be met for a verbal proof (textual evidence) to yield certainty in Islamic theology, and the logical interdependence between reason and transmitted evidence.

Core Idea
Verbal evidence yields certainty only after ten rigorous conditions are verified.

Razi, a leading Ash'ari theologian, presents a crucial methodological principle: textual evidence cannot be taken as certain unless a series of conditions are fulfilled. This lesson explains each of these ten conditions and the reasoning behind them, showing why even clear texts require careful scrutiny.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Razi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the ten conditions
Go through the list and identify each condition. Notice that they cover transmission, language, context, and rational consistency.
The tenth condition about rational objection is explained with a brief argument.
3
Understand the rational objection argument
Razi argues that if a rational objection exists, it outweighs the textual evidence. He then explains why preferring transmitted evidence over reason would be self-defeating.
This shows the importance of reason in validating transmitted knowledge.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Razi ends by pointing out that if the premises are only probable, the conclusion is even more uncertain. Think about the implications for theological arguments based on texts.
This highlights the need for rigorous methodology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : الدليل اللفظ لا يفيد اليقين إلا عند تيقن أمور عشرة ) عصمة رواة مفردات تلك الألفاظ وإعرابها وتصريفها وعدم الاشتراك والمجاز والنقل والتخصيص بالأشخاص والأزمنة وعدم الإضمار والتأخير والتقديم والنسخ وعدم المعارض العقلى الذي لو كان لرجح عليه، إذ ترجيح النقل على العقل يقتضى القدح في العقل المستلزم للقدح في النقل لافتقاره إليه وإذا كان المنتج ظنيا فما ظنك بالنتيجة
Issue: Verbal proof (al-dalīl al-lafẓī) does not yield certainty (yaqīn) except when ten matters are certain: the integrity (ʿiṣma) of the transmitters of the individual words, their inflection (iʿrāb), their morphology (taṣrīf), the absence of homonymy (ishtirāk), metaphor (majāz), transferred meaning (naql), specification by persons and times (takhṣīṣ), and the absence of ellipsis (iḍmār), postponement and advancement (taqdīm wa-taʾkhīr), abrogation (naskh), and the absence of a rational objection (al-muʿāriḍ al-ʿaqlī) which, if it existed, would outweigh it; for preferring transmission over reason would entail disparaging reason, which necessitates disparaging transmission due to its dependence on it. If the product of the process is opinion (ẓannī), what then do you think of the conclusion?
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage states a rule: a verbal proof (textual evidence) does not produce certainty unless ten conditions are verified. The conditions are: (1) the integrity of transmitters of the words; (2) their inflection (iʿrab); (3) their morphology (tasrīf); (4) absence of homonymy (ishtirāk); (5) absence of metaphor (majāz); (6) absence of transferred meaning (naql); (7) absence of specification by persons and times (takhṣīṣ); (8) absence of ellipsis (iḍmār), postponement or advancement (taqdīm/taʾkhīr); (9) absence of abrogation (naskh); and (10) absence of a rational objection (muʿāriḍ ʿaqlī) that would outweigh the textual evidence, because preferring transmitted evidence over reason would impugn reason, which ultimately undermines transmission itself since transmission depends on reason. The conclusion is that if the premises are only probable, the result is highly uncertain.

تخصيص

تقديم وتأخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One may assume that any clear text yields certainty without further conditions.
Correction: This passage shows that numerous conditions must be verified, including the integrity of transmission, linguistic clarity, and absence of rational objections.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi say that preferring transmitted evidence over reason would lead to disparaging transmission?
A) Because transmission is always weaker.
B) Because transmission depends on reason for verification.
C) Because reason is infallible.
D) Because the ten conditions are rarely met.

Key Takeaway
A verbal proof only yields certainty after ten conditions are verified, showing the interdependence of reason and transmitted evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How many of the ten conditions have you personally verified for the texts you rely on for your most deeply held beliefs? Are you comfortable with the possibility that your 'certainty' may only be probable?
?
Razi says that preferring transmitted evidence over reason would disparage reason, and thus the transmission itself. In your own pursuit of religious knowledge, do you ever suppress rational questions to avoid challenging what you've been taught? What would you need to change to truly honor both reason and revelation?
?
If the product of the process (the ten conditions) is only probable, what does that mean for the certainty of your current beliefs? Can you accept that some of your convictions may be built on a foundation that is less than certain, and still live faithfully?

Disagreement with Jurists on Verbal Proof and Certainty
Razi reports disagreement with jurists on verbal proof yielding certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate between theologians and jurists on whether verbal proof can yield certain knowledge.

Core Idea
Verbal proof can yield certainty—according to jurists, but not without conditions.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi highlights a key disagreement between theologians and jurists regarding the epistemological status of verbal proofs. While the author (al-Muṣannif) maintains that verbal proofs cannot yield certainty without fulfilling ten conditions, many jurists (fuqahā') assert that such proofs do yield certainty. Razi also notes an additional nuance about the clear verses of the Qur'an. This lesson examines the disagreement and its implications for theological methodology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation to get a sense of the arguments presented.
Pay attention to the phrase 'كثير من الفقهاء يقولون' which introduces the opposing view.
2
Identify the two opposing positions
Note that many jurists say verbal proof yields knowledge (ʿilm), while the author holds it does not. Understand the contrast.
The author is al-Muṣannif, likely referring to an earlier theologian or al-Razi himself.
3
Examine the added condition in some manuscripts
Some copies add 'absence of abrogation' and mention that clear Qur'anic verses (muḥkamāt) are free from doubt due to textual issues or rational objection.
This shows an effort to specify cases where certainty might be attained.
4
Understand the sources of doubt
Razi notes that doubt can still arise from metaphor (majāz), specification (takhṣīṣ), or ellipsis (iḍmār). Consider why these are problematic.
These are common rhetorical devices that affect clarity.
5
Reflect on the significance of the disagreement
Think about how this debate reflects different epistemological standards in kalam versus fiqh.
Jurists may be more permissive in accepting certainty from verbal proofs due to practical legal needs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: كثير من الفقهاء يقولون : الدليل اللفظي يفيد العلم ، وذهب المصنف إلى أنه لا يفيده عد هذه الأمور ، ويزاد في بعض النسخ وعدم النسخ ومحكمات القرآن لا يقع فيها شك بسبب رواة الألفاظ وتصريفها وإعرابها والاشتراك والنسخ والتقديم والتأخير و بسبب المعارض العقلى فإن وقع فيها شك بسبب المجاز أو التخصيص أو الإضمار فممكن .
I say: Many jurists (fuqahāʾ) say that verbal proof (al-dalīl al-lafẓī) yields knowledge (ʿilm), while the author (al-muṣannif) holds that it does not yield it, enumerating these matters. And in some copies it is added: the absence of abrogation (naskh), and the clear verses of the Qurʾān (muḥkamāt) are not subject to doubt due to transmitters of words, their morphology, inflection, homonymy, abrogation, postponement and advancement, and due to rational objection. If doubt occurs in them due to metaphor (majāz), specification (takhṣīṣ), or ellipsis (iḍmār), then it is possible.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This comment clarifies that many jurists hold a different opinion: they believe verbal proof can yield certain knowledge, contrary to the author's position that it does not without the ten conditions. The author (al-muṣannif) has listed these conditions to show why certainty is not attained. Some manuscript copies add the condition of absence of abrogation (naskh) and specify that the clear verses of the Qur'an (muḥkamāt) are not subject to doubt due to textual transmission issues (like transmitters, inflection, etc.) or rational objection. However, doubt can still arise due to metaphor, specification, or ellipsis. This highlights the stricter epistemological standards of the kalam tradition compared to fiqh.

علم
فقهاء
مصنف
محكمات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All scholars agree that verbal proofs never yield certainty.
Correction: In fact, many jurists hold that verbal proofs do yield certainty, and Razi notes this disagreement.

Checkpoint
According to this comment, what is the position of many jurists regarding verbal proof?
A) It yields only opinion.
B) It yields certain knowledge.
C) It yields certainty only after ten conditions.
D) It yields nothing.

Key Takeaway
The disagreement on whether verbal proof yields certainty highlights the different epistemological standards between kalam and fiqh.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a verse of the Qur'an, do you ever pause to consider whether your understanding is influenced by metaphor, specification, or ellipsis? How does that awareness affect the certainty you attach to your interpretation?
?
The jurists claim verbal proof yields certainty, but the author disagrees. Have you ever treated a religious text as absolutely certain without examining the conditions that must be met? What would it take for you to re-evaluate that certainty with the same rigor?
?
The passage mentions rational objection as a potential source of doubt. Is there a belief you hold from scripture that you have not subjected to rational scrutiny? What might you discover if you did?

The Circularity Problem in Transmitted Knowledge
Razi states the foundational principle and circularity condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why foundational truths about Allah cannot be proven by transmitted sources alone, due to the risk of circular reasoning.

Core Idea
Prerequisites for believing the Prophet must be proven by reason, not transmission.

Razi opens with a critical methodological principle: transmitted matters rest on the Prophet's truthfulness, but if a truth is itself needed to establish that truthfulness, proving it from transmitted sources would create a vicious circle. This lesson clarifies that principle and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening principle
Read Razi's statement that all transmitted matters depend on the Prophet's truthfulness. Notice the immediate qualification about circularity.
The Arabic term for circularity is 'دور' (dawr).
2
Identify the circularity condition
Understand that any matter that must be known before we can know the Prophet's truthfulness cannot be proven by transmission. Trace Razi's logical reasoning.
Example: the existence of the Creator is a prerequisite.
3
Reflect on examples
Think of examples that fall into this category, such as the existence of God, His attributes, and prophethood itself. These must be proven by reason.
Razi mentions 'إثبات الصانع' in the commentary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : النقليات بأثرها مستندة إلى صدق الرسول ) كل ما يتوقف العلم بصدق الرسول على العلم به لا يمكن إثباته بالنقل وإلا لزم الدور ، أما الذى لا يكون كذلك فكل ما كان خبرا بوقوع بما لا يجب عقلا وقوعه كان الطريق إليه النقل ليس إلا وهو إما العام كالعاديات أو الخاص كالكتاب والسنة ، والخارج عن القسمين يمكن إثباته في الجملة بالعقل و النقل معا . أقول : الذى يستند إلى صدق الرسول فقط كالأشياء التي نقلت عنه عليه الصلاة والسلام بالتواتر فإن النقل عنه يصير ضروريا كأمره بخمس صلوات تشتمل على سبع عشرة ركعة في اليوم والليلة وأمثال ذلك لا طريق إليها إلا العقل ، وكل ما كان العلم بصدق الرسول متوقفا عليه هو كإثبات الصانع العالم المقادر المختار المتكلم ، والخارج عن القسمين كتوحيد الإله وعصمة الأنبياء ، والنقل العام كالعاديات مثل ما ينقل بالتواتر الذى يحصل العلم به لمن يصدق الرسول ومن لا يصدقه ، والخاص لمن يصدقه هو ما اشتمل عليه الكتاب والسنة
Issue: Transmitted matters (al-naqliyyāt) in their entirety are based on the truthfulness (ṣidq) of the Messenger. Everything the knowledge of which depends on knowing the truthfulness of the Messenger cannot be proven by transmission, otherwise circularity (dawr) would result. As for that which is not so, everything that is a report of an occurrence that is not rationally necessary is reached only by transmission, and it is either general (ʿāmm) like customary matters (al-ʿādiyyāt) or specific (khāṣṣ) like the Book and the Sunna. What is outside these two categories can be proven in general by both reason and transmission together. I say: That which depends solely on the truthfulness of the Messenger is like things transmitted from him (peace and blessings upon him) by tawātur (mass transmission), for transmission from him becomes necessary (ḍarūrī), such as his command of five prayers comprising seventeen rakʿas in the day and night and the like—there is no way to them except reason. And everything upon which knowledge of the truthfulness of the Messenger depends is like proving the Creator as knowing, powerful, choosing, speaking. What is outside the two categories is like the oneness of God and the infallibility of prophets. General transmission (al-naql al-ʿāmm) is like customary matters transmitted by tawātur that yield knowledge to both those who believe the Messenger and those who do not. Specific transmission (al-naql al-khāṣṣ) for those who believe him is what the Book and Sunna contain.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage addresses the epistemological foundation of transmitted knowledge. The key principle: anything whose knowledge is a prerequisite for knowing the Prophet's truthfulness cannot be proven by transmission alone, because that would be circular. For example, proving the existence of God (the Creator) is required before we can accept the Prophet's claim; if we tried to prove it from the Prophet's words, we would be assuming his truthfulness. So such matters must be proven by reason. On the other hand, matters that are contingent (not rationally necessary) and reported as events, like specific rituals, can only be known through transmission. That transmission is either general (like commonly known customary events) or specific (like the Qur'an and Sunna). The commentator elaborates: matters known solely by the Prophet's truthfulness (like the number of rakʿas) are known by tawātur, which yields necessary knowledge even to non-believers. Matters that are prerequisites for knowing his truthfulness (like God's attributes) are proven by reason alone. Matters like tawḥīd and ʿiṣma are outside these divisions and can be proven by both reason and transmission. The comment clarifies the categories: general transmission (like historical events known by tawātur) gives knowledge to both believers and non-believers; specific transmission (the Book and Sunna) gives knowledge only to those who believe the Prophet.

Key Terms
نقليات
صدق الرسول
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all religious knowledge, including the existence of God, can be derived solely from the Qur'an.
Correction: Razi teaches that knowledge of God's existence and attributes must be proven by reason first, because they are prerequisites for believing the Prophet. Using the Qur'an to prove God would be circular.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, why can't the knowledge that serves as a prerequisite for knowing the Prophet's truthfulness be proven by transmission?
A) Because transmission is inherently unreliable.
B) Because it would lead to circular reasoning.
C) Because the Prophet forbade it.
D) Because reason is superior to transmission.

Key Takeaway
Any truth that logically precedes belief in the Prophet must be established by reason, not transmitted sources, to avoid circularity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek to strengthen your faith, do you rely on texts from the Quran and hadith? According to this lesson, what must you have already established by reason before you can properly trust those texts? Have you done that? Be honest.
?
If someone asked you why you believe the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) is truthful, what would you say? Is your answer circular? How can you avoid circularity using Razi's condition?
?
This lesson teaches that some truths must be proven by reason, not transmitted sources. Is there a truth about Allah you believe in that you have not personally reasoned through? What one step can you take today to rationally ground that belief?

Categories of Transmitted Matters
Razi classifies transmitted matters into general, specific, and mixed categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of matters that can be known through transmission into general, specific, and mixed categories.

Core Idea
Transmitted knowledge divides into general customs, specific texts, and matters known by both reason and report.

Having established the circularity principle, Razi now outlines what kinds of matters can be known through transmission. He introduces three broad categories: matters that are not rationally necessary and can only be known by transmission (further split into general and specific), and matters that can be known by both reason and transmission. The commentator then expands on each category with concrete examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read Razi's categorization of matters that are 'not rationally necessary' and are known only by transmission. Note the two subtypes: general (customary) and specific (Book and Sunna).
The third category is for matters that can be known by both reason and transmission.
2
Examine the commentator's examples
Read the 'Aqulu' section where the commentator explains each category with examples: tawatur for matters dependent on the Prophet's truthfulness, rational proofs for prerequisites, and matters like tawhid and 'isma for the mixed category.
Pay attention to the distinction between general and specific transmission.
3
Apply the categories
Think about which category each of the following belongs to: the number of daily prayers, the existence of God, the punishment of the grave.
The number of prayers is known only by transmission (specific), the existence of God by reason, the punishment of the grave by both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Everything the knowledge of which depends on knowing the truthfulness of the Messenger cannot be proven by transmission, otherwise circularity (dawr) would result. And everything upon which knowledge of the truthfulness of the Messenger depends is like proving the Creator as knowing, powerful, choosing, speaking. What is outside the two categories is like the oneness of God and the infallibility of prophets. Specific transmission (al-naql al-khāṣṣ) for those who believe him is what the Book and Sunna contain.

This passage addresses the epistemological foundation of transmitted knowledge. The key principle: anything whose knowledge is a prerequisite for knowing the Prophet's truthfulness cannot be proven by transmission alone, because that would be circular. How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the Qur'an or listen to a hadith, do you accept it solely because it comes from the Prophet, or have you ever paused to verify it with your reason? How would you respond if a transmitted report contradicted a rationally established truth?
?
The lesson distinguishes between matters known to everyone (like historical facts) and matters known only to believers (like the specifics of revelation). Which statements about your faith do you hold because they are rationally undeniable, and which do you hold purely on the authority of the Prophet? How does that affect your certainty?
?
Razi says that some matters like the oneness of God can be proven by both reason and transmission. Have you ever tried to reason your way to tawhid from first principles, or do you rely solely on what you've been taught? What would you say to someone who challenges the oneness of God without relying on scripture?

Philosophers on Quiddities: Not Unknown, Not Uncreated
Razi clarifies the philosophers' view on quiddities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' claim that quiddities are not unknown and how Razi clarifies that this does not mean quiddities are uncreated.

Core Idea
A quiddity's self-identity is a conceptual necessity, not a creative act.

Razi begins by addressing a subtle point about the philosophers' view on quiddities. Some might think that saying quiddities are 'not unknown' implies they are not created. Razi corrects this: the philosophers only mean that once a quiddity is supposed, its being that quiddity is a necessary property, not something produced by a maker. This is a key distinction for understanding the debate on the ontological status of essences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage where Razi reports the philosophers' view and then clarifies it. Focus on the phrase 'إذا فرضت ماهية فكونها تلك الماهية لا يكون مجمل جاعل'.
The term 'مجمل جاعل' (comprehensive maker) is key.
2
Identify the philosophers' position
What is the actual claim of those who say quiddities are not unknown? According to Razi, they do not deny creation; they affirm a conceptual necessity.
3
Understand Razi's clarification
Razi explains that the quiddity's self-identity (كونها تلك الماهية) is not due to a maker but is a necessary consequence of its being supposed. This preserves the possibility of creation while accounting for essential definitions.
This distinction avoids conflating conceptual analysis with causal explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول هذا نقل المذاهب وليس فيه موضع بحث. والقائلون بأن الماهيات غير مجهولة لم يقولوا بأنها غير مبدعة بل قالوا إذا فرضت ماهية فكونها تلك الماهية لا يكون مجمل جاعل وهذه ضرورة تلحقها بعد فرضها تلك الماهية. وقول المعتزلة: إن تأثير الفاعل ليس في جعل الذوات ذواتا ليس هكذا لأنهم يجعلون الذوات المعدومة ثابتة في الأزل من غير تأثير فاعل، ولما جعلوا الذوات متساوية في الذاتية احتاجوا إلى إثبات صفات الأجناس وإلا فكان الكل نوعا واحدا. والأعراض المشروطة بالحياة هي الاعتقادات والظنون والأنظار والقدر والشهوات والنفارات والآلام والإرادات والكراهات وهي مع الحياة عشرة، والموت عند أبي علي أيضا منها. والتحيز هي الصفة المختصة بالجواهر التي لأجلها يحتاج إلى حيز وتقتضيها الجوهرية وهي مشروطة بالوجود. أما الكائنية المعللة بالحصول في الحيز ككون الجوهر متحركا أو ساكنا أو مجتمعا أو متفرقا وهي معللة بالأكوان التي هي الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق بشرط الوجود. والاتصاف بالوجود يكون بالفاعل. والأعراض بدل التحيز، والحصول في الحيز صفة واحدة لأجلها يحتاج إلى محل. وأدلة كل قوم منهم والكلام فيها وعليها كثيرة لكنها قليلة الفائدة فلنعرض عنها.
I say: This is a report of the schools, and there is no place for discussion in it. And those who say that quiddities are not unknown do not say that they are not created; rather they say: if a quiddity is supposed, its being that quiddity is not due to a comprehensive maker; rather this is a necessity that attaches to it after it is supposed as that quiddity. And the statement of the Mu'tazila: 'The effect of the agent is not in making essences essences' is not like this, because they make non-existent essences established in pre-eternity without the effect of an agent. And since they made essences equal in essence-hood, they needed to establish genus-attributes; otherwise everything would be one type. And the accidents conditional upon life are beliefs, opinions, considerations, power, appetites, aversions, pains, wills, and dislikes; and they are ten together with life, and death according to Abu 'Ali is also among them. And spatial occupation (tahayyuz) is the attribute specific to substances, due to which it needs a place and which is required by substantiality, and it is conditional upon existence. As for the 'kainiyya' (spatial being) caused by position in place, such as the substance being moving, stationary, together, or separated, it is caused by the modes (akwan) which are motion, rest, conjunction, and separation, conditional upon existence. And the being described by existence is from the agent. And accidents are instead of spatial occupation, and position in place is one attribute due to which it needs a locus. And the proofs of each group and the discussion concerning them and against them are many but of little benefit, so we refrain from them.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This is an editorial note, likely by Razi himself, commenting on the previous reports. First, he clarifies that the philosophers' view that quiddities are 'not unknown' does not mean they are uncreated; rather, quiddity's self-identity is not due to a maker but is a necessary consequence of supposing that quiddity. Then he criticizes the Mu'tazila: they claim the agent's effect is not to make essences essences, yet they treat non-existent essences as eternally established without any agent, which is problematic. To avoid all essences being identical, they introduced genus-attributes. Then he lists the ten life-dependent accidents (beliefs, opinions, etc.) and notes that Abu 'Ali adds death. He defines spatial occupation (tahayyuz) as a substance-specific attribute requiring a place, and distinguishes it from the 'kainiyya' (the actual being in a state like motion or rest). He concludes that the arguments on these topics are many but of little benefit, and he will not delve into them. This note shows Razi's critical and sometimes dismissive attitude towards excessive dialectic.

Key Terms
ماهية
جعل
فاعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if quiddities are 'not unknown', they are eternal and uncreated.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the philosophers only mean that quiddity's self-identity is a necessary consequence of supposition, not that it exists without a creator.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the philosophers' actual claim about quiddities?
A) Quiddities are uncreated and eternal.
B) Quiddities are created but their self-identity is a conceptual necessity.
C) Quiddities are unknown.
D) Quiddities are made by a comprehensive maker.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers distinguish between the existence of a quiddity (created) and its essential self-identity (a conceptual necessity).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you ever treat your identity—your 'humanness'—as if it were self-sufficient and independent of being constantly created by Allah? How can you train yourself to see every aspect of your existence as contingent upon Him?
?
The philosophers admitted that the essence of a thing is a necessary truth after supposition, but they still affirmed that existence is created. Are there areas in your life where you assume certain truths are 'just the way things are' without acknowledging that Allah's will sustains them every moment? What would it mean to change that assumption?
?
This lesson shows that even non-Muslim philosophers recognized a distinction between necessary essence and created existence. If they could grasp that much, what does that say about your own responsibility to live with constant awareness of Allah's creative power in every thought and action? Will you commit today to pausing before every belief you hold and reminding yourself: 'Its existence is from Allah, not its definition'?

Critique of Mu'tazila on Essences and Genus-Attributes
Razi criticizes the Mu'tazilite view on essences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's critique of the Mu'tazila regarding their claim that the agent does not make essences essences, and why they had to introduce genus-attributes.

Core Idea
Mu'tazila's claim that essences are not made leads to an infinite regress or all essences being identical.

Razi now turns to the Mu'tazila. They assert that the agent's effect is not in making essences essences. Razi points out that this logically forces them to treat non-existent essences as eternally established without any agent, and to avoid all essences collapsing into one type, they need genus-attributes. This critique highlights the internal tensions in Mu'tazilite ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique
Read Razi's statement about the Mu'tazila. Notice how he first reports their view and then presents his objection.
The phrase 'ليس هكذا' signals the critique.
2
Understand the Mu'tazilite position
The Mu'tazila think the agent does not make essences essences; they treat essences as self-identical in pre-eternity.
3
Grasp Razi's objection
Razi argues that if essences are not made, they must be eternal, and if they are all equally essences, they would be one type unless differentiated by genus-attributes.
This shows the need for attributes that distinguish one essence from another.

According to Razi, why did the Mu'tazila need to assert genus-attributes?
A) To prove the existence of God.

D) Because they wanted to deny accidents.

Key Takeaway
Razi exposes an internal inconsistency in Mu'tazilite ontology: if essences are not made, they must be eternally distinct, which forces the addition of genus-attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Mu'tazila added genus-attributes to protect their theory. Are there beliefs you hold that require you to add 'explanations' to avoid contradictions, rather than surrendering to the simpler truth of God's absolute sovereignty? Which belief might that be, and will you examine it today?

Accidents Conditional upon Life: The Ten (or Eleven)
Razi enumerates the accidents conditional upon life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the list of accidents that depend on life according to Razi's editorial note, including the note on Abu 'Ali's addition of death.

Core Idea
Life-dependent accidents are ten (plus death for Abu 'Ali) and include mental and volitional states.

Razi provides a concise enumeration of the accidents that require life as a condition. These are attributes possible only for living beings. He lists nine specific items plus life itself, making ten, and notes that Abu 'Ali (al-Jubba'i) also counts death among them. This enumeration is important for understanding the ontology of living substances in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the enumeration
Read the Arabic list of life-dependent accidents. Count them and note the mention of Abu 'Ali.
The list includes: اعتقادات, ظنون, أنظار, قدر, شهوات, نفرات, آلام, إرادات, كراهات.
2
Identify what they share
These are all psychological states: beliefs, opinions, considerations, power, appetites, aversions, pains, wills, and dislikes.
3
Note the variant view
Razi mentions that Abu 'Ali adds death to this list. Consider why death might be considered a life-conditioned accident.
This shows different schools had different categorizations.

?

?

Tahayyuz and Kainiyya: Spatial Attributes of Substances
Razi defines and distinguishes tahayyuz and kainiyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between tahayyuz (spatial occupation) and kainiyya (spatial being) as defined by Razi.

Core Idea
Tahayyuz is a necessary attribute of substance; kainiyya is a contingent state like motion or rest.

Razi clarifies two key attributes of substances: tahayyuz, which is the essential property of occupying space and is conditional upon existence, and kainiyya, which refers to the substance's mode of being in a place (moving, stationary, together, or separated). This distinction helps in understanding how substances are categorized in kalam ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions
Read the passage defining tahayyuz and kainiyya. Note the conditions: tahayyuz is specific to substances and requires existence; kainiyya is caused by the modes (akwan).
2
Compare the two concepts
Tahayyuz is a static attribute of being spatially extended; kainiyya is the dynamic state of being in a particular spatial condition.
Both are conditional upon existence.
3
Understand the causes
Tahayyuz is required by substantiality itself; kainiyya is caused by the akwan (motion, rest, conjunction, separation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And the statement of the Mu'tazila: 'The effect of the agent is not in making essences essences' is not like this, because they make non-existent essences established in pre-eternity without the effect of an agent. And since they made essences equal in essence-hood, they needed to establish genus-attributes; otherwise everything would be one type. And the accidents conditional upon life are beliefs, opinions, considerations, power, appetites, aversions, pains, wills, and dislikes; and they are ten together with life, and death according to Abu 'Ali is also among them. And spatial occupation (tahayyuz) is the attribute specific to substances, due to which it needs a place and which is required by substantiality, and it is conditional upon existence. As for the 'kainiyya' (spatial being) caused by position in place, such as the substance being moving, stationary, together, or separated, it is caused by the modes (akwan) which are motion, rest, conjunction, and separation, conditional upon existence. And the being described by existence is from the agent. And accidents are instead of spatial occupation, and position in place is one attribute due to which it needs a locus. And the proofs of each group and the discussion concerning them and against them are many but of little benefit, so we refrain from them.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This is an editorial note, likely by Razi himself, commenting on the previous reports. First, he clarifies that the philosophers' view that quiddities are 'not unknown' does not mean they are uncreated; rather, quiddity's self-identity is not due to a maker but is a necessary consequence of supposing that quiddity. Then he criticizes the Mu'tazila: they claim the agent's effect is not to make essences essences, yet they treat non-existent essences as eternally established without any agent, which is problematic. To avoid all essences being identical, they introduced genus-attributes. Then he lists the ten life-dependent accidents (beliefs, opinions, etc.) and notes that Abu 'Ali adds death. He defines spatial occupation (tahayyuz) as a substance-specific attribute requiring a place, and distinguishes it from the 'kainiyya' (the actual being in a state like motion or rest). He concludes that the arguments on these topics are many but of little benefit, and he will not delve into them. This note shows Razi's critical and sometimes dismissive attitude towards excessive dialectic.

Key Terms
تحيز
كائنية
كون
حركة
سكون
اجتماع
افتراق

C) Existence
D) Spatial occupation (tahayyuz)

Key Takeaway
Tahayyuz is an essential attribute of substances; kainiyya is a contingent state caused by the four modes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if your spiritual state is an unchangeable essential property (like tahayyuz) or do you recognize that your actions and states are contingent (like kainiyya) and can be transformed by Allah's will? How does this distinction affect your hope for change and your reliance on Him?
?
Razi notes that both tahayyuz and kainiyya are conditional upon existence. If your very existence is contingent on Allah's will, how does that awareness shape your daily gratitude and dependence on Him?
?
Consider your own character: what are the essential traits that define you as a believer (like tahayyuz), and what are the accidental states you can change (like kainiyya)? Are you neglecting to work on the states that are within your control? What concrete step will you take today to improve one such state?

Concluding Remarks: Existence, Accidents, and Dismissal of Arguments
Razi concludes with remarks on existence and dismisses further debate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's concluding statements on existence being from the agent, accidents replacing tahayyuz, and his dismissal of further arguments as of little benefit.

Core Idea
Razi cuts short further dialectical debate, deeming it unfruitful.

Razi wraps up the editorial note with a few technical remarks: he clarifies that being described by existence is from the agent, that accidents serve in place of spatial occupation for non-substances, and that the proofs and counter-proofs on these topics are abundant but of little use. He explicitly decides to refrain from discussing them further, reflecting his pragmatic approach to theological debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding passage
Read the final sentences. Notice the statements about existence coming from the agent and accidents being substitutes for tahayyuz.
2
Understand the key points
Existence is from the agent; accidents have a different spatial relationship (instead of tahayyuz). The 'hulul' (inherence) is a single attribute requiring a locus.
3
Appreciate Razi's stance
Razi states that the many arguments on these topics are 'little of benefit' and he will not engage. This shows his preference for concise, useful discourse.
This might be seen as a criticism of excessive dialectics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول هذا نقل المذاهب وليس فيه موضع بحث. والقائلون بأن الماهيات غير مجهولة لم يقولوا بأنها غير مبدعة بل قالوا إذا فرضت ماهية فكونها تلك الماهية لا يكون مجمل جاعل وهذه ضرورة تلحقها بعد فرضها تلك الماهية. وقول المعتزلة: إن تأثير الفاعل ليس في جعل الذوات ذواتا ليس هكذا لأنهم يجعلون الذوات المعدومة ثابتة في الأزل من غير تأثير فاعل، ولما جعلوا الذوات متساوية في الذاتية احتاجوا إلى إثبات صفات الأجناس وإلا فكان الكل نوعا واحدا. والأعراض المشروطة بالحياة هي الاعتقادات والظنون والأنظار والقدر والشهوات والنفارات والآلام والإرادات والكراهات وهي مع الحياة عشرة، والموت عند أبي علي أيضا منها. والتحيز هي الصفة المختصة بالجواهر التي لأجلها يحتاج إلى حيز وتقتضيها الجوهرية وهي مشروطة بالوجود. أما الكائنية المعللة بالحصول في الحيز ككون الجوهر متحركا أو ساكنا أو مجتمعا أو متفرقا وهي معللة بالأكوان التي هي الحركة والسكون والاجتماع والافتراق بشرط الوجود. والاتصاف بالوجود يكون بالفاعل. والأعراض بدل التحيز، والحصول في الحيز صفة واحدة لأجلها يحتاج إلى محل. وأدلة كل قوم منهم والكلام فيها وعليها كثيرة لكنها قليلة الفائدة فلنعرض عنها.
I say: This is a report of the schools, and there is no place for discussion in it. And those who say that quiddities are not unknown do not say that they are not created; rather they say: if a quiddity is supposed, its being that quiddity is not due to a comprehensive maker; rather this is a necessity that attaches to it after it is supposed as that quiddity. This is an editorial note, likely by Razi himself, commenting on the previous reports. Objection: The Principle is Not Self-Evident
The objector argues that the principle of preponderator is not self-evident and demands a proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objector's challenge to the self-evidence of the principle of preponderator and how it sets the stage for further debate.

Core Idea
Is the principle of preponderator self-evident? The objector says no.

We have encountered a proof that relies on the principle 'when two sides are equal, preponderance requires a preponderator.' Now an objector questions the epistemic status of this very principle. Is it self-evident? Or does it require demonstration? This lesson presents that objection and the concessive reply that leads to further objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two claims the objector denies
The objector first denies that the principle is self-evident, then denies that it is demonstrative (unless a proof is provided). Notice the structure: 'if you claim it is self-evident... if you claim it is demonstrative...'
The objector does not outright reject the principle, but questions its epistemic foundation.
2
Understand the comparison with a more obvious truth
The objector compares the principle to the statement 'one is half of two' and finds the latter more certain. This difference indicates that the principle is not as obvious as claimed.
This is a standard method in kalam: comparing propositions to test self-evidence.
3
Note the concessive phrase 'we accept'
After presenting the challenges, the objector says 'we accept the correctness of what you mentioned' (sana). This shows he does not reject the principle outright but is about to oppose it with several counterarguments.
The word 'sana' (or 'sanna') is critical: it signals a concessive move before raising further objections.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : قوليكم ما استويا امتنع الأترجيح إلا لمرجح ، إن ادعيت أنه أمر بديهن فهو ممنوع فإنا لما عرضنا هذه القضية على العقل مع قولنا الواحد نصف الاثنين وجدنا الثانية أظهر والتفاوت يدل على تطرق الاحتمال بوجه ما إلى الأول ، وعند قيام احتمال النقيض لا يبقى اليقين التام ، فإن ادعيت أنه برهاني فأين البرهان . سانا صحة ما ذكرته ليكنه معارض بأمور :
For it might be said: Your statement 'when they are equal, preponderance is impossible except by a preponderator' — if you claim it is self-evident, it is denied, because when we present this proposition to the mind alongside our statement 'one is half of two', we find the second more apparent, and the difference indicates the entry of possibility in some way into the first. And when the possibility of the opposite arises, complete certainty does not remain. If you claim it is demonstrative, where is the proof? [But] we accept the correctness of what you mentioned, yet it is opposed by several things:
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the initial proof. The objector attacks the claim that the principle 'when the two sides are equal, preponderance requires a preponderator' is self-evident. He compares it to the obvious truth that 'one is half of two' and finds the latter more certain. The difference in certainty shows that the principle is not self-evident; it may be open to doubt. Then the objector challenges: if it is demonstrative, produce the proof. Finally, concessively ('sanā' meaning 'we accept'), he says that even if the principle is accepted, it faces several counterarguments ('umūr'). This sets up the subsequent objections.

Key Terms
بديهي
برهاني
الترجيح
المرجح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objector completely rejects the principle of preponderator.
Correction: The objector only questions its epistemic status (self-evidence or demonstrative status), not its truth. He explicitly accepts its correctness before raising counterarguments.

Checkpoint
How does the objector try to show the principle is not self-evident?
A) By claiming it leads to infinite regress.
B) By comparing it to a more obvious truth and noting the difference in certainty.
C) By stating that it is false.
D) By quoting scripture.

Key Takeaway
The objector challenges the self-evidence of the principle of preponderator by comparing it to a more obvious truth and demands a proof if the claim of self-evidence is rejected, then concessively accepts the principle before raising further objections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you held a belief as 'self-evident' only to find it challenged? How did that feel, and what did you do with the uncertainty? This lesson asks you to examine your most certain beliefs: are you willing to test them against the standard of proof?
?
The objector compares the principle to a mathematical truth. Are there truths in your life that you treat as certain but cannot demonstrate? How does that affect your faith and actions? Identify one such belief and ask yourself: is it genuinely self-evident, or do I rely on it without proof?
?
The objector concedes the principle's correctness but still opposes it. Are there arguments you accept as correct but resist because they contradict your desires? What would it take for you to truly accept a truth even when it is uncomfortable? Commit to following truth wherever it leads.
