Foundations: Mode, Substrate, and the Primary Division
Razi defines mode, substrate, subject, and divides possible into accident and substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of mode (hal), substrate (mahall), and subject (mawdu‘), and how possible things are primarily divided into accident and substance.

Core Idea
Everything possible is either an accident in a subject or a substance without one.

Before classifying possible things, the philosophers establish key terms: mode, substrate, and subject. They explain how a mode can sustain its substrate without leading to an infinite regress. This foundation clarifies that accidents reside in subjects, while substances exist independently.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definitions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the definitions of mode, substrate, and subject. Notice how Razi explains the two ways a mode can cause subsistence.
The passage uses technical terms that are foundational for the rest of the classification.
2
Distinguish between substrate and subject
Identify that a substrate that is self-subsistent and sustains what subsists in it is called a subject. Understand why the non-existence of a subject is more general than that of a substrate.
This distinction is crucial for the primary division.
3
Understand the primary division
The possible is either in a subject (accident) or not in a subject (substance). This is the starting point for all further classifications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الممكنات على رأي الحكماء

فنقول : الحال قد يكون سببا لقوام المحل إما بأن يقتضي الحال وجود المحل ثم تصير نفسه حالة فيه أو بأن يقتضي الأثر حلول مؤثره فيه، وعلى هذين التقديرين لا يلزم منه الدير، فالمحل المتقوم بنفسه المقوم لما يحل فيه يسمى بالموضوع وهو أخص من المحل، فيكون عدمه أعم من عدم المحل

أقول : المحل قابل للحال فلا يكون عندهم فاعلا فيه، فالقول بأن يقتضي الأثر حلول مؤثره فيه غيره معقول عندهم، والمراد مهنا من الحال الذي يكون سببا لقوام المحل ههنا هو الصورة ومن المحل الهيولى، ويريدون بهذا البيان أن امتناع الانفكاك بينهما لاحتياج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر لا يقتضي الدير والحال الذي لا يتقوم به محله هو العرض ومحله الموضوع.

إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الممكن إما أن يكون في الموضوع وهو العرض، أو لا يكون وهو الجوهر، والجوهر إما أن يكون في المحل وهو الصورة أو يكون محلا وهو الهيولى أو مركبا من الصورة والهيولي وهو الجسم فقط بالاستقراء أو لا حالا ولا محلا ولا مركبا منهما، وهو إما أن يكون متعلقا بالأجسام تعلق التدبير وهو النفس أو لا يكون وهو العقل

أقول : في قوله « أو مركبا من الصورة والهيولى وهو الجسم فقط بالاستقراء ، نظر فإن الحكماء لا يستعملون الاستقراء ههنا ولا يحتاجون إليه، بل يقسمون الموهر إلى الجسم وأجزائه وإلى ما ليس بجسم ولا بالأجزاء وهذه قسمة حاضرة، ويسمون القسم الأول بالمادى والقسم الثاني بالمفارق، ويقدمون الأول إلى نفس المادة وإلى ما يقومها وإلى ما يتقوم بها . والأول هو الهيولى . والثاني هو الصورة وهما جزاء الجسم والثالث هو الجسم . وأما المفارق فإما أن يتصرف في الماديات أو لا يتصرف وهما النفس والعقل وأسماء أنواع المكيف . أما النوع ا ع الأول فسمى بالانفعاليات والانفعالات، والأول راسخة كحمرة لدم والثاني غير راسخة كمرة الخجل". وأما النوع الثاني فسمى بالحال والملكة أما الحال فسريعة الزوال كغضب الحليم، وأما الملكة فبطيء الزوال كصحة الصحاح . وأما النوع الثالث فسمى بالقوة واللا قوة وليس اسم القوة للدفع فقط فإن الصلابة قوة وهى تهيؤ لأن لا ينفعل بسرعة والبطؤ لا قوة وليس يتأثر عن شيء بل هو ما يقطع ذو الحركة قليلة فالقوة اسم لاستعداد نسبية تفعل الشيء بسهولة أو تنفعل بعسر، واللا قوة اسم لاستعداد نسبية تفعل بعمر أو تنفعل بسهولة، والنوع الرابع ليس له اسم غير ما ذكر .
Division of possible things according to the philosophers.

We say: A mode (al-hal) may be a cause for the subsistence of a substrate (al-mahall) either by the mode necessitating the existence of the substrate, then itself becoming a mode in it, or by the effect necessitating the indwelling of its cause in it. On both suppositions, [infinite] regress does not follow. The substrate that is self-subsistent and that sustains what subsists in it is called the subject (al-mawdu‘), and it is more specific than substrate, so its non-existence is more general than the non-existence of the substrate.

I say: The substrate is receptive to the mode, so according to them it is not an agent in it. The statement that the effect necessitates the indwelling of its cause in it is accepted by them. What is meant here by the mode that is a cause for the subsistence of the substrate is the form (al-sura), and by the substrate is the matter (al-hayula). They intend by this explanation that the impossibility of separation between them due to each needing the other does not entail regress. And the mode that does not sustain its substrate is the accident and its substrate is the subject.

When you understand this, we say: The possible either exists in a subject – and it is an accident – or does not – and it is a substance. Substance either is in a substrate – and it is form – or is a substrate – and it is matter – or is composed of form and matter – and it is body, only by induction – or is neither a mode nor a substrate nor composed of them: either related to bodies with the relation of governance – and it is soul – or not – and it is intellect.

I say: Regarding his statement "or composed of form and matter, and it is body, only by induction", there is a consideration. The philosophers do not use induction here nor need it. Rather, they divide existents into body and its parts, and into what is neither body nor parts; this is a present division. They call the first division material and the second division separate. They take the first to the matter itself, to what sustains it, and to what is sustained by it. The first is matter, the second is form – both are parts of body – and the third is body. As for the separate, either it acts in material things or does not act; they are soul and intellect. Names of types of quality (al-kayf): The first type is called affectivities and passions; the first are stable like the redness of blood, the second are unstable like the redness of shyness. The second type is called state and disposition; state is quickly vanishing like the anger of a mild person, disposition is slowly vanishing like the health of the healthy. The third type is called power and non-power; the name 'power' is not only for repulsion, for hardness is a power – an aptitude not to be quickly affected – and slowness is non-power, not being affected by something but rather that which cuts off having little motion. So 'power' is a name for a relative preparedness to do a thing easily or be affected with difficulty; 'non-power' is a name for a relative preparedness to do with difficulty or be affected easily. The fourth type has no name other than what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a classification of possible things (tawjih al-mumkinat) according to the philosophers. It begins with a definition of 'mode' (al-hal) as something that may cause the subsistence of a substrate. The author distinguishes between two ways: the mode necessitates the existence of the substrate and then becomes a mode in it, or the effect necessitates the indwelling of its cause. In both cases, no infinite regress follows. Then the terms 'subject' (al-mawdu‘) and 'substrate' (al-mahall) are defined: a substrate that is self-subsistent and sustains what subsists in it is called subject. The marginal note clarifies that the substrate is receptive, not an agent, and identifies the mode as form and the substrate as matter in this context. The main classification then divides possible into accident (exists in a subject) and substance (does not exist in a subject). Substance is further divided into form (in a substrate), matter (is a substrate), body (composed of form and matter), soul (related to bodies by governance), and intellect (not related). The marginal note on page 88 criticizes the use of 'by induction' (bil-istiqra’) in the body definition, stating that philosophers prefer a different division. The note then digresses into a classification of qualities (kayfiyyat) into four types: affective qualities (stable and unstable), states and dispositions, powers and non-powers, and quantities-specific qualities. This quality classification seems to be an explanatory comment.

Key Terms
الحال
المحل
الموضوع
الجوهر
العرض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The term 'mode' (hal) here is the same as the kalam concept of 'state' (ahwal).
Correction: In this philosophical context, 'hal' refers to an accident or attribute that inheres in a substrate, not the kalam 'state' which is a distinct ontological category.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary division of possible things?
A) Form and matter
B) Substance and accident
C) Soul and intellect
D) Body and soul

Key Takeaway
The possible is either an accident (existing in a subject) or a substance (not existing in a subject).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that everything in your life that you rely on is either an accident (like health or wealth) or a substance (like your own body), do you truly feel your total dependence on the One who sustains all substrates? How can you actively remind yourself today of that dependence?
?
The passage defines a 'subject' as self‑subsistent, yet you know that only Allah is truly self‑subsistent. In what areas of your life do you pretend to be a 'subject'—self‑sufficient—when you are actually a contingent accident? What step will you take this week to admit your need?
?
Razi's logic shows that every possible thing is either an accident or a substance. Is your relationship with Allah built on the recognition that you are a possible being, or have you fallen into the illusion that you are necessary? What will change in your prayer life because of this truth?

The Five Types of Substance and the Philosophers' Critique
Razi lists five types of substance and includes a critical note on the definition of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five types of substance according to the philosophers and the critique of using induction to define body.

Core Idea
Substance is divided into five: form, matter, body, soul, and intellect.

After dividing possible into accident and substance, Razi presents the fivefold division of substance as per the philosophers. He also includes a critical note questioning the use of induction in defining body, offering an alternative classification.

Guided Path
1
Read the five types of substance
Read the passage listing the five types: form, matter, body, soul, intellect. Notice that body is defined as composed of form and matter 'by induction'.
This division is specific to the philosophers' tradition.
2
Analyze the critique of 'by induction'
The marginal note objects to the phrase 'only by induction' for body. Understand why the philosophers prefer a different division and what it is.
The alternative divides existents into material (body and its parts) and separate (soul and intellect).
3
Compare the two classifications
Compare Razi's fivefold division with the philosophers' material/separate classification. Notice how the material category further splits into matter, form, and body.
Both classifications are attempts to categorize substances.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الممكنات على رأي الحكماء

فنقول : الحال قد يكون سببا لقوام المحل إما بأن يقتضي الحال وجود المحل ثم تصير نفسه حالة فيه أو بأن يقتضي الأثر حلول مؤثره فيه، وعلى هذين التقديرين لا يلزم منه الدير، فالمحل المتقوم بنفسه المقوم لما يحل فيه يسمى بالموضوع وهو أخص من المحل، فيكون عدمه أعم من عدم المحل

أقول : المحل قابل للحال فلا يكون عندهم فاعلا فيه، فالقول بأن يقتضي الأثر حلول مؤثره فيه غيره معقول عندهم، والمراد مهنا من الحال الذي يكون سببا لقوام المحل ههنا هو الصورة ومن المحل الهيولى، ويريدون بهذا البيان أن امتناع الانفكاك بينهما لاحتياج كل واحد منهما إلى الآخر لا يقتضي الدير والحال الذي لا يتقوم به محله هو العرض ومحله الموضوع.

إذا عرفت هذا فنقول : الممكن إما أن يكون في الموضوع وهو العرض، أو لا يكون وهو الجوهر، والجوهر إما أن يكون في المحل وهو الصورة أو يكون محلا وهو الهيولى أو مركبا من الصورة والهيولي وهو الجسم فقط بالاستقراء أو لا حالا ولا محلا ولا مركبا منهما، وهو إما أن يكون متعلقا بالأجسام تعلق التدبير وهو النفس أو لا يكون وهو العقل

أقول : في قوله « أو مركبا من الصورة والهيولى وهو الجسم فقط بالاستقراء ، نظر فإن الحكماء لا يستعملون الاستقراء ههنا ولا يحتاجون إليه، بل يقسمون الموهر إلى الجسم وأجزائه وإلى ما ليس بجسم ولا بالأجزاء وهذه قسمة حاضرة، ويسمون القسم الأول بالمادى والقسم الثاني بالمفارق، ويقدمون الأول إلى نفس المادة وإلى ما يقومها وإلى ما يتقوم بها . والأول هو الهيولى . والثاني هو الصورة وهما جزاء الجسم والثالث هو الجسم . وأما المفارق فإما أن يتصرف في الماديات أو لا يتصرف وهما النفس والعقل وأسماء أنواع المكيف . أما النوع ا ع الأول فسمى بالانفعاليات والانفعالات، والأول راسخة كحمرة لدم والثاني غير راسخة كمرة الخجل". وأما النوع الثاني فسمى بالحال والملكة أما الحال فسريعة الزوال كغضب الحليم، وأما الملكة فبطيء الزوال كصحة الصحاح . وأما النوع الثالث فسمى بالقوة واللا قوة وليس اسم القوة للدفع فقط فإن الصلابة قوة وهى تهيؤ لأن لا ينفعل بسرعة والبطؤ لا قوة وليس يتأثر عن شيء بل هو ما يقطع ذو الحركة قليلة فالقوة اسم لاستعداد نسبية تفعل الشيء بسهولة أو تنفعل بعسر، واللا قوة اسم لاستعداد نسبية تفعل بعمر أو تنفعل بسهولة، والنوع الرابع ليس له اسم غير ما ذكر .
Division of possible things according to the philosophers.

We say: A mode (al-hal) may be a cause for the subsistence of a substrate (al-mahall) either by the mode necessitating the existence of the substrate, then itself becoming a mode in it, or by the effect necessitating the indwelling of its cause in it. On both suppositions, [infinite] regress does not follow. The substrate that is self-subsistent and that sustains what subsists in it is called the subject (al-mawdu‘), and it is more specific than substrate, so its non-existence is more general than the non-existence of the substrate.

I say: The substrate is receptive to the mode, so according to them it is not an agent in it. The statement that the effect necessitates the indwelling of its cause in it is accepted by them. What is meant here by the mode that is a cause for the subsistence of the substrate is the form (al-sura), and by the substrate is the matter (al-hayula). They intend by this explanation that the impossibility of separation between them due to each needing the other does not entail regress. And the mode that does not sustain its substrate is the accident and its substrate is the subject.

When you understand this, we say: The possible either exists in a subject – and it is an accident – or does not – and it is a substance. Substance either is in a substrate – and it is form – or is a substrate – and it is matter – or is composed of form and matter – and it is body, only by induction – or is neither a mode nor a substrate nor composed of them: either related to bodies with the relation of governance – and it is soul – or not – and it is intellect.

I say: Regarding his statement "or composed of form and matter, and it is body, only by induction", there is a consideration. The philosophers do not use induction here nor need it. Rather, they divide existents into body and its parts, and into what is neither body nor parts; this is a present division. They call the first division material and the second division separate. They take the first to the matter itself, to what sustains it, and to what is sustained by it. The first is matter, the second is form – both are parts of body – and the third is body. As for the separate, either it acts in material things or does not act; they are soul and intellect. Names of types of quality (al-kayf): The first type is called affectivities and passions; the first are stable like the redness of blood, the second are unstable like the redness of shyness. The second type is called state and disposition; state is quickly vanishing like the anger of a mild person, disposition is slowly vanishing like the health of the healthy. The third type is called power and non-power; the name 'power' is not only for repulsion, for hardness is a power – an aptitude not to be quickly affected – and slowness is non-power, not being affected by something but rather that which cuts off having little motion. So 'power' is a name for a relative preparedness to do a thing easily or be affected with difficulty; 'non-power' is a name for a relative preparedness to do with difficulty or be affected easily. The fourth type has no name other than what was mentioned.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a classification of possible things (tawjih al-mumkinat) according to the philosophers. It begins with a definition of 'mode' (al-hal) as something that may cause the subsistence of a substrate. The author distinguishes between two ways: the mode necessitates the existence of the substrate and then becomes a mode in it, or the effect necessitates the indwelling of its cause. In both cases, no infinite regress follows. Then the terms 'subject' (al-mawdu‘) and 'substrate' (al-mahall) are defined: a substrate that is self-subsistent and sustains what subsists in it is called subject. The marginal note clarifies that the substrate is receptive, not an agent, and identifies the mode as form and the substrate as matter in this context. The main classification then divides possible into accident (exists in a subject) and substance (does not exist in a subject). Substance is further divided into form (in a substrate), matter (is a substrate), body (composed of form and matter), soul (related to bodies by governance), and intellect (not related). The marginal note on page 88 criticizes the use of 'by induction' (bil-istiqra’) in the body definition, stating that philosophers prefer a different division. The note then digresses into a classification of qualities (kayfiyyat) into four types: affective qualities (stable and unstable), states and dispositions, powers and non-powers, and quantities-specific qualities. This quality classification seems to be an explanatory comment.

الصورة
الهيولى
الجسم
النفس

الاستقراء

Key Takeaway
Substance is divided into form, matter, body, soul, and intellect; philosophers also prefer a division into material and separate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage distinguishes soul (which governs the body) from intellect (which does not). In your daily life, do you experience moments when your intellect clearly directs your actions without being swayed by your emotions or bodily desires? What might change if you consciously sought that state today?
?
Razi criticises relying on induction to define body. In your spiritual journey, do you tend to draw conclusions about deeper truths based only on what you observe? How might a conceptual analysis, like that of the philosophers, open your understanding to realities beyond the senses?
?
The soul is described as 'governing' the body. Does your life reflect that governance? Consider one habit or desire that currently overrides your sense of purpose. What concrete step can you take within the next 24 hours to let your soul—not just your impulses—lead your action?

Three Categories of Contingent Beings
Razi enumerates three categories of contingent and critiques the argument against the third.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the threefold classification of contingent beings in kalam and why Razi considers the argument against a third category weak.

Core Idea
Shared negations do not imply similarity in quiddity.

Razi begins by laying out the fundamental division of contingent beings (al-muḥdathāt) according to the mutakallimun. They are classified into three categories: what occupies space (mutaḥayyiz), what inheres in what occupies space (qā'im bil-mutaḥayyiz), and what is neither. The majority rejects the third category, but Razi will show their strongest argument is flawed. This passage sets the stage for understanding the basic ontology of kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation. Pay attention to the three categories listed.
The third category is what is neither occupying space nor inhering.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the list of three possibilities for a contingent being. Notice that the first two are accepted by all; the third is disputed.
The first two correspond to substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad) in kalam.
3
Analyse the critique
Razi presents the mutakallimun's strongest argument against the third category: it would imply equality with God. Then he dismisses it. Understand why sharing negations does not entail similarity.
This critique is crucial because it defends the possibility of a third kind of existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المحدث إما أن يكون متحيزا أو قائما بالمتحيز أو لا متحيز ولا قائم بالمتحيز والقسم الثالث قد أنكره الجمهور من المتكلمين . وأقوى مالهم فيه أنا لو فرضنا موجودا غير متحيز ولا حال فيه لسكان مساويا لذات الله تعالى فيه ، ويلزم من الاستواء فيه الاستواء فى تمام الماهية وهذا ضعيف . لأن الاشتراك فى السلوب لا يقتضى التماثل وإلا لزم تماثل المختلفات ، لأن كل مختلفين فلابد وأن يشتركا في سلب كل ما عداهما عنهما . أما المنجز فقد قال المتكامون إنه إما أن يكون قابلا للانقسام أولا يكون والأول هو الجسم والثاني هو الجوهر الفرد. وعند المعتزلة اسم الجسم لا يقع إلا على الطويل العريض العميق وعلى ما قلناه الجسم ما فيه التأليف وأقله جوهران فهذا بحث لغوى (۳) (۳) أقول الأقدمون من المتكلمين قالوا المتحيز هو الجوهر والحال فيه هو العرض ، والموجود الذى
The contingent is either occupying space (mutaḥayyiz) or subsisting in what occupies space, or neither occupying space nor subsisting in what occupies space. The third category has been denied by the majority of the mutakallimun. Their strongest argument for this is: if we assume an existent that is neither occupying space nor inhering, it would be equal to God Most High in that respect, and equality in that respect necessitates equality in total quiddity. This is weak, because sharing in negations does not entail similarity; otherwise, all different things would be similar, since any two different things must share in the negation of everything else from them. As for the contingent, the mutakallimun have said: it is either divisible or not; the first is the body, the second is the indivisible atom (jawhar fard). According to the Mu'tazila, the name 'body' applies only to that which is long, wide, and deep. According to what we have said, the body is that which has composition (ta'līf), and its minimum is two atoms. This is a linguistic discussion. (3) I say: The earlier mutakallimun said that the occupying thing is the atom (jawhar), and what inheres in it is the accident. The existent that...
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the division of contingent things (المحدثات) according to the mutakallimun (theologians). Razi states three categories: (1) that which occupies space (متحيز) — i.e., substance; (2) that which subsists in what occupies space (قائم بالمتحيز) — i.e., accident; (3) that which is neither. The majority of mutakallimun reject the third category. Their strongest argument: if there were an existent that is neither occupying space nor inhering, it would be equal to God in that respect (both are non-occupied and non-inhering). Equality in that negation would imply equality in entire quiddity, which is impossible. Razi dismisses this argument as weak: sharing in negations (سلوب) does not imply similarity, because all different things share in the negation of everything else. He then turns to the contingent (المحدث, written as 'المنجز' in OCR, likely a typo). The mutakallimun say the contingent is either divisible (body) or indivisible (the atom, جوهر فرد). He notes a terminological dispute: the Mu'tazila define body as long, wide, and deep, while others (including Razi's own school?) define body as that which has composition (تأليف), with a minimum of two atoms. This is a linguistic debate. The marginal note (3) begins to explain that the earlier mutakallimun identified the occupying thing as the atom (جوهر) and what inheres as accident, but it cuts off. This lesson thus covers the classification and the critique of the third category's impossibility.

Key Terms
المحدث
المتحيز
القائم بالمتحيز
السلوب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third category of contingent (neither occupying space nor inhering) is impossible because it would make it equal to God.
Correction: Sharing a negative attribute (like not occupying space) does not imply equality in essence, as shown by the fact that all distinct things share many negations.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi consider the mutakallimun's strongest argument for rejecting the third category of contingent to be weak?
A) Because the third category does exist.
B) Because sharing in negations does not imply similarity in quiddity.
C) Because God is not comparable to anything.
D) Because the argument is circular.

Key Takeaway
The third category of contingent (neither occupying space nor inhering) is not proven impossible by the argument from shared negation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely heard others argue that something is impossible because it would make it 'like Allah'. After studying Razi’s critique, do you see the flaw in that reasoning? When you use or accept such an argument in the future, will you stop and examine whether it relies on a weak inference from a shared negation? Commit to one situation this week where you will test a claim about Allah using this lesson’s standard.
?
The majority of mutakallimun rejected the third category based on a weak argument. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold simply because ‘most people’ or ‘most scholars’ hold them, without truly examining the evidence? This lesson calls you to think critically. What is one belief about God or His attributes that you have accepted without question, and how can you re-evaluate it using sound reasoning?

Division into Body and Atom
Razi divides contingent into body and atom, then discusses the definition of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam division of the contingent into body (divisible) and atom (indivisible), and the terminological dispute regarding the definition of body.

Core Idea
Kalam divides matter into atoms and bodies; the definition of body is contested.

After discussing the three categories, Razi now focuses on the contingent (al-muḥdath) in terms of divisibility. The mutakallimun divide it into what is divisible (body) and what is not (the indivisible atom, jawhar fard). He then notes a terminological dispute: the Mu'tazila define body by its three dimensions, while others define it by composition (ta'līf) of at least two atoms. This linguistic debate has implications for how we understand physical reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the two parts: the division into body and atom, and the definitional dispute.
The term 'al-munjaz' appears; it is likely a typo for 'al-muḥdath' (contingent).
2
Identify the division
Razi states the contingent is either divisible (body) or indivisible (atom). Understand that this is a fundamental ontological split.
The atom (jawhar fard) is the basic unit; body is composed of atoms.
3
Understand the terminological dispute
Compare the Mu'tazila definition (long, wide, deep) with the alternative (composition of at least two atoms). Reflect on why this matters.
This is a linguistic debate: what does 'body' mean in kalam?

المحدث إما أن يكون متحيزا أو قائما بالمتحيز أو لا متحيز ولا قائم بالمتحيز والقسم الثالث قد أنكره الجمهور من المتكلمين . وأقوى مالهم فيه أنا لو فرضنا موجودا غير متحيز ولا حال فيه لسكان مساويا لذات الله تعالى فيه ، ويلزم من الاستواء فيه الاستواء فى تمام الماهية وهذا ضعيف . لأن الاشتراك فى السلوب لا يقتضى التماثل وإلا لزم تماثل المختلفات ، لأن كل مختلفين فلابد وأن يشتركا في سلب كل ما عداهما عنهما . أما المنجز فقد قال المتكامون إنه إما أن يكون قابلا للانقسام أولا يكون والأول هو الجسم والثاني هو الجوهر الفرد. وعند المعتزلة اسم الجسم لا يقع إلا على الطويل العريض العميق وعلى ما قلناه الجسم ما فيه التأليف وأقله جوهران فهذا بحث لغوى (۳) (۳) أقول الأقدمون من المتكلمين قالوا المتحيز هو الجوهر والحال فيه هو العرض ، والموجود الذى

General Knowledge: Known from One Aspect, Unknown from Another
Razi explains that general knowledge is not a distinct type but a single object with two aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why general knowledge is not a distinct type of knowledge but simply a single object considered from two aspects.

Core Idea
General knowledge is not a special type but a single thing seen from two sides.

This lesson tackles a common confusion in Islamic epistemology: the nature of general knowledge (al-`ilm al-ijmali). Imam al-Razi clarifies that when we say something is known generally, we are not referring to a special kind of knowledge. Instead, we are looking at the same object from two different perspectives—one known and one unknown. Understanding this distinction helps avoid mistaking a conceptual illusion for a real division of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic question and the comment. Notice the structure: a statement, then an explanation of the mistaken view, then a clarification from the author.
2
Identify the two aspects
The passage speaks of a thing known from one aspect and unknown from another. Find what aspect is known and what is unknown, and note that they are distinct.
3
Understand the mistake
Why do people think general knowledge is a type? Because both aspects belong to one thing. The text says 'لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد ظن أن العلم الجملي نوع يغاير العلم التفصيلي.'
4
Grasp the author's point
In the comment, Razi highlights that the author acknowledges that the thing containing both aspects is distinct from the aspects themselves. This reinforces that the distinction is not between two types of knowledge but between aspects of one thing.
5
Reflect
Consider how this relates to the earlier refutation about conception not being acquired.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: المعلوم على سبيل الجملة معلوم من وجه مجهول من وجه. والوجهان متغايران، فالوجه المعلوم لا إجمال فيه، والوجه المجهول غير معلوم البتة، لكن لما اجتمعا في شيء واحد ظن أن العلم الجملي نوع يغاير العلم التفصيلي.

(2) أقول: اعترف ههنا بأن الشيء المعلوم من وجه والمجهول من وجه يغاير الوجهين، وهذا ما ذكرته في صدر الكتاب عند إبطال قوله التصور ليس مكتسبا، ومطلوبه ههنا بيان تغاير الوجهين، لكن حصل منه وجوب تغاير ما اجتمع فيه الوجهان والوجهين.
Question: The known in a general way is known from one aspect and unknown from another aspect. And the two aspects are distinct, so the known aspect has no generality in it, and the unknown aspect is not known at all, but when they come together in a single thing, it was thought that general knowledge is a type distinct from detailed knowledge.

(2) I say: He acknowledges here that the thing known from one aspect and unknown from another aspect is distinct from the two aspects, and this is what I mentioned in the beginning of the book when refuting his statement that conception is not acquired, and his goal here is to show the distinctness of the two aspects, but from him arises the necessity of the distinctness of what contains the two aspects and the two aspects themselves.
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage is a short question (mas'alah) about the nature of general knowledge (al-`ilm al-ijmali). Razi states that something known in a general way is known from one aspect and unknown from another. The two aspects are distinct: the known aspect has no generality (ijmal) in it—it is fully determinate—and the unknown aspect is simply not known. The mistake occurs because both aspects belong to one thing, leading some to think that general knowledge is a separate type of knowledge distinct from detailed knowledge (al-`ilm al-tafsili). In the comment, Razi points out that the author (likely himself or the book's author) acknowledges that the thing known from one aspect and unknown from another is distinct from the two aspects themselves—a point he already made earlier. The goal is to show the distinctness of the aspects, but it also implies the distinctness of the thing that contains them.

Key Terms
علم جملي
علم تفصيلي
وجه
إجمال
تصور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: General knowledge is a special type of knowledge with its own nature, distinct from detailed knowledge.
Correction: General knowledge is not a separate type; it is the same object considered from two aspects (known and unknown).

Checkpoint
Why do some mistakenly think that general knowledge is a distinct type?
A) Because the two aspects merge into one.
B) Because the known aspect is not fully determinate.
C) Because the two aspects are found in a single thing.
D) Because the unknown aspect is still partially known.

Key Takeaway
General knowledge is not a separate type but one object known from one aspect and unknown from another.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about Islam that you consider 'general knowledge.' In what way are you ignoring the unknown aspects of that same belief? Will you today seek out what you don't know about it, or are you content with generalities?
?
You often say 'I know' about certain theological claims. But according to this lesson, could it be that you only know a part and mistake that for full knowledge? What specific area of your faith are you willing to admit you are ignorant of?
?
The passage says people mistakenly think general knowledge is a separate type. Where in your own learning have you assumed that a partial understanding is sufficient? Will you commit to moving from general knowledge to detailed knowledge in at least one topic this week?

The Incompatibility of Nazar with Knowledge of the Implication
An argument that nazar is incompatible with knowledge of the implication and requires evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why rational inference (nazar) cannot coexist with knowing the conclusion (madlul) and why it depends on prior knowledge of the evidence (dalil).

Core Idea
Nazar and knowing the conclusion cannot coexist; you must know the premise first.

Imam al-Razi presents a concise argument about the nature of rational inference. He asserts that the act of considering evidence (nazar) cannot occur simultaneously with knowledge of the conclusion. Moreover, inference depends on already knowing the evidence. To illustrate, he contrasts contradictory beliefs about the body: believing it is eternal conflicts with believing it originated in time, and each belief requires different prior knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Let the structure sink in before analyzing.
Notice the two parts separated by 'wa li'anna'.
2
Identify the two main claims
The first part states the thesis about nazar, madlul, and dalil. The second part gives an example using belief in the body's eternity and temporal origination.
The example is meant to reinforce the logical relationship.
3
Understand key terms: nazar, dalil, madlul
Nazar is rational inference or discursive thought. Dalil is the evidence or premise. Madlul is the conclusion or implication. The argument says nazar and knowledge of madlul cannot happen at the same time.
This is a foundational principle in Islamic logic.
4
Reflect on the incompatibility
Why can't you be inferring and already know the conclusion? Because inference is a process of seeking knowledge; once you have it, the process stops.
Think of it like searching for a lost item – you cannot search and have already found it simultaneously.
5
Connect the example to the principle
Believing the body is eternal contradicts believing it originated. Each belief requires different prior knowledge (e.g., essence of body, non-existence, temporality), showing that beliefs are conditioned by what is known beforehand.
This mirrors how nazar is conditioned by knowledge of the dalil.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن النظر مناف للعلم بالمدلول ومشروط بالعلم بالدليل، ولأن اعتقاد قدم الجسم يضاد اعتقاد حدوثه ومشروط بالعلم بماهية الجسم وماهية العدم والحدوث.
We have that consideration (nazar) is incompatible with knowledge of the implication (madlul) and is conditioned by knowledge of the evidence (dalil), and because the belief in the eternity of the body contradicts the belief in its temporal origination and is conditioned by knowledge of the essence of the body and the essence of non-existence and temporal origination.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is a brief argument (lana anna) stating two points. First, consideration (nazar) – which here means rational inference or discursive thought – is incompatible with (munaaf) knowledge of the implication (madlul). In other words, while one is engaged in inference, one does not yet know the conclusion; once the conclusion is known, the inference ceases. Additionally, nazar is conditioned by (mashrut bi) knowledge of the evidence (dalil); one must already know the premise to infer. Second, the argument uses the example of contradictory beliefs about the body: belief in its eternity contradicts belief in its temporal origination, and each belief is conditioned by knowledge of different things (the body's essence, non-existence, etc.). This reinforces that conditions differ and thus the beliefs differ.

Key Terms

مناف
علم
مدلول
دليل
اعتقاد
قدم
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That nazar (inference) is the same as knowledge; they can happen at the same time.
Correction: The text explicitly states they are incompatible (munaaf): you cannot be in the process of inferring and simultaneously possess the knowledge you are seeking.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what is nazar (consideration) incompatible with?
A) Knowledge of the evidence (dalil).
B) Knowledge of the implication (madlul).
C) Belief in the eternity of the body.
D) The impossibility of contradictory beliefs.

Key Takeaway
Rational inference (nazar) and direct knowledge of the conclusion are mutually exclusive, and inference always requires prior knowledge of the evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek knowledge about your faith, do you actually engage in nazar—examining evidence—or do you rely on inherited certainty? Which pathway dominates your heart?
?
If nazar requires prior knowledge of the evidence, what evidence for God's existence have you truly studied? Are you willing to set aside your comfort and examine the proofs from scratch?
?
How often do you jump to conclusions about religious matters without first mastering the evidence? Will you commit today to learning one essential dalil before drawing final conclusions?

All Sciences Are Necessary: Razi's Argument
Razi argues that all sciences are necessary, clarifying the meaning of 'daruri'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that all sciences are necessary in the sense of being certain (yaqini), and how he distinguishes this technical meaning from other senses of 'daruri'.

Core Idea
Daruri here means certain (yaqini), not merely self-evident or sensory.

This lesson examines a concise but crucial argument from Razi: all sciences are necessary. The term 'daruri' is often misunderstood, so Razi clarifies that he means certainty (yaqini), not just self-evidence or sensory knowledge. Understanding this distinction is key to following the proof and its implications for Islamic epistemology.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that it consists of two parts: the main argument and a clarifying comment.
Pay attention to the logical structure of the proof.
2
Analyze the proof
Identify the two cases: sciences are either necessary from the start or necessarily implied by something necessary. If any possibility of non-implication remained, it would not be science.
This shows that science requires absolute certainty.
3
Understand the clarification
Razi explicitly says that 'daruri' here means 'yaqini' (certain), not 'badihi' (self-evident) or 'hissi' (sensory). This avoids a common misunderstanding.
He also notes that the author earlier called sensory things 'daruriyyat', but here uses the term more broadly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة: العلوم كلها ضرورية، لأنها إما ضرورية ابتداء أو لازمة عنها لزوما ضروريا، فإنه إن بقي احتمال عدم اللزوم ولو على أبعد الوجوه لم يكن علم، وإذا كان كذلك كانت بأسرها ضرورية.

(2) أقول: يريد الضروري ههنا اليقيني لا البديهي ولا المحسوس وحده، فإنه قال من قبل المحسوسات هو الضروريات، وقد سمى كل اليقينيات ضروريا موافقة لقول أبي الحسن الأشعري.
Question: All sciences are necessary, because they are either necessary from the start or follow from them with a necessary following; for if the probability of non-following remained, even in the most remote ways, it would not be science; and if that is the case, then all of them are necessary.

(2) I say: He means by 'necessary' here the certain (yaqini), not the self-evident (badihi) nor the sensory alone; for he said earlier that the sensory things are the necessary things, and he called all certain things necessary in agreement with Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is a short question asserting that all sciences (al-`ulum) are necessary (daruriyya). The proof: sciences are either immediately necessary or necessarily implied by something necessary. If any possibility of non-implication remained, even remotely, it would not be science (because science requires certainty). Therefore, all sciences are necessary. In the comment, Razi clarifies that 'daruri' here means 'yaqini' (certain), not just 'badihi' (self-evident) or 'hissi' (sensory). He notes that the author earlier called sensory things 'daruriyyat' and, following al-Ash'ari, used 'daruri' for all certain knowledge.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'daruri' means only self-evident or sensory knowledge.
Correction: In this passage, Razi specifies that 'daruri' means certain (yaqini), encompassing both self-evident and acquired certain knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the author mean by 'daruri' (necessary) in this passage?
A) Self-evident (badihi).
B) Sensory (hissi).
C) Certain (yaqini).
D) Acquired (kasbi).

Key Takeaway
Razi defines 'daruri' in this context as synonymous with 'yaqini' (certain), not merely self-evident or sensory knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever considered your Islamic beliefs as 'daruri' (certain) in the Razi's sense of yaqini? If you truly believed them to be as certain as sensory knowledge, how would your daily actions change?
?
When you learn a new Islamic ruling from a text or scholar, do you ever entertain the possibility that it might not be true? Razi argues that if any possibility of non-following remains, it is not science. How can you eliminate that possibility in your own learning?
?
If derived knowledge can be just as necessary as immediate knowledge, what does that mean for your responsibility to seek the premises? Are you comfortable relying on blind faith, or must you trace every belief back to a certain foundation?

Knowledge of Principle Cannot Be Acquired If Branch Is Necessary
A notice on the impossibility of mixing acquired and necessary knowledge in a premise-conclusion relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is impossible for the principle (asl) to be known through acquisition while the branch (far') is known necessarily, and how Razi qualifies this rule.

Core Idea
Necessity in the branch demands necessity in the principle.

This short but powerful notice establishes a logical constraint on how knowledge can be structured. Before you can claim that a conclusion is necessary, the premises it depends on must themselves be certain. The passage also contains an important clarification from Imam Razi about what kinds of principles this rule applies to.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the logical flow sink in.
Notice the if-then structure and the term 'khalaf' (absurd).
2
Identify the main claim and its proof
The notice states a point of agreement: it is impossible for the principle to be acquired while the branch is necessary. The proof is by contradiction: if the principle could be doubted, doubt would spread to the branch, making the necessary non-necessary, which is absurd.
3
Understand Razi's clarification
Razi distinguishes between two meanings of 'principle'. If it means affirmations (tasdiqat) that other affirmations depend on, the rule holds. But if it includes conceptions (tasawwurat), then the rule is not necessarily true because conceptions can be acquired while the affirmations depending on them are necessary.
This shows Razi's precision in defining terms.
4
Reflect on the logical implication
Ask yourself: why is this rule important for theology? It ensures that chains of reasoning do not rest on uncertain foundations at any point where necessity is claimed.
This connects to the broader methodology of kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: اتفقوا على أنه لا يجوز أن يكون العلم بالأصل كسبيا وبالفرع ضروريا، وإلا فعند وقوع الشك يحصل الشك في الفرع فيصير الضروري غير ضروري، هذا خلف.

(3) أقول: إن كان المراد من الأصل التصديقات التي يتوقف عليها تصديقات فهو حق، وإن كان المراد أعم من ذلك ففيه نظر، لأن التصورات ممكن أن تكون كسبية والتصديقات المترتبة عليها ضرورية.
Notice: They agreed that it is not permissible for the knowledge of the principle (asl) to be acquired and the knowledge of the branch (far') to be necessary; otherwise, when doubt occurs, doubt would occur in the branch, and then the necessary would become non-necessary, which is absurd.

(3) I say: If by 'principle' he means the affirmations (tasdiqat) upon which affirmations depend, then it is correct; but if he means more general than that, then there is consideration, because conceptions (tasawwurat) can be acquired and the affirmations dependent upon them are necessary.
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This is a notice (tanbih) stating a point of agreement: it is impossible for knowledge of the principle (asl) to be acquired (kasbi) while knowledge of the branch (far') is necessary (daruri). The reason is that if the principle could be doubted, then doubt would extend to the branch, undermining its necessity—this is absurd (khalaf). In the comment, Razi clarifies that if 'principle' means the affirmations (tasdiqat) that other affirmations depend on, then the rule holds. But if it means more generally, including conceptions (tasawwurat), then there is room for consideration because conceptions can be acquired while the affirmations depending on them are necessary.

Key Terms
أصل
فرع
كسبي
ضروري
تصديقات
تصورات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the rule applies to all principles without distinction, including conceptual definitions.
Correction: Razi clarifies that the rule only applies when the principle is an affirmation (tasdiq) that another affirmation depends on. Conceptions (tasawwurat) can be acquired while the affirmations derived from them are necessary.

Checkpoint
What would happen if knowledge of the principle is acquired and the branch is necessary?
A) The branch would still be necessary.
B) Doubt in the principle would cause doubt in the branch.
C) The principle would become necessary.
D) The branch would become acquired.

Key Takeaway
Necessity in a conclusion requires necessity in its premise, but this only holds when the premise is an affirmation, not a conception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a core belief you hold as absolutely certain—like the existence of God or the truth of a key doctrine. What foundation (principle) did you use to reach that belief? Is that foundation itself certain, or is it something you acquired from education or culture? If it is acquired, then according to this lesson, your certainty is logically unstable. Will you examine that foundation today and seek a necessary basis for it?
?
Have you ever experienced doubt about a religious truth that you once considered self-evident? Perhaps you realized that your certainty was built on an assumption you had not verified. This lesson warns that doubt in the principle inevitably corrupts the branch. What principle in your faith have you neglected to secure? What specific step will you take this week to ground it properly?
?
Razi distinguishes between conceptions (tasawwurat) and affirmations (tasdiqat). Do you sometimes confuse a mere concept—like 'mercy of Allah'—with a deeper affirmation about it that should be necessary? How can you transform a beautiful concept into a firm, necessary judgment that anchors your life? Name one concept you will work to convert into a certain affirmation today.

Why Speculative Reasoning Opposes Direct Knowledge
Imam Razi presents a proof that nazar is incompatible with knowledge of the conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why speculative reasoning (nazar) is incompatible with direct knowledge of the conclusion and requires prior knowledge of the evidence.

Core Idea
Speculative reasoning cannot coexist with direct knowledge; it always builds on prior evidence.

Imam Razi presents a foundational argument about the nature of speculative reasoning. Before we can engage in theological investigation, we must understand how reasoning relates to knowledge. This passage clarifies that nazar both opposes knowing the conclusion directly and depends on knowing the evidence first.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the structure: two claims followed by an analogy.
The footnote (1) indicates a possible citation or variant.
2
Identify the two conditions
First, nazar opposes knowledge of the conclusion (if you already know, why reason?). Second, nazar is conditional upon knowledge of the evidence (you need prior evidence to reason).
These are two distinct logical points.
3
Reflect on the analogy
The analogy of belief in pre-eternity vs. temporal origination illustrates the mutual exclusivity and conditional nature of such beliefs, mirroring the relationship between nazar and knowledge.
Understanding the analogy deepens appreciation of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لنا أن النظر مناف للعلم بالمدلول ومشروط بالعلم بالدليل ، ولان اعتقاد قدم الجسم يضاد اعتقاد حدوثه ومشروط بالعلم بماهية الجسم وماهية العدم والحدوث (1)
We hold that speculative reasoning (النظر) is incompatible with knowledge of the conclusion (المدلول) and is conditional upon knowledge of the evidence (الدليل), and because the belief in the pre-eternity of the body (قدم الجسم) contradicts the belief in its temporal origination (حدوثه) and is conditional upon knowledge of the essence of the body, the essence of non-existence, and temporal origination (1).
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage presents a proof establishing the relationship between speculative reasoning (nazar) and knowledge. The author first states two claims: (1) nazar is incompatible with knowledge of the conclusion, meaning that if one already has direct knowledge, reasoning is unnecessary; (2) nazar requires prior knowledge of the evidence. Then he provides an analogy: belief that the body is pre-eternal contradicts belief that it is temporally originated, and each such belief depends on specific knowledge (of the body's essence, non-existence, and origination). The argument demonstrates that nazar and knowledge are distinct and have conditions.

Key Terms
النظر
العلم
المدلول
الدليل
قدم الجسم
حدوث الجسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that nazar and knowledge can coexist simultaneously in the same respect.
Correction: The passage shows they are mutually exclusive: if you have direct knowledge of something, reasoning about it is unnecessary and opposed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is required for speculative reasoning (النظر)?
A) Knowledge of the conclusion
B) Knowledge of the evidence
C) Belief in the pre-eternity of the body
D) Direct sensory perception

Key Takeaway
Speculative reasoning (nazar) is conditional upon prior knowledge of the evidence and cannot coexist with direct knowledge of the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in theological reasoning, do you first ensure that your premises are firmly rooted in revelation? Or do you sometimes proceed with speculation, trusting your intellect over the evidence of the Quran and Sunnah? Will you commit to verifying every premise before constructing an argument?
?
Imagine you are convinced of a certain belief about Allah. Did that conviction come from direct knowledge (clear text) or from your own reasoning? If from reasoning, does that reasoning depend on prior knowledge that you have verified? How will you guard against building your faith on shaky premises that contradict revealed truths?
?
This passage shows that belief in the pre-eternity of the body contradicts belief in its temporal origination. Are there any beliefs you hold that, upon honest reflection, are mutually exclusive with the clear teachings of Islam? What will you do to resolve such contradictions in your heart and mind?

The Intellect as Universal Self-Evident Knowledge
Imam Razi presents the well-known view and supports it with arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial definition of the intellect as the basis of obligation and the arguments that lead to identifying it with universal self-evident knowledge.

Core Idea
The intellect is not sensory or theoretical knowledge but universal self-evident knowledge.

Imam Razi begins by presenting the widely accepted view that the intellect is the knowledge of necessary obligations and impossibilities. He then supports this with a series of logical arguments, eliminating alternatives. This lesson traces that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of the flow. Then read the translation to understand the meaning.
Notice the structure: definition, argument, exclusions, conclusion.
2
Identify the main claim
What is the well-known definition of the intellect that the passage begins with? Look for the phrase 'المشهور أن العقل...'.
3
Understand the argument against sensory knowledge
Why does the author say the intellect cannot be knowledge of sensibles? Find the reason given.
Compare with animals and the insane.
4
Understand the argument against theoretical knowledge
Why can't the intellect be theoretical knowledge? Note the circularity argument.
5
See the conclusion
What does the author conclude the intellect is? Paraphrase the final sentence.
The conclusion leads to the objection in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : في بيان العقل الذي هو مناط التكليف المشهور أن العقل الذى هو مناط التكليف هو العلم بوجوب الواجبات واستحالة المستحيلات ، لأن العقل لو لم يكن من قبيل العلوم يصح انفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر لكن محال لاستحالة أن يوجد عاقل لا يعلم شيئا البتة أو عالم بجميع الأشياء ولا يكون اقلا ، وليس هو علما بالمحسوسات لحصوله في البهائم والمجانين فهو إذا علم بالأمور الكلية وليس ذلك من العلوم النظرية لأنها مشروطة بالعقل ، فلو كان العقل عبارة عنها لزم اشتراط الشيء بنفسه أوه و محال ، فهو إذاً عبارة عن علوم كلية بديهية وهو المطلوب . فقيل عليه : لم قالت إن المتغاير يقتضى جواز الا فكاك ، فإن الجوهر والعرض متلازمان وكذا العملة والمعلول سلناه لكن العقل قد ينفك من العلم كما في حق النائم أو اليقظان الذى لا يكون مستحضرا لشيء من وجوب الواجبات واستحالة المستحيلات، وعند هذا ظهر أن العقل غريزة يلزمها هذه العلوم البديهية عند سلامة صحة الحواس (2)
Issue: On explaining the intellect which is the basis of obligation. The well-known view is that the intellect which is the basis of obligation is knowledge of the necessity of obligations and the impossibility of impossibilities. Because if the intellect were not of the category of knowledge, it would be possible for one to be separate from the other, but that is impossible, because it is impossible for a rational being to exist who knows nothing at all or who knows all things yet is not rational. And it is not knowledge of sensibles, because that occurs in animals and the insane. So it is knowledge of universal matters, and that is not from theoretical sciences because they are conditional upon the intellect. So if the intellect were identical to them, it would entail self-conditioning, which is impossible. So it is universal self-evident knowledge, which is what is sought. Then it was objected: Why did you say that distinctness entails permissibility of separation? For substance and accident are inseparable, as are cause and effect. We grant it, but the intellect may be separate from knowledge, as in the case of the sleeper or the awake person who is not conscious of any of the duties or impossibilities. Upon this, it becomes clear that the intellect is a faculty (غريزة) which entails these self-evident sciences when the senses are sound (2).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage investigates the nature of 'aql (intellect) that is the basis (manāṭ) for religious obligation (taklīf). It first presents the well-known (mashhūr) view: the intellect is identical to knowledge of necessary obligations and impossible things. Several arguments are given: (1) if intellect were not a kind of knowledge, it could be separate from knowledge, but that is impossible because a rational being must know something; (2) it is not sensory knowledge (because animals have that); (3) it is knowledge of universals, but not theoretical knowledge because theory presupposes intellect. The argument concludes that intellect is self-evident universal knowledge. Then an objection is raised: distinct things are not always separable (e.g., substance and accident are inseparable). The objection is accepted, but it is noted that intellect can be separate from actual knowledge (e.g., in sleep). Therefore, the correct conclusion is that the intellect is a faculty (gharīza) that produces these self-evident sciences when the senses are sound.

Key Terms
العقل
مناط التكليف
العلم بوجوب الواجبات
استحالة المستحيلات
العلوم البديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Intellect is the same as theoretical knowledge learned through reasoning.
Correction: Imam Razi argues that theoretical knowledge presupposes intellect, so intellect cannot be identical to it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why cannot the intellect be sensory knowledge?
A) Because sensory knowledge is not universal
B) Because sensory knowledge occurs in animals and the insane
C) Because sensory knowledge is not self-evident
D) Because sensory knowledge is not necessary

Key Takeaway
The intellect is defined as universal self-evident knowledge, not sensory or theoretical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever act against what your innate intellect tells you is right, and what does that say about your sincerity in using the intellect Allah gave you as your basis of accountability?
?
If the intellect is universal and self-evident, why do you sometimes claim you 'didn't know' something you clearly did? How can you better honour this innate knowledge?
?
When you prioritize desires over your innate sense of moral obligation, are you not effectively denying the very intellect that makes you human? What step will you take today to realign your actions with that inner knowledge?

Refining the Intellect as a Faculty
Imam Razi addresses an objection and refines the definition of the intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objection forces a refined understanding of the intellect as a faculty rather than simply knowledge.

Core Idea
The intellect is a faculty that produces self-evident knowledge when the senses are sound.

After establishing the intellect as self-evident knowledge, an objection arises: distinct things are not always separable. This leads to a crucial refinement: the intellect is actually a faculty (gharīza) that gives rise to such knowledge under proper conditions. This lesson examines that objection and the final definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage containing the objection and the response. Notice how the objector questions the logic of separability.
The objector gives counterexamples of inseparable distinct things.
2
Understand the counterexample
The objector says that substance and accident are always together, yet distinct. How does the author respond?
The author concedes the point but then provides a counterexample from sleep.
3
See the final conclusion
After accepting the objection, what new definition does the author offer? Look for the phrase 'فعل هذا ظهر أن العقل غريزة...'.
This is the refined definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة : في بيان العقل الذي هو مناط التكليف المشهور أن العقل الذى هو مناط التكليف هو العلم بوجوب الواجبات واستحالة المستحيلات ، لأن العقل لو لم يكن من قبيل العلوم يصح انفكاك أحدهما عن الآخر لكن محال لاستحالة أن يوجد عاقل لا يعلم شيئا البتة أو عالم بجميع الأشياء ولا يكون اقلا ، وليس هو علما بالمحسوسات لحصوله في البهائم والمجانين فهو إذا علم بالأمور الكلية وليس ذلك من العلوم النظرية لأنها مشروطة بالعقل ، فلو كان العقل عبارة عنها لزم اشتراط الشيء بنفسه أوه و محال ، فهو إذاً عبارة عن علوم كلية بديهية وهو المطلوب . فقيل عليه : لم قالت إن المتغاير يقتضى جواز الا فكاك ، فإن الجوهر والعرض متلازمان وكذا العملة والمعلول سلناه لكن العقل قد ينفك من العلم كما في حق النائم أو اليقظان الذى لا يكون مستحضرا لشيء من وجوب الواجبات واستحالة المستحيلات، وعند هذا ظهر أن العقل غريزة يلزمها هذه العلوم البديهية عند سلامة صحة الحواس (2)
Issue: On explaining the intellect which is the basis of obligation. The well-known view is that the intellect which is the basis of obligation is knowledge of the necessity of obligations and the impossibility of impossibilities. Because if the intellect were not of the category of knowledge, it would be possible for one to be separate from the other, but that is impossible, because it is impossible for a rational being to exist who knows nothing at all or who knows all things yet is not rational. And it is not knowledge of sensibles, because that occurs in animals and the insane. So it is knowledge of universal matters, and that is not from theoretical sciences because they are conditional upon the intellect. So if the intellect were identical to them, it would entail self-conditioning, which is impossible. So it is universal self-evident knowledge, which is what is sought. Then it was objected: Why did you say that distinctness entails permissibility of separation? For substance and accident are inseparable, as are cause and effect. We grant it, but the intellect may be separate from knowledge, as in the case of the sleeper or the awake person who is not conscious of any of the duties or impossibilities. Upon this, it becomes clear that the intellect is a faculty (غريزة) which entails these self-evident sciences when the senses are sound (2).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage investigates the nature of 'aql (intellect) that is the basis (manāṭ) for religious obligation (taklīf). It first presents the well-known (mashhūr) view: the intellect is identical to knowledge of necessary obligations and impossible things. Several arguments are given: (1) if intellect were not a kind of knowledge, it could be separate from knowledge, but that is impossible because a rational being must know something; (2) it is not sensory knowledge (because animals have that); (3) it is knowledge of universals, but not theoretical knowledge because theory presupposes intellect. The argument concludes that intellect is self-evident universal knowledge. Then an objection is raised: distinct things are not always separable (e.g., substance and accident are inseparable). The objection is accepted, but it is noted that intellect can be separate from actual knowledge (e.g., in sleep). Therefore, the correct conclusion is that the intellect is a faculty (gharīza) that produces these self-evident sciences when the senses are sound.

Key Terms
غريزة
العلوم البديهية
العقل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect is simply the set of self-evident propositions.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the intellect is a faculty that produces those propositions, not the propositions themselves.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude the intellect is after the objection?
A) A set of theoretical sciences
B) A faculty that entails self-evident knowledge when senses are sound
C) Identical to self-evident knowledge
D) The sum total of sensory knowledge

Key Takeaway
The intellect is a faculty that produces self-evident knowledge under proper sensory conditions, not the knowledge itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are asleep, is your intellect still present? How does the awareness that your intellect is a dormant faculty, not constant knowledge, change how you view the moments you are awake? Will you now guard your waking hours more carefully to produce self-evident truths?
?
The objector used substance and accident as inseparable distinct things. In your own spiritual life, what is distinct from your faith yet inseparable? How does that analogy help you see that your intellect is not your knowledge of God but the faculty to attain it? Are you feeding that faculty?
?
If the intellect only produces knowledge when senses are sound, what does that say about the state of your heart when it is clouded by sin or heedlessness? How can you ensure your senses remain 'sound' to receive the self-evident truths of obligation and impossibility?

The Composition of Bodies: Composite and Simple
Imam Razi presents two categories of bodies and their essential property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's categorization of bodies into composite and simple, and the rational necessity of divisibility of bodies.

Core Idea
Every perceptible body is divisible into parts.

This lesson initiates the discussion on bodies. Imam Razi divides the study into constituents and accidents. He begins with a foundational issue: all composite bodies are composed of parts, and even simple perceptible bodies are divisible. This sets the stage for kalam arguments about atomism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Imam Razi introduces the topic and the specific issue.
The passage uses the term 'لاشك' to assert certainty.
2
Identify the two categories
The passage distinguishes between 'composite bodies' and 'simple perceptible bodies'. Note the claim about each.
Composite bodies are explicitly stated to be composed of parts; simple bodies are stated to be divisible.
3
Reflect on why divisibility matters
Think about why this distinction is important for kalam. It relates to the concept of atoms (jawahir fard) and the possibility of division.
This connects to arguments about the composition of the universe.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأجسام فالنظر فى مقوماتها وعوارضها . أما المقومات ففيها مسائل . ( مسألة : لاشك فى تركب الأجسام المركبة عن الأجزاء أما البسيط المحسوس فلاشك أنه قابل للانقسام ) الثاني ، بل الممكن إذا حصل مع ترجيح أحد الطرفين وجب حصول ذلك الطرف وجودا كان أو عدما ، إلا ما كان الطرفان متساويين في النسبة إلى ماهيته. وقوله : شرط الجوهر محتاج إلى انحصار الشرائط فيه فإن الجوهر قابل للعرض فقط وربما يحتاج فاعله إلى وجود شرط آخر ، فإن الشمس فاعلة لإضاءة وجه الأرض، وشرطه المحاذاة فإنها إن زالت صار وجه الأرض غير مضيء وإن كان القابل والفاعل موجودين ، وباقى الكلام ظاهر .
As for bodies, the consideration is of their constituents and accidents. As for constituents, there are issues. Issue: There is no doubt that composite bodies are composed of parts; as for the simple perceptible body, there is no doubt that it is divisible. Second: Rather, the possible, when it occurs with a preponderance of one of the two sides, the occurrence of that side becomes necessary, whether existence or non-existence, except when the two sides are equal in their relation to its quiddity. And his statement: the condition of the substance requires the restriction of conditions to it, for the substance is only receptive to accident; perhaps its agent needs the existence of another condition, for the sun is the agent of lighting the earth's surface, and its condition is alignment; if it disappears, the earth's surface becomes non-illuminated even if the receptive and agent exist. And the rest of the statement is clear.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage begins a new topic on bodies. Imam Razi states the division of bodies into composite and simple, and affirms that composite bodies are made of parts, and simple perceptible bodies are divisible. Then a second statement discusses the principle of possibility: if a possible thing has a preponderance (tarjih) for one side (existence or non-existence), that side becomes necessary (wajib), unless both sides are equal. Then there is a remark about the condition of substance: it is only receptive to accidents, but sometimes additional conditions are needed for the agent, like the sun and alignment. This is a basic principle of causality in kalam.

Key Terms
تركيب الأجسام
البسيط المحسوس
قابل للانقسام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Imam Razi assert about simple perceptible bodies?
A) They are indivisible.
B) They are composed of parts.
C) They are divisible.
D) They are simple in reality.

Key Takeaway
All bodies, whether composite or simple in perception, are divisible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You look at your hand—it seems one unified thing. But this lesson says even the simplest perceptible body is divisible. If everything you own and even your own body is merely a collection of divisible parts, what are you really holding onto? How does this change what you consider 'yours'?
?
You trust your senses to tell you what is real—but your senses see the body as one piece. Imam Razi taught that reason reveals division. Where else in your life does reason contradict your senses? Will you follow your perception or your intellect?

The Principle of Preponderance: When a Possible Becomes Necessary
Imam Razi states the principle of preponderance for possible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalam rule that a possible thing with a preponderance of one side becomes necessary, and the exception when both sides are equal.

Core Idea
A possible with preponderance is necessary.

This lesson introduces a crucial logical principle: if a possible thing has a stronger reason (tarjih) for existence or non-existence, then that side becomes necessary. However, if both sides are equally balanced relative to its essence, it remains merely possible. This principle underlies many kalam arguments about causality and contingency.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: it is a second proposition ('الثاني').
2
Distinguish the three scenarios
The principle covers two cases: preponderance for existence or non-existence (both lead to necessity), and the exception when both sides are equal (remains possible).
Focus on the phrase 'إلا ما كان الطرفان متساويين'.
3
Consider the significance
Think about how this principle is used to argue for a preponderating cause for the universe, leading to the existence of God.
This is a cornerstone of the kalam cosmological argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الأجسام فالنظر فى مقوماتها وعوارضها . أما المقومات ففيها مسائل . ( مسألة : لاشك فى تركب الأجسام المركبة عن الأجزاء أما البسيط المحسوس فلاشك أنه قابل للانقسام ) الثاني ، بل الممكن إذا حصل مع ترجيح أحد الطرفين وجب حصول ذلك الطرف وجودا كان أو عدما ، إلا ما كان الطرفان متساويين في النسبة إلى ماهيته. وقوله : شرط الجوهر محتاج إلى انحصار الشرائط فيه فإن الجوهر قابل للعرض فقط وربما يحتاج فاعله إلى وجود شرط آخر ، فإن الشمس فاعلة لإضاءة وجه الأرض، وشرطه المحاذاة فإنها إن زالت صار وجه الأرض غير مضيء وإن كان القابل والفاعل موجودين ، وباقى الكلام ظاهر .
As for bodies, the consideration is of their constituents and accidents. As for constituents, there are issues. Issue: There is no doubt that composite bodies are composed of parts; as for the simple perceptible body, there is no doubt that it is divisible. Second: Rather, the possible, when it occurs with a preponderance of one of the two sides, the occurrence of that side becomes necessary, whether existence or non-existence, except when the two sides are equal in their relation to its quiddity. And his statement: the condition of the substance requires the restriction of conditions to it, for the substance is only receptive to accident; perhaps its agent needs the existence of another condition, for the sun is the agent of lighting the earth's surface, and its condition is alignment; if it disappears, the earth's surface becomes non-illuminated even if the receptive and agent exist. And the rest of the statement is clear.

Imam Razi states the division of bodies into composite and simple, and affirms that composite bodies are made of parts, and simple perceptible bodies are divisible. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This principle implies determinism — that everything is predetermined.
Correction: The principle only applies when a preponderance exists; it does not deny free will or divine choice. It is about logical consequence from a cause.

Checkpoint
What happens when a possible thing has a preponderance for existence?
A) It remains possible.
B) It becomes necessary.
C) It becomes impossible.
D) It becomes contingent.

Key Takeaway
A possible thing that has a preponderance for existence or non-existence becomes necessary in that respect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The principle says that when there is a preponderant cause, a possible becomes necessary. In your own life, what preponderant causes are tilting your heart toward or away from Allah? If you are honest, which direction are you being pulled right now? What would it take for you to strengthen the cause that leads to obedience, making it necessary for you to act?
?
Imam Razi excludes the case when both sides are equal. Are there areas in your faith where you are 'equal'—neither firm in belief nor in disbelief, but indifferent? Is indifference itself a preponderance toward neglect? What will tip the scale for you today?
?
Your own existence is a possible thing—it could have been otherwise. What preponderant cause keeps you existing every moment? Does remembering that your being is not self-sufficient make your heart turn in gratitude to the One who weights your existence toward life? If not, what is blocking that realization?

The Condition of Substance: Causality and External Conditions
Imam Razi clarifies that agents may need extra conditions beyond the receptive substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that a substance is receptive to accidents, but its agent may require additional conditions for effect, illustrated by the example of the sun.

Core Idea
Agents often need external conditions to produce their effects.

Imam Razi comments on another scholar's statement about the condition of substance. He explains that a substance (jawhar) is only receptive to accidents; however, the agent that acts upon it may need additional conditions to be present. For instance, the sun causes light on the earth, but only if there is direct alignment; when alignment ceases, the light disappears even though the agent and receiver still exist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the point about substance and the example about the sun.
2
Understand the point about substance
The substance is only receptive to accidents (qabil li-l-‘arad). That is its condition.
3
Analyze the sun example
The sun is the agent, earth is the receptive substance. Additional condition of alignment is needed for light to appear. When alignment is absent, light does not occur even though sun and earth exist.
This illustrates that causality often involves multiple conditions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

What happens to illumination if the alignment condition is absent?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on conditions like health, time, or opportunity to fulfill your religious duties. When a condition is missing, do you use it as an excuse to abandon the act entirely, or do you seek to restore the condition? How does this lesson challenge your tendency to expect results without ensuring the necessary conditions?
?
The sun and earth exist, but without alignment there is no light. What 'alignment' might be missing in your relationship with Allah? Are you content with just having the capacity (fitrah) and the means (tawfeeq), while neglecting the specific conditions like sincere intention and consistent action that bring about spiritual illumination?
