Critique of the Second Way
Razi criticizes the second way as rhetorical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Razi considers the second way rhetorical and insufficient to prove a single Maker for the whole world.

Core Idea
The second way proves each part needs a cause, but not that the whole needs a single cause.

In this passage, Razi evaluates an argument (the second way) for the existence of a Maker. He acknowledges it shows each part's dependency but argues it falls short of establishing a single cause for the entire universe. This distinction is crucial in kalam methodology for building sound proofs.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and translation. Note how Razi distinguishes between proving each part's need and the whole's need.
2
Identify the criticism
Razi says the argument is 'rhetorical' (khatabi). Understand why he uses that term.
Rhetorical arguments appeal to common belief but lack logical rigor.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider that if the argument only proves each part needs a cause, then the whole could have multiple causes, not necessarily one. This is a key nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : بعض هذا الكلام وهو الطريق الثاني خطابي ، وليس يدل على أن للعالم صانعا بل يدل على احتياج كل ممكن أو حادث من أجزاء العالم إلى مؤثر ولا يدل على أن الجميع محتاج إلى مؤثر ، وذلك لا يمكن إلا بالرجوع إلى الطريق الثاني . وقوله : إن كانت النطفة مركبة من بسائط والمؤثر غير ذي شعور لزم أن يكون المتخلف كرات مضمومة بعضها إلى بعض ليس بشيء ، لأن البسائط حال الامتزاج لا يجب أن تقتضى ما يقتضى كل واحد منها حال الانفراد. أقول : في إبطال التسلسل موضع نظر ، وذلك أنه أثبت لمجموع الأمور الغير المتناهية مؤثرا بسبب احتياج المجموع إلى آحاده وإنما يجب من ذلك أن يكون للمجموع مؤثرات لا نهاية لها هي الآحاد ، وإذا لم يكن كل واحد من تلك الآحاد علة لنفسه ولا للعلة يلزم أن لا يكون علة بانفراده للمجموع ، ولا يلزم أن لا يكون سائر الآحاد علة بل الحق ذلك وحينئذ يكون عامل المجموع داخلة فيه، ولا يلزم من ذلك أن يكون علة المجموع خارجة فلا يتم مطلوبه . وفى أوله : وإذا لم يكن علة لنفسه ولا لعلته لم يكن علة لذلك المجموع ، نظر لأنه إذا أراد أنه لم يكن علة قامة كان صحيحا لأنا إن فرضنا مجموعا مؤلفا من واجب وممكن هو معلوله لم يكن الواجب علة لنفسه ولم يكن الممكن علة لا لنفسه ولا لعلته ، ومع ذلك يكون كل واحد منهما جزءا من علة المجموع ولا يكون لذلك المجموع علة خارجة منه . وأما إثبات امتناع ما لا نهاية له في الوجود بدليل التطبيق كما قالوه في الكتب الحكمية فلا يتم ، والدليل أن ينقض من غير المتناهي جملة متناهية ويتوهم تطبيق الباقي على المجموع قبل النقصان ويقال لابد من أن يكون إحدى الجملتين أنقص من الأخرى بعدد متناه فيكون الجملتان غير متناهيتين كما مر بيانه ، وإنما لا يتم بمثل ما قلنا في الحوادث ويتم بمثل ما مر وهو أن يكون من مدار العالم إلى ما لا نهاية له جملة من العلل غير متناهية مترتبة كلها موجود والجملتان متطابقتان في الخارج من غير احتياج إلى توهم ومتساويتان في الجانب الذي على العالم، ومن الواجب أن يكون جملة العلل زائدة على جملة المعلولات بواحدة من العلل في الجانب الآخر الذي فرض غير متناه ، ويلزم من ذلك انقطاع المعلولات قبل انقطاع العامل المقتضي تناهيهما مع فرضهما غير متناهيين وذلك خلف فإذا كان المال غير متناهية مجال فالنسية مجال .
I say: Some of this discourse, namely the second way, is rhetorical and does not prove that the world has a Maker but proves the need of every contingent or originated thing among the world's parts for a cause, but does not prove that the whole needs a cause; that cannot be except by recourse to the second way. And his statement: 'If the sperm is composed of simple elements and the agent is without consciousness, then what results would be spheres joined to each other' is nothing, because the simple elements in a state of mixture do not necessarily necessitate what each would necessitate in isolation. I say: In the refutation of infinite regress there is a point of investigation. For he established for the sum of infinite things a cause due to the sum's need for its individuals; but from that it only follows that the sum has infinite causes which are the individuals. If each of those individuals is not a cause of itself nor of its cause, it does not follow that it is not a cause of the sum by itself; but it does not follow that the other individuals are not causes; rather the truth is that, and then the cause of the sum is internal to it, and it does not follow that the cause of the sum is external, so his desired conclusion is not attained. And at the beginning: 'And if it is not a cause of itself nor of its cause, it is not a cause of that sum' – this is open to investigation because if he meant it is not a cause of the whole, that is correct; but if we suppose a sum composed of a Necessary and a contingent which is its effect, then the Necessary is not a cause of itself, and the contingent is not a cause of itself nor of its cause, yet each of them is part of the cause of the sum, and that sum has no cause external to it. As for proving the impossibility of an infinite in existence by the argument of matching (mutābaqa) as they say in the philosophical books, it is not valid. The proof is that one takes from the infinite a finite part and imagines matching the remainder with the whole before the subtraction, and it is said that one of the two sets must be less than the other by a finite number, so the two sets are infinite, as previously explained. But it is not valid by what we say about temporal events; it is valid by something like what came before: that from the world's director ad infinitum there is an infinite series of causes, all existing and ordered; the two sets (causes and effects) are matched in external existence without needing imagination and are equal on the side that is upon the world; and it is necessary that the set of causes exceed the set of effects by one cause on the other side supposed infinite; that entails the effects ending before the causes end, necessitating their finitude while they are supposed infinite—a contradiction. So if the [causes] are infinite, it is invalid, so the [series] is invalid.
Source pages p. 151

Commentary
This passage contains critical glosses by Razi (or a commentator) on several points. First, the second way (not fully given) is deemed rhetorical—it only proves each part's need for a cause, not the whole's need, unless recourse is made to the second way itself. Second, the third way's argument about the sperm being composed of simple elements is criticized: the objection that mixing does not preserve the individual effects is valid; Razi (the glossator) says the original author's argument about spheres is invalid because when elements mix, they do not act as they would in isolation. Third, the refutation of infinite regress is examined. The glossator argues that the original proof that the sum of an infinite series needs an external cause fails because the individuals together could be the cause without being individually self-caused; a counterexample is given of a sum composed of a Necessary and a contingent (the contingent could be part of the cause of the whole). Finally, the philosophical argument from matching (muṭābaqa) to prove the impossibility of an infinite is discussed; the glossator suggests a specific formulation using ordered causal series that can be matched externally, leading to a contradiction if both series are infinite. This passage is highly technical and shows internal critique within the kalam tradition.

Key Terms
خطابي
مؤثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second way successfully proves that the world has a single Maker.
Correction: Razi argues it only proves each part needs a cause, not that the whole needs a single cause.

Checkpoint
What does Razi claim the second way proves?
A) The world has a single Maker.
B) Every part of the world needs a cause.
C) The whole world needs a cause.
D) The second way is invalid.

Key Takeaway
A proof that each part has a cause does not automatically prove the whole has a single cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the proofs for God's existence in your own faith journey, do you ever settle for arguments that only prove each thing has a cause without truly grasping that all of creation points to a single, unified Creator? How does that gap affect the strength of your conviction?
?
Razi warns against accepting a flawed argument even if it seems to support a correct conclusion. Is there a commonly used argument for Tawheed in your community that you suspect might be only rhetorical? Are you willing to re-examine it critically, even if it feels uncomfortable?

5 min
Critique of the Third Way
Razi dismisses the third way's objection about composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's objection to the third way's argument about composition and the behavior of simple elements.

Core Idea
When simple elements mix, they do not act as they do in isolation.

The third way argued that if the sperm is composed of simple parts and the agent is unconscious, then the result would be random. Razi dismisses this by noting that mixed elements do not produce the same effects as when separate. This critique shows the importance of considering emergent properties in causal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the original claim and Razi's rebuttal.
2
Understand the objection
The original argument says that without a conscious agent, the mixture would produce spheres (ordered forms). Razi says this does not follow because mixing changes properties.
The term 'basa'it' means simple elements.
3
Reflect on the significance
This criticism challenges the assumption that the mere existence of parts explains the order of the whole. It defends the need for an external agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Why does Razi dismiss the argument about the sperm?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an objection to your faith, do you instinctively reject it or do you carefully examine its hidden assumptions like Razi does here? Commit today to analyzing one doubt you have with the same intellectual rigor. Will you do that?
?
The passage teaches that mixing can change how things act. Think of a time you assumed the outcome of a situation based on its parts alone, but the 'mixture' (circumstances, relationships) led to something unexpected. How does this remind you to trust Allah's wisdom even when His design seems complex beyond your understanding? What specific area of your life will you surrender to that trust?

Critique of the Refutation of Infinite Regress
Razi critically examines the refutation of infinite regress and the matching argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's criticism of the standard refutation of infinite regress and the matching argument.

Core Idea
The sum of an infinite series may have an immanent cause, not necessarily an external one.

This longer passage examines the refutation of infinite regress (tasalsul). Razi argues that the proof that an infinite sum needs an external cause fails because the individuals in the series could together constitute the cause. He also critiques the philosophical matching argument (dalil al-tatbiq) for proving the impossibility of an actual infinite, suggesting a specific formulation using ordered causes and effects.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. This is a dense logical critique.
2
Identify the first criticism
Razi challenges the proof that the sum of infinite things needs an external cause. He gives a counterexample with a Necessary and contingent.
3
Understand the matching argument criticism
Razi says the standard matching argument is not valid (la yatim). He then presents a specific version that does work with ordered causal series.
The matching argument attempts to show two infinite sets cannot be compared.
4
Reflect on the implication
Razi is not rejecting the principle that an infinite regress is impossible; he is refining the arguments for it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Third, the refutation of infinite regress is examined. The glossator argues that the original proof that the sum of an infinite series needs an external cause fails because the individuals together could be the cause without being individually self-caused; a counterexample is given of a sum composed of a Necessary and a contingent (the contingent could be part of the cause of the whole). Finally, the philosophical argument from matching (muṭābaqa) to prove the impossibility of an infinite is discussed; the glossator suggests a specific formulation using ordered causal series that can be matched externally, leading to a contradiction if both series are infinite. This passage is highly technical and shows internal critique within the kalam tradition.

Key Terms
إبطال التسلسل
دليل التطبيق
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation of infinite regress is straightforward and universally accepted.
Correction: Razi shows that the standard proofs need refinement and that the matching argument is not valid in its usual form.

The refutation of infinite regress requires careful logical formulation, and not all arguments for it are valid.

Handling Apparent Anthropomorphic Verses
Imam Razi explains the two valid approaches to anthropomorphic verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two valid methodological approaches (tafwid and ta'wil) for dealing with scriptural verses that appear to imply divine corporeality, and why they do not conflict with definitive rational proofs.

Core Idea
Apparent anthropomorphic verses must be understood in harmony with definitive reason, either by consigning the modality to God or by interpretation.

Imam Razi provides a brief but crucial methodological note on how to approach verses of the Quran that, if taken at face value, seem to attribute a body or direction to Allah. These verses cannot be taken in opposition to the clear rational proofs that establish divine transcendence. In this passage, Razi outlines the two mainstream approaches: tafwid (consignment) of the true meaning to God, which is the way of the Salaf, and ta'wil (interpretation) in a manner consistent with reason, which is the approach of most theologians. Understanding these approaches is essential for reconciling revelation and reason.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the author's structure come through clearly.
2
Identify the two approaches
Find the two options Razi presents: tafwid (consigning to God) and ta'wil (interpretation). Notice how he attributes each to a group.
The Salaf are associated with tafwid, most mutakallimun with ta'wil.
3
Understand the role of rational proofs
Razi says the apparent meanings are not to oppose definitive rational proofs. Reflect on why this principle is foundational.
This ensures no conflict between revelation and reason.
4
Contrast the two approaches
Consider the difference: one refrains from interpretation, the other engages in it. Both are valid but have different methodologies.
Tafwid does not mean ignorance; it affirms the meaning befitting Allah.
5
Apply the lesson
Think of an example of an anthropomorphic verse (e.g., 'the Hand of Allah') and how each approach would handle it.
This cements the conceptual understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : الظواهر المقتضية للجسمية والجهة لا تكون معارضة الأدلة العقلية القطعية التي لا تقبل التأويل . وحينئذ إما أن يفوض عليها إلى الله تعالى على ماهو مذهب السلف ، وقول من أوجب الوقف على قوله وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله، وإما أن يستقل بتأويلها على الفصيل على ما هو مذهب أكثر المتكلمين وتلك التأويلات مستقصاة في المطولات
Note: The apparent meanings (al-zawahir) implying corporeality and direction are not to be opposed to the definitive rational proofs that admit no interpretation. Then, either one consigns them to God, according to the madhhab of the Salaf and the statement of those who mandated suspension on His word, and 'none knows its interpretation except God'; or one undertakes to interpret them in detail, according to the madhhab of most theologians, and those interpretations are fully covered in the lengthy works.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note on how to handle apparent (zahir) scriptural verses that suggest divine corporeality or direction. Imam Razi states that such verses cannot contradict definitive rational proofs (adillah qat'iyyah) that are not open to interpretation. Therefore, there are two valid approaches: the first is tafwid, consigning the true meaning to God, which is the way of the Salaf and those who say 'none knows its interpretation except God'; the second is ta'wil, interpreting these verses in a way consistent with reason, which is the approach of most theologians (mutakallimun). He adds that detailed interpretations are given in the longer works. This note clarifies that reason and revelation do not conflict; when they appear to, revelation must be understood in light of reason.

Key Terms
ظواهر
تفويض
تأويل
أدلة قطعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that tafwid means complete ignorance or negation of the verse's meaning.
Correction: Tafwid means consigning the modality (kayfiyyah) to God while affirming the meaning that befits Him, not complete ignorance.

Checkpoint
What are the two valid approaches to handling anthropomorphic verses according to this passage?
A) Rejecting them or reinterpreting them.
B) Tafwid (consignment) or ta'wil (interpretation).
C) Literal reading or metaphorical reading.
D) Ignoring them or affirming corporeality.

Key Takeaway
The apparent anthropomorphic verses must be reconciled with definitive rational proofs, either through tafwid (consigning the reality to God) or through ta'wil (interpretation).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Have you ever truly submitted to the fact that definitive rational knowledge from God cannot contradict His revelation, or do you let emotion lead you to deny reason? What will you do the next time such a verse unsettles you?
?
Both the Salaf and the theologians sought to protect God's transcendence, yet they chose different paths. Which approach do you find yourself naturally leaning toward—trusting tafwid or seeking ta'wil? Are you willing to learn from the other camp instead of dismissing it?
?
In your personal relationship with God, have you ever avoided deeply reflecting on certain verses because they seemed contradictory to what you believe? What would it cost you to commit to studying how scholars have reconciled these verses, rather than ignoring them?

Two Approaches to Anthropomorphic Texts: Tafwid and Ta'wil
Imam al-Razi's note on handling anthropomorphic texts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical approaches (tafwid and ta'wil) to handling scriptural texts that appear to attribute corporeality or direction to Allah, and how they relate to definitive rational proofs.

Core Idea
Apparent anthropomorphic texts neither override reason nor require rejection.

Imam al-Razi presents a brief but crucial methodological note: scriptural texts that seem to imply corporeality or direction for Allah cannot contradict definitive rational proofs. Instead, scholars have adopted two valid approaches: entrusting the meaning to Allah (tafwid) or providing allegorical interpretation (ta'wil). This note clarifies that both methods are acceptable within Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style of Imam al-Razi.
2
Identify the two approaches
The passage mentions two approaches for dealing with apparent anthropomorphic texts. Find them and note how each is associated with a different school (Salaf vs. mutakallimun).
The terms tafwid and ta'wil are key.
3
Understand the guiding principle
Before presenting the two approaches, the author states a key principle: these texts do not oppose definitive rational proofs. Ask yourself why this principle is foundational.
This principle ensures scripture and reason are not in conflict.
4
Reflect on the harmony
Consider how the two approaches—tafwid and ta'wil—both maintain the transcendence of Allah while respecting the primacy of rational proofs. This reflects the broader Islamic commitment to both revelation and reason.
Both approaches affirm that Allah is beyond corporeality and direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه : الظواهر المقتضية للجسمية والجهة لا تكون معارضة للأدلة العقلية القطعية التي لا تقبل التأويل . وحينئذ إما أن يفوض عليها إلى الله تعالى على ماهو مذهب السلف ، وقول من أوجب الوقف على قوله وما يعلم تأويله إلا الله، وإما أن يستقل بتأويلها على الفصيل [؟] على ما هو مذهب أكثر المتكلمين وتلك التأويلات مستقصاة في المطولات
A note: The apparent meanings that necessitate corporeality and direction are not to be opposed to definitive rational proofs that do not accept interpretation. Then either they are entrusted to God Almighty according to the school of the Salaf, and the saying of those who require stopping at His saying 'and none knows its interpretation except Allah', or one undertakes their interpretation in detail according to the school of most of the mutakallimun, and those interpretations are fully covered in the lengthy works.
Source pages p. 160

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note on how to handle scriptural texts that appear to attribute corporeality (jismiyya) and direction (jiha) to God. The author states that such texts cannot be used to oppose definitive rational proofs that are not open to allegorical interpretation. Two approaches are then mentioned: the first is tafwid (entrusting the meaning to God), which is the way of the Salaf and those who follow the verse 'and none knows its interpretation except Allah'. The second is ta'wil (interpretation), which is the way of most mutakallimun, who provide detailed interpretations that are set out in lengthy works. The author does not favor one over the other here, simply presenting both as valid.

Key Takeaway
Scriptural texts that appear to attribute bodily features to Allah are not to be taken as contradicting rational proofs, and scholars accept both tafwid and ta'wil as valid responses.

Limitation of Previous Proof and Comprehensive Argument
Commentator critiques previous proof and gives comprehensive argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the previous proof for will only applies to temporal acts and how the comprehensive argument based on specification establishes will for all acts.

Core Idea
All acts of specification need a divine specifier—this is will.

The commentator begins by noting that the earlier proof for will only works for acts occurring in time. Acts like creating time itself require a different argument. He then offers a comprehensive proof: the specification of any possible thing for existence requires a specifier, which is will. This sets the foundation for the subsequent discussion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem with the previous proof
Read the first part of the passage to see how the commentator distinguishes between temporal acts and non-temporal acts like creating time. Understand why a new argument is needed.
Non-temporal acts include the creation of time, body, etc.
2
Understand the comprehensive argument
The comprehensive argument states that specification (takhsis) of any possible thing for existence needs a mukhassis (specifier), which is will. Identify this key claim.
3
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is 'comprehensive'—it covers all acts, not just temporal ones. This is the main proof for will.
The argument relies on the need for a specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : الحجة التي أوردها على إثبات الإرادة خاصة بأفعال تقع فى أزمنة أما التي لا تكون واقعة في أزمنه مثل خلق الزمان والجسم وسائر علل الزمان إن كانت بإرادة احتيج فى إثبات الإرادة هناك إلى حجة أخرى ، إلا أن يقال أنها تحصل من غير إرادة وذلك ما لم يقولوا به . والحجة التي تشمل الكل هى أن يقال تخصيص ما يخصص بالإيجاد من جميع المقدورات يحتاج إلى مخصص وهو الإرادة ، إلا أن المصنف لما جوز أن يخصص القادر أحد الطرفين من غير مخصص انسد عليه باب إثبات الإرادة مطلقا، وكان لقائل أن يقول إن قدرته تعالى تعاق بوقت للإيجاد دون وقت من غير مخصص وقوله : المخصص ليس القدرة . مناقض لما ذهب إليه فيما مر ، وهو أن المختار يمكنه الترجيح من غير مرجح . وقوله : ولا العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم تناقض قوله: ما علم الله وقوعه يجب أن يقع لاستحالة كون الموجب تابعا لموجبه والاعتراض بتجويز كون الإمكان خاصا بوقت معين لا يتوجه على الأفعال التي لا نقع في زمان . والجواب بأن الموصوف بإمكان الحركة هو الجسم يقتضى أن يكون الجسم هو الحاصل قبل ذلك الزمان وهو ليس بصحيح؛ لأن إمكان الحركة المشروطة بذلك الزمان لا يكون حاصلا قبل ذلك الزمان فكيف يكون الجسم موصوفا بة وكون الإمكان من لوازم الماهية لا ينتقض بماذكره ، لأن الإمكان المطلق من لوازم الماهية والإمكان المقيد بشيء غير لازم لا يكون من لوازمها ولا يتناقضان باختلاف الدوام واللادوام لا يكون لاختلاف موضوعا . وقوله في الجواب عن تجويز كون الإمكان مقيدا بوقت : أن الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يكون له أثر ، وإن كان موجودا كان الكلام فيه كما فى الأول لأجل دليله على إثبات الإرادة، بأن يقال الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يتخصص بالإرادة وإن كان موجودا احتاج إلى وقت آخر وإرادة أخرى تخصصه به ويتسلسل . قوله : كون المساهية متقررة قبل وجودها بناء على أن الماهية متقررة حال عدمها فيه نظر ، لأن الماهية متقررة قبل وجودها وقبل عدمها قبلية بالذات ولا يلزم منه أن يكون تقررها حال صدمها إلا إذا كانت القبلية بالزمان . والقول بأن الحوادث مستندة إلى الاتصالات الفلكية إن أريد بالاستناد كون الاتصالات شرطا لوجوداتها لا ينافي كونها واقعة بقدرة الله تعالى
I say: The argument he presented for affirming will is specific to acts that occur in times. As for those that do not occur in time, such as the creation of time, body, and all causes of time, if they are by will, another argument is needed to affirm will there, unless it is said that they occur without will, which they did not say. The comprehensive argument is that the specification of what is specified for existence from among all things within power needs a specifier, and that is will. However, when the author allowed that the powerful one can specify one of the two sides without a specifier, the door of affirming will was blocked absolutely. And it was possible for someone to say that His power attaches to a time for existence rather than another time without a specifier. And his statement: 'The specifier is not power' contradicts what he held earlier, namely that the free chooser can give preponderance without a preponderant. And his statement: 'Nor knowledge because it is consequent upon the known' contradicts his statement: 'What God knows will occur must occur' because it is impossible for the necessitating to be consequent upon its necessitated. And the objection by allowing that possibility is specific to a certain time does not apply to acts that do not occur in time. And the answer that the thing described with possibility of movement is the body implies that the body exists before that time, which is not correct; because the possibility of movement conditioned by that time is not present before that time, so how can the body be described with it? And the fact that possibility is among the necessary concomitants of the essence is not invalidated by what he mentioned, because absolute possibility is among the necessary concomitants of the essence, while restricted possibility is not necessary nor among its concomitants, and they do not contradict each other due to difference in perpetuity and non-perpetuity, because of the difference of subject. And his statement in answer to allowing that possibility is restricted to a time: that if time is not existent, it is impossible for it to have an effect, and if it is existent, the discussion is as in the first case, because of his proof for affirming will, namely: if time is not existent, it is impossible for it to be specified by will, and if it is existent, it needs another time and another will to specify it, leading to infinite regress. His statement: 'The determination of the essence before its existence based on the essence being determined in the state of its non-existence' is questionable, because the essence is determined before its existence and before its non-existence by essential priority, and it does not necessitate that its determination be in its state of non-existence unless the priority is temporal. And the statement that events are dependent on celestial conjunctions: if by dependence is meant that the conjunctions are a condition for their existence, it does not contradict that they occur by the power of God.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's (perhaps the editor's) analysis of the arguments for will. It begins by noting that the proof Imam Razi gave for will only applies to temporal acts; for non-temporal acts like creating time itself, another argument is needed. The commentator then provides a comprehensive proof: the specification (takhsis) of any possible thing for existence needs a specifier (mukhassis), which is will. He criticizes the author (al-musannif) for allowing that a powerful agent can specify one of two alternatives without a specifier, which would block any proof for will. He then points out contradictions in the author's statements: that power can specify without a specifier contradicts the earlier claim that the free chooser can preponderate without a preponderant; and that knowledge cannot be the specifier because it follows the known, yet the author also said that what God knows to exist must exist (which would make knowledge a cause). He also discusses the objection about possibility being time-bound: it does not apply to acts outside time. He then critiques the answer about the body being described with possibility of movement: the possibility of movement at a specific time is not present before that time, so the body cannot be described with it. He distinguishes absolute possibility (imkan mutlaq) which is a necessary concomitant of essence, from restricted possibility which is not. He then comments on the answer about time: if time is non-existent, it cannot be specified; if existent, it needs another time and will ad infinitum. Finally, he addresses the claim of essences being determined before existence: this is only true if priority is essential, not temporal. And celestial conjunctions can be conditions (shart) without negating divine power.

Key Terms
الإرادة
التخصيص
المخصص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) All temporal acts need a cause.
B) Specification of possibles needs a specifier, which is will.
C) God's knowledge determines everything.
D) Will is an essential attribute of God.

Key Takeaway
The comprehensive proof for will is that the specification of any possible thing for existence requires a specifier, which is will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The comprehensive argument shows that nothing happens by chance—not even the creation of time itself. Contradictions in the Author's Position on Power and Knowledge
Commentator points out contradictions in author's arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the contradictions the commentator identifies in the author's views on power, preponderance, and knowledge as potential specifiers.

Core Idea
The author's own statements contradict each other, undermining his denial of a specifier.

The commentator now attacks the author's (Imam Razi's) position. The author had allowed that a powerful agent can specify one side without a specifier, which the commentator says contradicts the earlier claim that the free chooser can preponderate without a preponderant. He also shows that the author's view on knowledge—that it follows the known—contradicts his statement that what God knows must occur. These contradictions need resolution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first contradiction: power
The author said the specifier is not power, but earlier he said the free chooser can preponderate without a preponderant. The commentator argues these are inconsistent.
Power cannot be both the specifier and not the specifier.
2
Identify the second contradiction: knowledge
The author said knowledge is not the specifier because it follows the known. Yet he also said that what God knows to exist must exist. This makes knowledge a necessitating cause.
If knowledge necessitates existence, then it is not merely following the known.
3
Understand the implications
These contradictions show that the author's framework is inconsistent, and a proper proof for will must avoid them.

أقول : الحجة التي أوردها على إثبات الإرادة خاصة بأفعال تقع فى أزمنة أما التي لا تكون واقعة في أزمنه مثل خلق الزمان والجسم وسائر علل الزمان إن كانت بإرادة احتيج فى إثبات الإرادة هناك إلى حجة أخرى ، إلا أن يقال أنها تحصل من غير إرادة وذلك ما لم يقولوا به . والحجة التي تشمل الكل هى أن يقال تخصيص ما يخصص بالإيجاد من جميع المقدورات يحتاج إلى مخصص وهو الإرادة ، إلا أن المصنف لما جوز أن يخصص القادر أحد الطرفين من غير مخصص انسد عليه باب إثبات الإرادة مطلقا، وكان لقائل أن يقول إن قدرته تعالى تعاق بوقت للإيجاد دون وقت من غير مخصص وقوله : المخصص ليس القدرة . مناقض لما ذهب إليه فيما مر ، وهو أن المختار يمكنه الترجيح من غير مرجح . وقوله : ولا العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم تناقض قوله: ما علم الله وقوعه يجب أن يقع لاستحالة كون الموجب تابعا لموجبه والاعتراض بتجويز كون الإمكان خاصا بوقت معين لا يتوجه على الأفعال التي لا نقع في زمان . والجواب بأن الموصوف بإمكان الحركة هو الجسم يقتضى أن يكون الجسم هو الحاصل قبل ذلك الزمان وهو ليس بصحيح؛ لأن إمكان الحركة المشروطة بذلك الزمان لا يكون حاصلا قبل ذلك الزمان فكيف يكون الجسم موصوفا بة وكون الإمكان من لوازم الماهية لا ينتقض بماذكره ، لأن الإمكان المطلق من لوازم الماهية والإمكان المقيد بشيء غير لازم لا يكون من لوازمها ولا يتناقضان باختلاف الدوام واللادوام لا يكون لاختلاف موضوعا . وقوله في الجواب عن تجويز كون الإمكان مقيدا بوقت : أن الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يكون له أثر ، وإن كان موجودا كان الكلام فيه كما فى الأول لأجل دليله على إثبات الإرادة، بأن يقال الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يتخصص بالإرادة وإن كان موجودا احتاج إلى وقت آخر وإرادة أخرى تخصصه به ويتسلسل . قوله : كون المساهية متقررة قبل وجودها بناء على أن الماهية متقررة حال عدمها فيه نظر ، لأن الماهية متقررة قبل وجودها وقبل عدمها قبلية بالذات ولا يلزم منه أن يكون تقررها حال صدمها إلا إذا كانت القبلية بالزمان . والقول بأن الحوادث مستندة إلى الاتصالات الفلكية إن أريد بالاستناد كون الاتصالات شرطا لوجوداتها لا ينافي كونها واقعة بقدرة الله تعالى
I say: The argument he presented for affirming will is specific to acts that occur in times. As for those that do not occur in time, such as the creation of time, body, and all causes of time, if they are by will, another argument is needed to affirm will there, unless it is said that they occur without will, which they did not say. The comprehensive argument is that the specification of what is specified for existence from among all things within power needs a specifier, and that is will. However, when the author allowed that the powerful one can specify one of the two sides without a specifier, the door of affirming will was blocked absolutely. And it was possible for someone to say that His power attaches to a time for existence rather than another time without a specifier. And his statement: 'The specifier is not power' contradicts what he held earlier, namely that the free chooser can give preponderance without a preponderant. And his statement: 'Nor knowledge because it is consequent upon the known' contradicts his statement: 'What God knows will occur must occur' because it is impossible for the necessitating to be consequent upon its necessitated. And the objection by allowing that possibility is specific to a certain time does not apply to acts that do not occur in time. And the answer that the thing described with possibility of movement is the body implies that the body exists before that time, which is not correct; because the possibility of movement conditioned by that time is not present before that time, so how can the body be described with it? And the fact that possibility is among the necessary concomitants of the essence is not invalidated by what he mentioned, because absolute possibility is among the necessary concomitants of the essence, while restricted possibility is not necessary nor among its concomitants, and they do not contradict each other due to difference in perpetuity and non-perpetuity, because of the difference of subject. And his statement in answer to allowing that possibility is restricted to a time: that if time is not existent, it is impossible for it to have an effect, and if it is existent, the discussion is as in the first case, because of his proof for affirming will, namely: if time is not existent, it is impossible for it to be specified by will, and if it is existent, it needs another time and another will to specify it, leading to infinite regress. His statement: 'The determination of the essence before its existence based on the essence being determined in the state of its non-existence' is questionable, because the essence is determined before its existence and before its non-existence by essential priority, and it does not necessitate that its determination be in its state of non-existence unless the priority is temporal. And the statement that events are dependent on celestial conjunctions: if by dependence is meant that the conjunctions are a condition for their existence, it does not contradict that they occur by the power of God.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's (perhaps the editor's) analysis of the arguments for will. It begins by noting that the proof Imam Razi gave for will only applies to temporal acts; for non-temporal acts like creating time itself, another argument is needed. The commentator then provides a comprehensive proof: the specification (takhsis) of any possible thing for existence needs a specifier (mukhassis), which is will. He criticizes the author (al-musannif) for allowing that a powerful agent can specify one of two alternatives without a specifier, which would block any proof for will. He then points out contradictions in the author's statements: that power can specify without a specifier contradicts the earlier claim that the free chooser can preponderate without a preponderant; and that knowledge cannot be the specifier because it follows the known, yet the author also said that what God knows to exist must exist (which would make knowledge a cause). He also discusses the objection about possibility being time-bound: it does not apply to acts outside time. He then critiques the answer about the body being described with possibility of movement: the possibility of movement at a specific time is not present before that time, so the body cannot be described with it. He distinguishes absolute possibility (imkan mutlaq) which is a necessary concomitant of essence, from restricted possibility which is not. He then comments on the answer about time: if time is non-existent, it cannot be specified; if existent, it needs another time and will ad infinitum. Finally, he addresses the claim of essences being determined before existence: this is only true if priority is essential, not temporal. And celestial conjunctions can be conditions (shart) without negating divine power.

Key Terms
الترجيح
المرجح
المختار
العلم
المعلوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first contradiction the commentator identifies?
A) The author says power is the specifier but then denies it.
B) The author says the free chooser can preponderate without a preponderant, but then denies power is the specifier.
C) The author says knowledge is a cause but then denies it.
D) The author says will is not needed.

Key Takeaway
The author's own statements on power and knowledge contradict each other, showing his denial of a specifier is inconsistent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a scholar's reasoning can be inconsistent. Are there areas in your own understanding of Allah's attributes where you hold contradictory ideas? Will you take time today to identify one such area and seek clarity from reliable sources?
?
The commentator exposes a contradiction by using the author's own words against him. Are you willing to let the statements of the Quran and authentic hadith challenge your own preconceived notions about Allah, even if it feels uncomfortable? Can you humbly admit when your reasoning is flawed?
?
The denial of a specifier leads to blocking the door of affirming will. Have you ever avoided a difficult theological question because it seemed too complex? Will you commit to studying this topic further to strengthen your conviction in Allah's will and power?

Objection of Time-Bound Possibility and the Distinction of Imkan
Commentator refutes objection about time-bound possibility using the body example and distinguishes absolute and restricted possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that possibility (imkan) may be restricted to a specific time, and the responses involving the body and the distinction between absolute and restricted possibility.

Core Idea
Absolute possibility is an essential attribute; restricted possibility is not, so it cannot be used to avoid the need for a specifier.

The commentator addresses a potential objection: what if possibility (imkan) is specific to a certain time, so that an act could only happen at that time without a specifier? He first notes this objection doesn't apply to non-temporal acts. Then he critiques the answer that the body is described with possibility of movement: the possibility conditioned by a future time does not exist before that time. He then explains that absolute possibility (imkan mutlaq) is a necessary concomitant of essence, while restricted possibility (imkan muqayyad) is not; thus the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection says that the possibility of an act might be restricted to a specific time, so no external specifier is needed. The commentator notes this does not apply to non-temporal acts.
2
Examine the response about the body
The answer says the body is described with possibility of movement. But the possibility of movement at a specific time is not present before that time, so the body cannot be described with it.
This shows the problem with time-bound possibility.
3
Distinguish absolute vs restricted possibility
Absolute possibility (imkan mutlaq) is a necessary concomitant of essence, meaning it always applies. Restricted possibility (imkan muqayyad) is conditional and not a necessary concomitant. So the objection confuses the two types.
Only absolute possibility is essential; restricted possibility does not remove the need for a specifier.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all possibility is time-bound, removing the need for a specifier.
Correction: Absolute possibility is an essential attribute of essence and is not time-bound; only restricted possibility is conditional.

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction made in this passage?
A) Between temporal and non-temporal acts.
B) Between absolute and restricted possibility.
C) Between essence and existence.
D) Between power and knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Absolute possibility is an essential concomitant of essence, while restricted possibility is not; thus the objection of time-bound possibility does not eliminate the need for a specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What absolute possibility do you have as a human being—such as the capacity to know your Creator—that you have neglected because you are preoccupied with time-bound possibilities like worldly achievements? Will you reorient your focus today toward that which is essential?
?
Have you ever treated a temporary condition—like a mood, a circumstance, or a sin—as if it defined your very essence, thereby avoiding the need to seek a higher cause for your actions? How can this lesson help you distinguish what is truly essential from what is merely conditional?
?
The need for a specifier is established despite all objections. When you feel distant from Allah, what 'time-bound possibility' do you cling to that distracts you from your absolute need for Him? Commit to one concrete action today that acknowledges your absolute dependence on the Creator.

Infinite Regress in Time Specification and Essence Priority
Commentator refutes time specification leading to regress, critiques essence priority, and clarifies celestial conjunctions as conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two further arguments: the infinite regress problem if time itself needs to be specified by will, and the critique of essence being determined before existence with temporal priority.

Core Idea
If time is used to specify will, it leads to infinite regress; essence priority is essential, not temporal.

The commentator continues to dismantle objections. If we say that will specifies acts at a certain time, then time itself must exist either before or after specification. If before, it needs another time and will, leading to infinite regress. He also criticizes the idea that essences are determined in the state of non-existence: priority is essential (bi-dhat), not temporal (bi-zaman). Finally, he notes that celestial conjunctions can be conditions without negating divine power.

Guided Path
1
Understand the infinite regress argument
If time is not existent, it cannot be specified; if it is existent, it needs another time and will. This leads to an infinite chain.
This refutes the idea that will can specify a time.
2
Examine the critique of essence priority
The claim that essence is determined before existence (in non-existence) is only valid if priority is essential, not temporal. Temporal priority would mean it exists before existence, which is contradictory.
3
Consider the celestial conjunctions point
Celestial conjunctions can be a condition (shart) for events, but that does not negate that events occur by God's power. The condition is not a cause.
This preserves divine causality while acknowledging secondary causes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : الحجة التي أوردها على إثبات الإرادة خاصة بأفعال تقع فى أزمنة أما التي لا تكون واقعة في أزمنه مثل خلق الزمان والجسم وسائر علل الزمان إن كانت بإرادة احتيج فى إثبات الإرادة هناك إلى حجة أخرى ، إلا أن يقال أنها تحصل من غير إرادة وذلك ما لم يقولوا به . والحجة التي تشمل الكل هى أن يقال تخصيص ما يخصص بالإيجاد من جميع المقدورات يحتاج إلى مخصص وهو الإرادة ، إلا أن المصنف لما جوز أن يخصص القادر أحد الطرفين من غير مخصص انسد عليه باب إثبات الإرادة مطلقا، وكان لقائل أن يقول إن قدرته تعالى تعاق بوقت للإيجاد دون وقت من غير مخصص وقوله : المخصص ليس القدرة . مناقض لما ذهب إليه فيما مر ، وهو أن المختار يمكنه الترجيح من غير مرجح . وقوله : ولا العلم لأنه تابع للمعلوم تناقض قوله: ما علم الله وقوعه يجب أن يقع لاستحالة كون الموجب تابعا لموجبه والاعتراض بتجويز كون الإمكان خاصا بوقت معين لا يتوجه على الأفعال التي لا نقع في زمان . والجواب بأن الموصوف بإمكان الحركة هو الجسم يقتضى أن يكون الجسم هو الحاصل قبل ذلك الزمان وهو ليس بصحيح؛ لأن إمكان الحركة المشروطة بذلك الزمان لا يكون حاصلا قبل ذلك الزمان فكيف يكون الجسم موصوفا بة وكون الإمكان من لوازم الماهية لا ينتقض بماذكره ، لأن الإمكان المطلق من لوازم الماهية والإمكان المقيد بشيء غير لازم لا يكون من لوازمها ولا يتناقضان باختلاف الدوام واللادوام لا يكون لاختلاف موضوعا . وقوله في الجواب عن تجويز كون الإمكان مقيدا بوقت : أن الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يكون له أثر ، وإن كان موجودا كان الكلام فيه كما فى الأول لأجل دليله على إثبات الإرادة، بأن يقال الوقت إن لم يكن موجودا استحال أن يتخصص بالإرادة وإن كان موجودا احتاج إلى وقت آخر وإرادة أخرى تخصصه به ويتسلسل . قوله : كون المساهية متقررة قبل وجودها بناء على أن الماهية متقررة حال عدمها فيه نظر ، لأن الماهية متقررة قبل وجودها وقبل عدمها قبلية بالذات ولا يلزم منه أن يكون تقررها حال صدمها إلا إذا كانت القبلية بالزمان . والقول بأن الحوادث مستندة إلى الاتصالات الفلكية إن أريد بالاستناد كون الاتصالات شرطا لوجوداتها لا ينافي كونها واقعة بقدرة الله تعالى

And the fact that possibility is among the necessary concomitants of the essence is not invalidated by what he mentioned, because absolute possibility is among the necessary concomitants of the essence, while restricted possibility is not necessary nor among its concomitants, and they do not contradict each other due to difference in perpetuity and non-perpetuity, because of the difference of subject. And his statement in answer to allowing that possibility is restricted to a time: that if time is not existent, it is impossible for it to have an effect, and if it is existent, the discussion is as in the first case, because of his proof for affirming will, namely: if time is not existent, it is impossible for it to be specified by will, and if it is existent, it needs another time and another will to specify it, leading to infinite regress. His statement: 'The determination of the essence before its existence based on the essence being determined in the state of its non-existence' is questionable, because the essence is determined before its existence and before its non-existence by essential priority, and it does not necessitate that its determination be in its state of non-existence unless the priority is temporal. And the statement that events are dependent on celestial conjunctions: if by dependence is meant that the conjunctions are a condition for their existence, it does not contradict that they occur by the power of God.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a commentator's (perhaps the editor's) analysis of the arguments for will. It begins by noting that the proof Imam Razi gave for will only applies to temporal acts; for non-temporal acts like creating time itself, another argument is needed. The commentator then provides a comprehensive proof: the specification (takhsis) of any possible thing for existence needs a specifier (mukhassis), which is will. He criticizes the author (al-musannif) for allowing that a powerful agent can specify one of two alternatives without a specifier, which would block any proof for will. He then points out contradictions in the author's statements: that power can specify without a specifier contradicts the earlier claim that the free chooser can preponderate without a preponderant; and that knowledge cannot be the specifier because it follows the known, yet the author also said that what God knows to exist must exist (which would make knowledge a cause). He also discusses the objection about possibility being time-bound: it does not apply to acts outside time. He then critiques the answer about the body being described with possibility of movement: the possibility of movement at a specific time is not present before that time, so the body cannot be described with it. He distinguishes absolute possibility (imkan mutlaq) which is a necessary concomitant of essence, from restricted possibility which is not. He then comments on the answer about time: if time is non-existent, it cannot be specified; if existent, it needs another time and will ad infinitum. Finally, he addresses the claim of essences being determined before existence: this is only true if priority is essential, not temporal. And celestial conjunctions can be conditions (shart) without negating divine power.

Key Terms
القبلية بالذات
القبلية بالزمان
المساهية
الاتصالات الفلكية
الشرط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the commentator say about celestial conjunctions?
A) They are the sole causes of events.
B) They are conditions for events, not negating divine power.
C) They are irrelevant to divine will.
D) They necessitate events without God.

Key Takeaway
Time specification leads to infinite regress, essence priority is essential not temporal, and celestial conjunctions are conditions, not independent causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see events unfolding in your life—whether successes or trials—do you truly attribute them to God's power alone, or do you secretly rely on visible conditions (like your effort or timing) as if they are sufficient? What would it mean to change that today?
?
The lesson shows that time cannot confine God's will without leading to absurdity. Are there ways you have placed God in a box of your own timing, expecting Him to act according to your schedule? Will you surrender that clock to Him?
?
Your essence is prior to your existence essentially, not temporally. That means your entire being is contingent on God at every moment. Do you live as if you are autonomous, or do you feel the constant dependence that this truth demands? Commit to one concrete reminder of your contingency today.

Critique of Proof from Need for Separate Proof
Commentator critiques Razi's proof on knowledge being superadded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the need for a separate proof does not necessarily prove a real distinction between existence and essence, according to the commentator's critique.

Core Idea
Different proofs can point to different aspects of one reality, not separate realities.

The commentator responds to an earlier argument that because knowledge of existence requires a separate proof, existence and knowledge must be distinct realities. He shows that this reasoning confuses the order of proof with the order of being. Using two analogies, he demonstrates that different proofs can refer to different conceptual considerations of the same thing, rather than to distinct entities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall argument.
Notice that this is a critical response, not a standalone proof.
2
Identify the main claim
The commentator argues that the need for a separate proof does not prove real distinctness.
Look for the phrase 'لا يدل على تغاير'.
3
Analyze the first analogy
The Creator is distinct from the proof that He is one, but that does not make the Creator composite.
The analogy shows that distinctness of proof does not imply real composition.
4
Analyze the second analogy
If one proof indicates existence and another indicates identity of existence and essence, that only shows different considerations, not different realities.
The key phrase is 'تغاير الاعتبارين لا على تغاير الحقيقتين'.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this critique highlights the importance of distinguishing between epistemological order (proofs) and ontological reality.
This is a methodological point in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول: افتقار العلم بالوجود إلى دليل آخر يدل على العلم لا يدل على تغاير الوجود والعلم، الصانع مغاير للدليل الدال على أنه واحد، ومع ذلك لا يلزم كون الصانع الموجود فان الدليل الدال على وجود غير ذلك الواحد وأيضاً إذا دل الدليل على وجوده وآخر على كون وجوده عين ذاته لم يدل ذلك على أن وجوده غير كون وجوده غير ذاته يدل ذلك على تغاير الاعتبارين لا على تغاير الحقيقتين.
I say: The need for another proof indicating knowledge after the knowledge of existence does not prove the distinctness of existence and knowledge. The Creator is distinct from the proof that He is one, yet that does not entail that the existing Creator is [something else]. For the proof that indicates existence is other than that one [proof]. Also, if a proof indicates His existence and another indicates that His existence is identical to His essence, that does not prove that His existence is other than that His existence is not other than His essence; that only proves a difference of considerations (iʿtibārān), not a difference of realities.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary (aqūl) on the previous proof. The commentator argues that the need for a separate proof does not necessarily entail ontological distinctness. He gives two analogies: 1) The Creator is distinct from the proof that He is one, but that does not mean the Creator is composed of two parts. 2) If a proof indicates existence and another indicates that existence is identical to essence, that only shows a difference in how we consider (iʿtibār) the same reality, not a real distinction. Thus, the previous argument fails because it confuses the order of proof with the order of being.

Key Terms
دليل
تغاير
اعتبار
حقيقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That different proofs always imply different objects or realities.
Correction: Different proofs can refer to different conceptual aspects of one and the same reality.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's main objection to Razi's proof?
A) The proof is too weak.
B) The need for a separate proof does not prove real distinction.
C) Knowledge is not an attribute.
D) Essence cannot be proven.

Key Takeaway
The need for a separate proof indicates only a difference in how we consider a thing, not that the thing itself is composed of distinct realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you seek proofs for God's existence, do you ever assume that each proof points to a separate aspect of God? How does this lesson challenge you to see multiple proofs as different considerations of one simple reality?
?
The commentator shows that the structure of our reasoning does not necessarily mirror the structure of reality. In your own journey of faith, have you ever confused the order of your proofs with the order of God's being? What would change if you deeply internalized this distinction?
?
This lesson distinguishes between consideration (iʿtibār) and reality (ḥaqīqah). Are you content to know God through many considerations, or do you feel a need to reduce Him to a single proof? Examine your heart: is your desire for multiple proofs a sign of humility or a lack of trust in His simplicity?

Critique of the First Answer: Agent-Receiver and Difference
The commentator critiques the first answer and redefines difference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator rejects Razi's definition of difference and proposes a better one based on essences and attributes.

Core Idea
Attributes are not independent essences, so they cannot be 'distinct' from the essence they qualify.

Razi attempted to answer an objection by allowing a thing to be both agent and recipient and by defining difference as separation in four aspects. The commentator finds this problematic because many inseparable things are still distinct. He offers a refined definition: difference applies only to two essences, not to an essence and its attribute. This distinction is crucial for avoiding confusion about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two positions
Read the passage and locate Razi's original claim (agent-receiver and difference as separation) and the commentator's critique.
2
Understand the problem with Razi's definition
The commentator notes that many causes and effects cannot separate despite being distinct, so separation is not a necessary condition for difference.
Think of examples like a cause and its effect that always coexist.
3
Grasp the commentator's alternative
Learn that true difference (taghāyur) is between two independent essences, and an attribute is not an essence separate from its subject.
This has implications for divine attributes: they are not distinct entities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : قوله الجواب عن الأول قد تقدم يريد به تجويز كون الشىء فاعلا وقابلا وفي تفسير التغاير يجواز المفارقة في أحد الأمور الأربعة موضع نظر ، وذلك لأن كثيرا من العلل والمحاولات ممتنع المفارقة مع وجوب تغايرها ، والأولى أن يقال المتغايران هما ذاتان والذات لا تغاير صفتها لان صفتها لا تكون مغايرة بالذات لها أولا ذات لها ولمثل ذلك لا تتغاير الصفات . وما قال في الجواب عن الخامس فيه نظر لأن العلم على تقدير كونه نسبة أو تعلقا إلى معلوم فالنسب التي تكون إلى معلوم واحد تكون متماثلة ولا يندفع بقياسها على الوجود ولا في الوجود على وجوده وعلى وجوده.
I say: His statement 'the answer to the first has already preceded' intends by it the permissibility of a thing being both agent and recipient, and in the interpretation of difference (taghāyur) the permissibility of separation in one of the four things is subject to scrutiny, because many causes and effects [or attempts] cannot separate despite the necessity of their difference. It is better to say that two distinct things are two essences, and the essence does not differ from its attribute, because its attribute is not essentially distinct from it, nor does it have an essence of its own, and for such things attributes do not differ. And what he said in the answer to the fifth is subject to scrutiny, because knowledge, given that it is a relation or attachment to a known, the relations that are to one known are similar, and it is not refuted by analogy to existence, nor in existence regarding His existence and His existence.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's critique of Razi's answers. First, regarding the answer to the first objection, the commentator says that the concept of a thing being both agent and recipient, and the interpretation of difference as allowing separation in four aspects (time, place, existence, non-existence), is problematic because many causes and effects cannot separate even though they are distinct. He suggests a better definition: two distinct things are two essences, but an attribute is not an essence distinct from its subject, so it is not a 'different' thing. Second, regarding the answer to the fifth objection, the commentator argues that if knowledge is a relation to a known, then all relations to the same known are similar, so the analogy to existence is invalid. This challenges Razi's attempt to avoid the problem of similar knowledges.

Key Terms
فاعل
قابل
تغاير
ذات
صفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that essence and attribute are two distinct things in the same way that two substances are distinct.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that an attribute is not an independent essence, so it does not count as a distinct entity from its essence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the proper definition of 'two distinct things'?
A) Two things that can separate in time and place.
B) Two essences, not an essence and its attribute.
C) Anything that is numerically two.
D) The attribute and the essence.

Key Takeaway
True difference (taghāyur) applies only between two independent essences, not between an essence and its attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking of God's attributes—like His power, knowledge, or mercy—as separate 'things' that somehow add up to make Him? After this lesson, what must you change in how you conceive of His attributes?
?
When you reflect on your own character, do you treat your good qualities as distinct from who you are? How does the commentator's point about essence and attribute challenge you to see your own identity more honestly?
?
If difference (taghāyur) truly requires two independent essences, then nothing in creation is truly separate from Allah's will or existence. Are you living as if your actions and choices are independent of Him? What would it look like to repent of that independence today?

Critique of the Fifth Answer: Knowledge as Relation
The commentator critiques the fifth answer regarding knowledge as a relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the commentator finds Razi's analogy between existence and knowledge invalid.

Core Idea
All relations to the same object are identical, so the problem of multiple knowledges remains.

Razi attempted to answer the fifth objection by drawing an analogy between existence and knowledge. The commentator shows that this analogy fails because knowledge is a relation, and all relations to a single known object are similar. Unlike existence, which can be considered as existence itself, relations do not admit variety. This critique underscores the unique challenges of understanding divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the critique passage
Focus on the part starting with 'وما قال في الجواب عن الخامس' and understand the commentator's objection.
2
Understand knowledge as relation
The commentator assumes knowledge is a relation (nisba) or attachment (taʿalluq) to a known object. Recognize that all relations to the same object are similar (mutamāthila).
This is a key point: relations are determined by their relata.
3
See why the analogy fails
Razi likely compared knowledge to existence, but the commentator argues that existence is not a relation in the same way, so the analogy does not hold.
The text ends with a cryptic remark about existence, but the main point is clear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : قوله الجواب عن الأول قد تقدم يريد به تجويز كون الشىء فاعلا وقابلا وفي تفسير التغاير يجواز المفارقة في أحد الأمور الأربعة موضع نظر ، وذلك لأن كثيرا من العلل والمحاولات ممتنع المفارقة مع وجوب تغايرها ، والأولى أن يقال المتغايران هما ذاتان والذات لا تغاير صفتها لان صفتها لا تكون مغايرة بالذات لها أولا ذات لها ولمثل ذلك لا تتغاير الصفات . وما قال في الجواب عن الخامس فيه نظر لأن العلم على تقدير كونه نسبة أو تعلقا إلى معلوم فالنسب التي تكون إلى معلوم واحد تكون متماثلة ولا يندفع بقياسها على الوجود ولا في الوجود على وجوده وعلى وجوده.
I say: His statement 'the answer to the first has already preceded' intends by it the permissibility of a thing being both agent and recipient, and in the interpretation of difference (taghāyur) the permissibility of separation in one of the four things is subject to scrutiny, because many causes and effects [or attempts] cannot separate despite the necessity of their difference. It is better to say that two distinct things are two essences, and the essence does not differ from its attribute, because its attribute is not essentially distinct from it, nor does it have an essence of its own, and for such things attributes do not differ. And what he said in the answer to the fifth is subject to scrutiny, because knowledge, given that it is a relation or attachment to a known, the relations that are to one known are similar, and it is not refuted by analogy to existence, nor in existence regarding His existence and His existence.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage is the commentator's critique of Razi's answers. First, regarding the answer to the first objection, the commentator says that the concept of a thing being both agent and recipient, and the interpretation of difference as allowing separation in four aspects (time, place, existence, non-existence), is problematic because many causes and effects cannot separate even though they are distinct. He suggests a better definition: two distinct things are two essences, but an attribute is not an essence distinct from its subject, so it is not a 'different' thing. Second, regarding the answer to the fifth objection, the commentator argues that if knowledge is a relation to a known, then all relations to the same known are similar, so the analogy to existence is invalid. This challenges Razi's attempt to avoid the problem of similar knowledges.

Misconception: Some might think that knowledge can be multiple in the same way that existence is multiple.
Correction: The commentator shows that knowledge as a relation to a single object forces all such relations to be identical, so multiplicity is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the problem with the analogy between knowledge and existence?
A) Knowledge is a relation and all relations to one known are similar, unlike existence.
B) Existence is a relation and knowledge is not.
C) Knowledge and existence are both relations.
D) The analogy is correct.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge as a relation to a known object does not allow variation; all such relations are identical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge of every detail of your life is a single, unchanging relation to you, how does that confront your tendency to think He is 'closer' when you obey and 'farther' when you sin? Will you let this truth still your heart's anxiety about His awareness of your failures?
?
When you imagine God's knowledge of your future, do you secretly treat it as multiple pieces of information (each moment known differently)? This passage says all relations to the same object are identical. How does this reshape your trust in His single, all-encompassing knowledge—and will you today surrender the illusion that you can surprise Him or change His knowledge by your actions?
?
The commentator insists that an analogy between knowledge and existence fails because existence is not a relation. In what areas of your own faith do you rely on weak analogies to make God more 'manageable' or predictable? What would it cost you to accept that God's knowledge is simply not like anything else—and to live in that surrender?

The Imamate: Critique of Rational Proof and Sunni Alternative
Critique of rational proof for imamate and presentation of Sunni scriptural-consensus proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rational proof for the imamate is problematic within the Ash'ari framework and how the Sunni school grounds the imamate in scripture and communal consensus.

Core Idea
The imamate is established by divine command and community consensus, not rational necessity.

Imam al-Razi presented a proof for the necessity of the imamate that mixes rational and scriptural elements. However, as this passage shows, the rational part conflicts with Ash'ari theology. The commentator then points to a stronger, purely scriptural and historical argument preferred by the Ahl al-Sunna. This lesson unpacks both the critique and the alternative proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structure of al-Razi's proof
The proof has a rational minor premise (from good and evil) and an auditory major premise (consensus). Notice the tension.
The minor premise is problematic for Ash'aris who deny rational obligation.
2
Understand the critique
The minor premise is not from the author's own school. The major premise (consensus) is clearer, but the proof as a whole is flawed.
The critique shows the inconsistency of mixing rational and scriptural arguments.
3
Explore the Sunni proof
The passage recommends relying on scripture (Quran 4:59, hadith) and the historical consensus of the Companions. Trace how the first four caliphs were appointed.
The Sunni proof emphasizes designation (nass) or choice of the people of authority (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والدليل الذى جاء به المصنف على وجوب الإمامية سمعا فصغراء عقلى من باب الحسن والقبح وهو ليس من مذهبه ، وكبراءه التي أحالها إلى الإجماع أوضح عقلا من الصغرى، والأولى أن يعتمد على قوله تعالى : « أطيعوا الله وأطيعوا الرسول وأولى الأمر منكم ، وعلى قوله عليه الصلاة والسلام : من مات ولم يعرف إمام زمانه مات ميتة جاهلية ، وعلى أمثال ذلك ، ومن الظاهر أن أصحاب النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم وآله بعد وفاته أجمعوا على طاعة إمام بعده فذهب بعضهم إلى أنه نص صلى الله عليه وسلم على على كرم الله وجهه ، وبعضهم قالوا إنا ننصب إماما ونصبوا أبا بكر رضى الله عنه وبايعوه جميعا وبايعه على رضى الله عنه أيضاً ، ولو لم يكن نصب إمام واجبا لمخالفهم من الأمة أحد فى ذلك ، ثم اجتمعوا على عمر رضى الله عنه بنص أبي بكر رضى ثم على عثمان رضی الله عنه بسبب الشورى ، ثم على على رضى الله عنه لإجماع أكثر أهل الحل والعقد عليه ، وعرف من ذلك أن الإمام ينصب إما بنص من الذى قبله وإما باختيار أهل الحل والعقد إياه وهذا هو العمدة عند أهل السنة ، ولم يذكره المصنف رحمه الله تعالى في هذا الكتاب .
The proof that the author brought for the necessity of the imamate is auditory (sam'i), but the minor premise is rational (aqli) from the category of good and evil, and that is not from his school. Its major premise, which he referred to consensus, is clearer rationally than the minor. It is better to rely on His saying, the Most High: 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you,' and on his saying, peace be upon him: 'Whoever dies and does not know the imam of his time dies a death of ignorance,' and on similar examples. And it is apparent that the Companions of the Prophet, may God bless him and his family, after his death, agreed upon obeying an imam after him. So some went to the view that he, peace be upon him, designated 'Ali, may God honor his face. And some said: 'We appoint an imam,' and they appointed Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, and all gave him allegiance, and 'Ali, may God be pleased with him, also gave him allegiance. If the appointment of an imam were not obligatory, no one from the community would have opposed them in that. Then they agreed upon 'Umar, may God be pleased with him, by the designation of Abu Bakr, may God be pleased with him, then upon 'Uthman, may God be pleased with him, through consultation (shura), then upon 'Ali, may God be pleased with him, due to the consensus of most of the people of authority (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd) upon him. And it is known from this that the imam is appointed either by designation from the one before him or by the choice of the people of authority. This is the primary proof (al-'umda) for the Ahl al-Sunna, and the author, may God have mercy on him, did not mention it in this book.
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage criticizes the rational proof for the necessity of the imamate that the author (presumably al-Razi) presented. It states that the proof is auditory (sam'i) in its conclusion, but its minor premise is rational, based on the categories of good and evil, which contradicts the author's own school (Ash'ari) that does not affirm rational obligation. The major premise relies on consensus, which is rationally clearer than the minor premise. The passage then recommends a stronger proof based on scripture and historical practice: the Quranic verse 'Obey Allah and obey the Messenger and those in authority among you' (4:59), the hadith 'Whoever dies without knowing the imam of his time dies a death of ignorance,' and the actual consensus (ijma') of the Companions after the Prophet's death. The Companions agreed on the need for an imam, though they differed on who it should be. Some held that the Prophet explicitly designated 'Ali, while others appointed Abu Bakr. The fact that no one objected to the principle of appointing an imam shows it is obligatory. The passage then traces the successive appointments of the first four caliphs, concluding that an imam is appointed either by designation from the previous imam or by choice of the people of authority (ahl al-hall wa al-'aqd). This is the primary argument of the Ahl al-Sunna, but the author did not mention it in this book.

Key Terms
الدليل
السمع
العقل
الحسن والقبح
الإجماع
النص
أهل الحل والعقد
أهل السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imamate can be proven rationally through the category of good and evil.
Correction: The passage argues that such a rational proof is inconsistent with Ash'ari theology and that the correct proof is based on scripture and consensus.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary proof (al-'umda) for the Ahl al-Sunna regarding the appointment of an imam?
A) Rational proof based on good and evil
B) Designation by the previous imam or choice of the people of authority
C) The consensus of the entire Muslim community
D) The direct command of the Prophet

Key Takeaway
The imamate is not rationally necessary but is established by divine command and the historical consensus of the Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Companions prioritized the appointment of an imam immediately after the Prophet's death, showing its urgency. In your own life, do you prioritize establishing legitimate leadership in your family, workplace, or community? Or do you allow leadership vacuums, comfortable with 'dying without knowing the imam of your time'? What step will you take this week to ensure you are under proper authority or to support the appointment of a righteous leader?
?
The passage shows that the method of appointing imams varied—designation, consultation, consensus—yet all were accepted by the community. Are you willing to accept a leader even when the process does not match your personal preference? What pride or bias in your heart prevents you from submitting to the collective wisdom and historical practice of the Salaf?
?
If the imamate is not a rational necessity but a divine command, what does that reveal about your tendency to rely on your own intellect before submitting to revelation? In what area of your life are you currently resisting a clear command because your mind cannot fully grasp its wisdom? Will you repent and practice 'sam'an wa tā‘atan' (we hear and we obey)?
